<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. (a) Schematic diagram of a confocal multimode cavity QED apparatus like those in [21, 30, 37–41]. Multiple atomic spin clusters (orange) are located at distinct positions (ri) in the central plane of the confocal cavity, and are illuminated by the transverse pumping laser (red) causing light to be scattered into the cavity. The cavity field has both a local component (blue), and a non-local component (green) through which the interactions between the spin clusters are mediated. (b) Schematic diagram of intersecting single-mode cavities (cavity fields shown in light blue), with two atomic clusters (orange) in each cavity, arranged to realize the nearest-neighbor coupling geometry of...</figcaption>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Stable steady-state solutions shown through the total z-spin components scaled by the number of clusters plotted as a function of the coupling strength, g, for different values of nc. As the number of clusters in the system is increased, the number of stable steady-state solutions in the superradiant phase increases, as given by Eq. (14). The legends label the pairs of solutions based on the sign of the x-spin component of each cluster. Note that some values of Ztot correspond to multiple distinct solution pairs due to reflection symmetry about the central cluster. In addition, some of the solutions pairs that are not linked via symmetries, but have the same amount of groups of...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13072_figures/2606.13072_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Example mean-field evolution of a four cluster system towards an oscillatory steady-state solution in the superradiant phase. (a) The cavity field occupations converge to fixed values, with finite occupation in the cavity modes that connect clusters within superradiant pairs (modes 1 and 3), and with zero occupation in the cavity mode connecting two superradiant pairs (mode 2). (b) The paths traced out by the spin of each cluster on its own generalized Bloch sphere (blue) converge to closed phase space orbits (green), leading to oscillations in the spin components. The orbits for the first two clusters are identical and the orbits for the last two clusters are identical, and the two...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13072_figures/2606.13072_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. Example mean-field evolution of a three cluster sys- tem towards an oscillatory steady-state solution in the normal phase. (a) The cavity field occupations for both modes con- verge to zero as expected for the normal phase. (b) The paths traced out by the spin of each cluster on its own generalized Bloch sphere (blue) converge to closed phase space orbits (green), leading to oscillations in the spin components. The orbits for all three clusters trace out the same circle, but the orbit of the middle cluster is out of phase with that of the two edge clusters so that xi = −xi+1 and yi = −yi+1. The initial conditions used to generate these plots are (xi, 0, − p</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13072_figures/2606.13072_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 5. Comparison of the mean-field solutions (dashed black curve) with results obtained using TWA for finite-size clusters with varying numbers of emitters per cluster. The comparison is made for the expectation value of the x-spin component of the first cluster scaled by the cluster size N. (a) Non-oscillatory superradiant steady-state solution, ± ± ± ∓∓, for five clusters. For large clusters the deviations from the mean-field solution are small indicating stability of the non-oscillatory solution even for finite-size clusters. (b) Superradiant phase oscillations for two clusters. (c) Normal phase oscillations for two clusters. Oscillations are observed in both phases even for...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper analyzes the multimode Dicke model using an ordered nearest-neighbor coupling geometry realized with ultracold atoms in optical cavities. It demonstrates that the system exhibits Fibonacci scaling in the number of stable steady states within the superradiant phase, and these states can host persistent quantum oscillations. The findings are robustly confirmed by comparing mean-field results against treatments incorporating quantum fluctuations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides deep insights into how geometric constraints (nearest-neighbor coupling) dictate complex collective behavior, leading to mathematically rich structures like Fibonacci sequences in the number of stable solutions. The combination of mean-field theory with rigorous fluctuation treatments makes it highly relevant for modern quantum optics research.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the stable steady-states and persistent oscillations within the multimode Dicke model when subjected to an ordered nearest-neighbor coupling geometry.

**Main result.** The number of mean-field stable steady-states in the superradiant phase exhibits Fibonacci scaling with the number of atomic clusters, and a subset of these states show persistent oscillations.

**Method.** Analysis employs mean-field theory, supplemented by advanced techniques like the Truncated Wigner Approximation (TWA) to account for quantum fluctuations beyond the classical limit.

**Model / system.** The system is an ordered multimode Dicke model realized using ultracold atoms in optical cavities with nearest-neighbor coupling. Dynamics are governed by a Lindblad master equation, which is simplified into an atom-only description via cavity mode elimination.

**Key observables.** Stable steady-state solutions (characterized by spin components $x_i, y_i, z_i$), and the presence of persistent oscillations observed as closed orbits in phase space.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The system is analyzed across a phase transition from Normal Phase to Superradiant Phase, controlled by coupling strengths ($g$) relative to critical values ($g_c$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Initial analyses rely on the mean-field limit or the TWA; stability proofs are contingent on small parameters ($\epsilon \ll 1$); the nearest-neighbor geometry is a key focus.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate schematics of cavity setups, show multiple stable pairs of solutions exhibiting Fibonacci scaling in the superradiant phase, and depict closed orbits corresponding to persistent oscillations in both normal and superradiant regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the physical model (Dicke model with ordered coupling) to deriving mean-field equations. It then analyzes stability using perturbative methods, confirming Fibonacci scaling for steady states, before extending the analysis to include quantum fluctuations via TWA to confirm persistence in finite systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Ultracold atoms in multimode optical cavities provide a rich testbed for many-body phenomena enabled by light-mediated interactions. Recent experiments include realizations of spin glasses and associative memories, as described by multimode Dicke models with disordered couplings. However, the properties of multimode Dicke models with ordered coupling geometries remain largely unexplored. In this work, we investigate the stable steady-states of the multimode Dicke model with an ordered nearest-neighbor coupling geometry, where $n_c$ atomic clusters are coupled via $n_c-1$ cavity modes. We show that the number of mean-field stable steady-states in the superradiant phase exhibits Fibonacci scaling with the number of atomic clusters, and that a subset of these steady-states exhibit persistent oscillations. Using both the truncated Wigner approximation and the numerically-exact hierarchy of pure states, we further demonstrate that these features of the stable steady-state solutions persist for finite cluster sizes. Ordered multimode Dicke models, such as the nearest-neighbor coupling geometry considered here, are accessible with current experimental technologies and point toward a broader class of strongly interacting dissipative systems with similarly rich behavior.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12525"></a>
### ⭐ [Composite Quantum Geometry and Semiclassical Dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12525v1)

**Highlighted author(s):** Johannes Knolle  
**Authors:** Henry Davenport, Yoonseok Hwang, Johannes Knolle, Frank Schindler  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12525v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12525_figures/2606.12525_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Trions in MATBG at filling ν = 0, in the chiral limit w0/w1 = 0 with gate distance ξ = 20 nm [18]. (a) Trion dispersion with gapped trion away from the Γ point. (b) QGD [De1,h(p)] for the lowest energy bound trion. (c) Dynamics of trion starting at k = (0, 0.75) with applied field in ˆx direction.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper develops a theoretical framework to describe the semiclassical dynamics of composite bound states, such as trions, by generalizing topological concepts like Berry curvature. It introduces the Quantum Geometric Dipole (QGD) term into the equations of motion, showing that this geometric contribution dictates unique physical behaviors, including transverse drifts, which are absent in single-particle descriptions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful generalization of Berry curvature physics to multi-particle excitations (composites), which is crucial for understanding transport phenomena in strongly correlated or excitonic systems where simple single-band models fail.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study derives semiclassical equations of motion for general composite bound states (like excitons and trions) in insulators, extending topological concepts beyond single-electron dynamics.

**Main result.** The equations of motion are modified by a Quantum Geometric Dipole (QGD) term, which is necessary to describe the physics of neutral composites when an external electric field is applied. This QGD can induce unique dynamics, such as transverse drift in trions.

**Method.** The authors use adiabatic approximations and gauge transformations on the Hamiltonian to derive semiclassical equations for position expectation values, generalizing concepts like Berry curvature to composite systems.

**Model / system.** The model concerns composite bound states (e.g., excitons, trions) in semiconductors/insulators, analyzed using Bloch Hamiltonians and projected position operators. The analysis is extended to specific materials like magic-angle twisted bilayer graphene (MATBG).

**Key observables.** Berry connection ($A_\alpha(p)$), Quantum Geometric Dipole ($D_{\alpha,eta}(p)$), and the expectation values of composite position operators $\langle \hat{R}_\alpha angle$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** External electric field ($\mathbf{E}$), momentum ($\mathbf{p}$), and the nature of the composite (neutral vs. charged).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies on the adiabatic approximation, assuming the bound state follows instantaneous eigenstates, and involves careful handling of gauge invariance.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate Berry curvatures ($\Omega$) and QGDs ($D$) for trions in MATBG, showing their spatial dependence across the Brillouin zone.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically derives generalized semiclassical equations of motion by first defining species-specific position operators and calculating gauge-invariant quantities like the QGD. It then applies these to analyze the dynamics of composite particles under an electric field, culminating in specific examples like trions in MATBG.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We derive semiclassical equations of motion for general composite bound states in insulators and semiconductors, covering excitations such as excitons and trions. For neutral composites we find that a uniform external electric field does not couple to a Berry curvature term, contrary to the naive expectation from single-electron dynamics. Instead, a distinct quantum geometric quantity appears generically in the equations of motion. This quantity is the difference between inequivalent Berry connections that can be defined for the composite, generalising the concept of the quantum geometric dipole previously studied for excitons. In the case of charged composites such as trions, we find an additional Berry curvature contribution to the equations of motion. As we demonstrate, however, there is an infinite family of inequivalent composite Berry curvatures, and so care must be taken to make the correct choice that describes the physical dynamics. We explain how this choice should be made dependent on the definition of a spatial centre for the composite. We end by discussing composite dynamics that have no single-electron counterpart. We find that trions in magic-angle twisted bilayer graphene undergo a transverse drift under an applied electric field and that this is driven not only by the Berry curvature contribution but also by the quantum geometric dipole. The interplay of these two geometric contributions further imprints itself on the trion's internal dynamics, causing its dipole moment to oscillate in time.

</details>

### [Driven-dissipative entanglement of distant giant atoms](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13375v1)

**Summary.** The research demonstrates the generation and preservation of remote entanglement between two distant superconducting giant atoms using driven-dissipative protocols. By continuously driving the coupled system, they exploit correlated dissipation to create an entangled state, which is then protected by tuning the qubit frequencies away from the high-dissipation regime. This proves that engineered dissipation is a robust pathway for quantum network distribution.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides an experimental realization of reservoir engineering principles—using engineered dissipation to drive a quantum system into a desired entangled steady state—which is highly relevant for building scalable quantum networks.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is demonstrating a viable method for distributing entanglement across quantum networks using driven-dissipative protocols, contrasting with traditional coherent methods.

**Main result.** The authors successfully generated remote entanglement between two distant giant atoms, achieving a Bell-state fidelity of $F = 0.89 \pm 0.02$.

**Method.** Entanglement is generated by continuously driving the superconducting system through the waveguide, exploiting correlated dissipation, followed by in situ frequency tuning to preserve the entangled state.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is a superconducting circuit containing two giant artificial atoms coupled sequentially to a waveguide (wQED setup). The dynamics are modeled using master equations incorporating tunable individual and correlated dissipation via interference effects (superradiance/subradiance).

**Key observables.** Bell-state fidelity ($F$), Concurrence ($C$), transmission spectrum $|S_{21}|$, and qubit decay rates ($\Gamma$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Optimal drive amplitude ($\Omega/2\pi = 45 	ext{ MHz}$), stabilization time $	au$, and the ratio of coupling strengths to dissipation rates.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that suppressing individual dissipation post-generation (by tuning to subradiant states) is sufficient to preserve entanglement, despite the initial dissipative generation mechanism.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate various entanglement schemes (coherent vs. driven-dissipative), show spectroscopy measurements of single and two atoms, and plot fidelity dependence on drive amplitude ($\Omega$) and time ($	au$).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the need for dissipative protocols to detailing the physical system (giant atoms in a waveguide), demonstrating entanglement generation via driving near superradiance, and finally showing state preservation by tuning into protected subradiant states.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum interconnects distribute entanglement via controlled light-matter interactions for quantum computing and sensing applications. Many entanglement generation schemes use coherent, reversible interactions that require precisely calibrated pulses to execute. In contrast, driven-dissipative protocols use a continuous-wave drive in the presence of correlated dissipation to stabilize entanglement in protected (dark) states. However, the same dissipation that generates the entanglement also limits its utility once the stabilization protocol ends. Here, we engineer a superconducting system of two giant artificial atoms coupled sequentially to a waveguide, with tunable individual and correlated dissipation enabled by interference between coupling points. Continuously driving the atoms through the waveguide exploits correlated dissipation to generate remote entanglement. We then tune the qubit frequencies in situ to suppress individual dissipation and thereby preserve the entanglement, achieving a Bell-state fidelity F = 0.89 +/- 0.02. This demonstration indicates that the driven dissipation of giant atoms is a viable approach for distributing entanglement across quantum networks.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper investigates how interference effects associated with zero modes manifest during rapid changes (quenches) in a lattice model. By analyzing the zero-mode transfer probability and edge defects, the authors show that these observables act as sensitive probes of critical dynamics. The results quantitatively link observed interference patterns, such as period doubling, to the accumulated phase acquired by the system's Hamiltonian along the quench path.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed theoretical framework connecting non-equilibrium dynamics (quenches) with topological properties (zero modes). The ability to predict specific oscillatory behaviors like period doubling based on path topology is highly relevant for designing quantum simulation protocols.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the interference of critical dynamics associated with zero modes (ICDZM) during nonadiabatic quench processes across multiple critical points.

**Main result.** The ICDZM pattern exhibits rich behavior, including period doubling when linking topologically nontrivial phases, and this phenomenon is successfully mapped onto the accumulated interference phase calculated via WKB analysis.

**Method.** The authors employ time-dependent quantum mechanics, utilizing quench protocols through parameter space paths. Key calculations involve WKB approximation to determine the accumulated interference phase ($\Delta\varphi$) governing the zero-mode transfer probability ($p_Z$).

**Model / system.** The system is modeled using generalized Creutz ladders described by an OBC Hamiltonian involving hopping amplitudes and phases. The dynamics are controlled by varying parameters like $	heta$ or $\mu$, leading to topological phase transitions.

**Key observables.** Zero-mode transfer probability ($p_Z$), edge defect ($	ext{d}_{	ext{left}}$), bulk excitation density ($p_B$), and the interference period ($T$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Quench path parameters ($	heta, \mu$), quench time scale ($	au_Q$), and system coupling constants ($K, J_X, J_Y$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis heavily relies on WKB approximations for calculating the interference phase in both bulk and edge observables. The Hamiltonian is assumed to be quadratic in single-particle operators.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare $p_Z$ and $	ext{d}_{	ext{left}}$ across different quench protocols, showing oscillatory patterns, period doubling ($T_Z \simeq 2T_B$), and power-law decay for one-way quenches. Other figures illustrate the energy gap structure ($\Delta_Z$ vs $\Delta_B$) and phase accumulation integrals.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes three quench protocols (varying $	heta$ or $\mu$). It first establishes the connection between $p_Z$, edge defects, and ICDZM. Subsequently, it uses WKB analysis to derive quantitative relationships for the interference phase ($\Delta\varphi$) and the resulting periods ($T$), confirming the dependence on topological winding numbers.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the interference of critical dynamics associated with zero modes (ICDZM) in the generalized Creutz ladders using closed quench paths that pass through two critical points successively. By reading out the final zero-mode transfer probability, we find rich ICDZM interference patterns dependent on the quench path. In particular, when the closed path links two topologically nontrivial phases, the ICDZM pattern may either vanish or exhibit period doubling. Within the framework of WKB analysis, this phenomenon is well clarified by the interference phase accumulated in the quench procedure. We also demonstrate that the zero-mode transfer probability can be detected by the deviation of the boundary particle number from its initial fractional value, which arises from the blending of bulk modes in the critical dynamics. As an edge defect, the zero-mode transfer probability captures both the ICDZM oscillation and the known anomalous defect production in a non-closed quench path. These results identify ICDZM and the corresponding edge defect as probes for critical dynamics associated with topological zero modes.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.13005"></a>
### [Experiment-compatible measurement--feedback quantum state preparation with reinforcement learning](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13005v1)

**Authors:** Xiaotian Nie, Tao Zhang, Linghui Chen  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13005v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **5/5** · 🔥 `analog quantum simulation` **4/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13005_figures/2606.13005_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Schematic of the measurement–feedback reinforcement-learning framework. (a) Closed-loop control: at each time step the system is weakly measured, a recurrent policy selects the next measurement and feedback action from the measurement record, and a feedback unitary is applied. (b) Experiment-compatible reinforcement: a terminal reward is constructed from one-shot measurements of randomly sampled Hamiltonian terms, providing an unbiased energy estimator for PPO training. (c) Applications: Bose–Hubbard ground-state preparation and GHZ-state generation.</figcaption>

Energy evolution during measurement–feedback ground-state preparation of the four-site Bose–Hubbard model at unit filling, in the (a) non-interacting (U/J = 0), (b) strong-interaction (U/J = 5), and (c) near-critical (U/J = 3) regimes, with measurement strength γ/J = 0.3. Thin col- ored lines show 100 sampled trajectories, the bold line is the ensemble mean, and the shaded region indicates ±1 standard deviation over 1000 episodes. The dashed line marks the ex- act ground-state energy Egs.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13005_figures/2606.13005_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">

The same plotting conventions as Fig. 2 are used. In both cases, the adaptive policy using only single-qubit Z-type mea- surements and Y -type feedback drives the energy close to Egs = −4, preparing the target entangled states with high fidelity.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper presents a novel, hardware-compatible method for preparing complex quantum states, such as ground states of the Bose-Hubbard model, using reinforcement learning applied to measurement--feedback control. By utilizing only the history of measurable outcomes, the protocol bypasses the need for full state knowledge, making it highly relevant for current experimental quantum simulation platforms. The results demonstrate robust and efficient preparation across various physical regimes.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the practical gap between theoretical quantum control schemes and experimental feasibility in cold atom/quantum gas platforms by embedding the control loop within an RL framework compatible with limited measurement data.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing practical, scalable protocols for preparing target ground states in many-body systems using measurement--feedback control.

**Main result.** The developed RL framework successfully drives the system to low-energy states (e.g., BHM ground state) and generates entangled states (GHZ states), outperforming fixed or handcrafted baselines.

**Method.** An adaptive measurement--feedback protocol is implemented using Reinforcement Learning (RL) under partial observability, allowing real-time control based only on accessible measurement outcomes.

**Model / system.** The method is demonstrated on the Bose–Hubbard model (BHM) and for generating GHZ states. The control loop involves weak measurements followed by feedback unitaries applied over discrete time steps.

**Key observables.** Ground state energy ($E_{gs}$), measurement outcomes, and fidelity of generated entangled states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Measurement strength ($\gamma$), interaction ratio ($U/J$), and the training duration ($\gamma T$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The protocol is designed to operate using only experimentally accessible measurements, avoiding the need for full quantum-state reconstruction or tomography.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically illustrate the closed-loop control process at each time step, the experiment-compatible RL training setup using one-shot measurements, and applications to BHM ground-state preparation and GHZ state generation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the limitations of existing quantum state preparation methods, proposes the RL-based measurement--feedback framework (POMDP formulation), details the hardware-compatible reward design, and validates the method by applying it to both the Bose-Hubbard model and GHZ state generation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Ground-state preparation is a critical task in quantum simulation and quantum computing, as it enables the study of correlated phases and the generation of entangled resource states. While measurement--feedback control has emerged as a promising route to state preparation, existing schemes either rely on handcrafted, task-specific policies or are designed using full quantum-state information that is unavailable in real experiments and becomes impractical for large many-body systems. Here we develop an adaptive measurement--feedback protocol based on reinforcement learning under partial observability. The controller uses only the history of experimentally accessible measurement outcomes to choose both the measurement operator and the feedback action in real time. To make training compatible with experiments, we introduce a stochastic terminal reward built from one-shot measurements of randomly sampled Hamiltonian components, avoiding unphysical full-state reconstruction while remaining an unbiased estimator of the target energy. We demonstrate the method by preparing ground states of the Bose--Hubbard model and by generating GHZ states, establishing a scalable and hardware-compatible route to quantum state preparation.

</details>

### [Quantized time in quantum walks under weak rank-K measurements](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13552v1)

**Summary.** This paper theoretically examines the quantization of time statistics when monitoring a quantum walk using multiple channels of weak or strong measurements. The authors demonstrate that this multi-channel monitoring preserves the universal quantization property, showing that the mean return time is proportional to the winding number divided by the coupling strength parameter. This extends fundamental results on recurrence times to higher-dimensional monitored systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work connects fundamental concepts from quantum measurement theory (weak measurements, projection) with quantum transport phenomena (quantum walks), providing a theoretical framework for observing quantized time statistics in open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether monitoring a quantum walk evolution using multi-channel strong or indirect measurements leads to a universally quantized time statistic for the mean return time.

**Main result.** It is shown that under such multi-channel monitoring, the mean return time of the quantum walk in the projected subspace remains quantized, suggesting universal time quantization even for higher dimensional dynamics.

**Method.** The analysis involves calculating transition amplitudes using matrix elements derived from the unitary evolution operator and projecting onto a $K$-dimensional subspace defined by projectors. The key mathematical tool is relating the mean return time to the winding number of these amplitudes in the complex plane.

**Model / system.** The model is a quantum walk whose evolution is monitored by repeated measurements, which can be modeled as direct projection or indirectly via an ancilla coupling. The system's dynamics are analyzed within a projected $K$-dimensional subspace.

**Key observables.** Mean return time ($ar{t}$), mean number of measurements ($ar{n}$), and the winding number ($w$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling strength parameter $\eta$ (or related variable $x$), and the dimension of the projected subspace, $K$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes orthogonal states $|\psi_langle$ and relies on specific algebraic manipulations involving matrix inverses.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds upon known results for mean return time quantization in 1D walks ($K=1$) and extends this framework to the general case of multi-channel monitoring ($K>1$), deriving relationships between $ar{n}$, $w$, and $\eta$.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Measurements can be used to monitor the evolution of quantum systems and may lead to a universally quantized time statistics. It is known that the mean return time is quantized for strong and indirect monitoring through the winding number of the return amplitude in a one-dimensional space. Here we discuss that under multi-channel strong or indirect monitoring, where the latter is achieved through ancilla coupling, the mean return time of a quantum walk in the projected subspace is also quantized. This reflects a universal time quantization for a higher dimensional evolution.

</details>

### [Supersymmetry of dissipative Bose-Fermi systems with application to Jaynes-Cummings and Dicke models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12682v1)

**Authors:** Colin V. Coane, Francesco Iachello  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12682v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **4/5** · 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **4/5** · `Dicke superradiance` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `superradiant laser` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops advanced theoretical techniques using supersymmetries to analytically determine the eigenvalues for both the Hamiltonian and the Liouvillian superoperator governing coupled Bose-Fermi systems like the JC and Dicke models. By exploiting underlying algebraic symmetries, the authors reduce large matrix eigenvalue problems into diagonalizing small blocks, providing exact solutions for open quantum dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides powerful mathematical tools—leveraging supersymmetry—to solve complex open quantum system dynamics (Liouvillians) in systems central to cavity QED and quantum optics. The systematic reduction of dimensionality is highly valuable for theoretical modeling.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to find explicit analytic solutions for the eigenvalues of both the Hamiltonian and the Liouvillian superoperator for various coupled Bose-Fermi systems.

**Main result.** Supersymmetry methods allow the reduction of large eigenvalue problems (for both $H$ and $\mathcal{L}$) into diagonalizing small, manageable block matrices. This yields explicit analytic formulas for the eigenvalues in terms of system parameters.

**Method.** The authors employ techniques from superalgebra theory ($u(n|m)$) to exploit symmetries that commute with conserved quantities (Casimir invariants). The Liouvillian is analyzed by constructing a basis and showing it takes a block-triangular form, allowing diagonalization of smaller blocks.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on coupled Bose-Fermi systems, specifically applying the framework to the Jaynes-Cummings (JC) model and the Dicke model. The dynamics are described by Hamiltonians and governed by Lindblad master equations ($\partial_tho = \mathcal{L}ho$).

**Key observables.** Eigenvalues of the Hamiltonian ($H$) and the Liouvillian superoperator ($\mathcal{L}$); steady-state properties.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling strengths ($g$), cavity frequency ($\omega_c$), qubit/fermion frequency ($\omega_q$), and dissipation rates ($\gamma, \gamma'$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis heavily relies on the existence of symmetries (supersymmetries) that commute with the Hamiltonian or Casimir operators, leading to block-diagonalization of the Liouvillian matrix.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate spectral properties for JC and Dicke models, showing energy spectra in different parameter regimes and basis representations.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops methods by first solving the Hamiltonian spectrum using superalgebra symmetries. It then extends this methodology to the Liouvillian superoperator, deriving explicit formulas for eigenvalues by analyzing block-diagonal structures arising from bosonic and fermionic dissipation channels.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We demonstrate how supersymmetries of Hamiltonians for coupled Bose-Fermi systems can be used to place the Hamiltonians of the Jaynes-Cummings model and Dicke model under the rotating wave approximation in matrix form and provide explicit analytic solutions for their eigenvalues. We then use this supersymmetry to place the Liouvillians of the associated Markovian open systems in matrix form and provide explicit solutions for their eigenvalues. These results are a consequence of the fact that the Hamiltonian of the Jaynes-Cummings model commutes with the linear Casimir invariant of the superalgebra $u(1|1)$ and that the Hamiltonian of the Dicke model commutes both with the linear invariant of $\sum_{i} u_{i}(1|1)$ and with the invariant of an additional $su(2)$ algebra. Our methods apply to various coupled Bose-Fermi systems with $u(1|1)$ and more generally with $u(n|m)$ dynamical superalgebras, and may provide efficient tools for studying more complicated examples.

</details>

### [Dynamical Control of Superconductivity in Superconductor-Ferromagnet Bilayers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12544v1)

**Summary.** The paper theoretically explores controlling superconductivity in superconductor-ferromagnet bilayers by coupling them to an external microwave cavity. By applying a resonant drive, the system undergoes a non-equilibrium phase transition from a nodal p-wave state to a fully gapped superconducting state. This work demonstrates how coherent electromagnetic driving can be used as a tool to engineer exotic quantum phases of matter.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work sits at the intersection of circuit QED and condensed matter theory, providing a framework to engineer novel quantum states (like changing gap symmetry) using coherent electromagnetic driving fields.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how coupling superconducting materials (SC) with ferromagnets (FM) to a microwave resonator can dynamically control the superconducting pairing symmetry.

**Main result.** Driven magnon-polaritons induce a non-equilibrium crossover from an expected nodal p-wave superconductor state to a fully gapped $(p_x+ip_y)$-superconductor.

**Method.** The analysis uses theoretical modeling involving coupled Bogoliubov excitations, ferromagnetic magnons, and cavity photons, often employing perturbation theory and time-averaging techniques.

**Model / system.** The system is an SC-FM bilayer integrated with a microwave resonator, modeled by coupling the superconducting gap dynamics to the magnon-photon polariton modes. The low-energy excitations include magnons ($\omega_m$), Bogoliubov quasiparticles, and cavity photons ($\omega_r$).

**Key observables.** The temperature-dependent shift of the cavity photon frequency, $\delta\omega_r(T)$, is used to diagnose the superconducting gap symmetry (nodal vs. nodeless).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnon-photon detuning ($\Delta$), external drive strength ($V_d$), and temperature ($T$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes weak, slow driving fields and utilizes approximations like integrating out certain degrees of freedom or using the adiabatic limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the coupled system setup (SC-FM/resonator), magnon-polariton dispersion, and theoretical predictions for $\delta\omega_r(T)$ under both equilibrium and driven conditions, highlighting characteristic power laws.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the coupled Hamiltonian, then analyzing the dynamics of the polaritons under external driving, which in turn dictates the non-equilibrium evolution of the superconducting pairing state ($\Delta_k$), culminating in predictions for measurable spectroscopic signatures like $\delta\omega_r(T)$.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study a simplified model of a ferromagnetic metal proximitized by a fully-gapped $s-$wave superconductor and integrated with a microwave resonator. The low-energy excitations in the combined system consist of ferromagnetic magnons, Bogoliubov excitations of the superconductor, and cavity photons. We show here that when the magnons and photons have comparable frequencies and are subject to an external drive, the hybridized driven magnon-polaritons induce a non-equilibrium crossover from the expected proximitized nodal $p-$wave superconductor to a fully gapped $(p_x+ip_y)-$superconductor. Moreover, the characteristic crossover temperature is inversely related to the magnon-photon detuning. We compute the temperature-dependent renormalization of the cavity photon frequencies across this nodal to nodeless evolution, which modifies the kinetic inductance of the resonator, and find a number of non-trivial features tied to the non-equilibrium (i.e., driven) nature of the problem. We compare and contrast these results with a recent circuit quantum electrodynamics (cQED) based experiment studying a permalloy-niobium bilayer, where a non-trivial dependence of the low-temperature cavity response on the magnon-photon detuning was observed. Our results pave the way for a principled exploration of engineering novel states of matter by coupling cavity photons to electronic collective modes in correlated two-dimensional materials and interfaces.

</details>

### [Thermoelectric information engine driven by an autonomous Maxwell demon across quantum-to-classical transitions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13500v1)

**Authors:** Maximiliano Bernal Santibañez, Felipe Barra, Jose Mondaca  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13500v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **2/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper models a three-dot thermoelectric engine acting as an information processor driven by a Maxwell demon mechanism. It rigorously analyzes the system dynamics using multiple master equation approximations to map out different quantum-to-classical transition regimes. The findings clarify the competition between coherent tunneling, decoherence from phonons, and incoherent transport in enabling energy conversion via information flow.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it merges concepts from open quantum systems, non-equilibrium thermodynamics (Maxwell's demon), and quantum transport, providing a rigorous framework for understanding how coherence affects energy/information conversion in nanoscale devices.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates a thermoelectric information engine driven by an autonomous Maxwell demon across different quantum-to-classical transition regimes.

**Main result.** The model demonstrates that the system's operation as an information engine is maintained across varying coupling strengths, with coherence and decoherence playing competing roles in determining transport mechanisms.

**Method.** The dynamics are analyzed using open quantum system formalisms, comparing results from Redfield master equations against two distinct Lindblad approximations (global vs. semilocal) to determine the appropriate theoretical description for different parameter regimes.

**Model / system.** The physical model is a three-terminal thermoelectric engine comprising two working dots (L and R) coupled to a monitoring 'Maxwell demon' dot (D). The system interacts with fermionic reservoirs and an explicit phonon bath, leading to complex non-equilibrium dynamics.

**Key observables.** Information flow ($\dot{I}$), energy/heat/work flows ($\dot{E}, \dot{Q}, \dot{W}$), coherence measures ($C_{l1}$), and the rate of entropy production ($\dot{\sigma}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The ratio of interdot tunneling strength to bath coupling ($g/\kappa_L$) controls the crossover between dynamical regimes, while phonon coupling strength ($J_0$) drives decoherence.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Key approximations include the Born-Markov limit and the use of secular/partial-secular expansions for the master equation; spinless electrons are assumed.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare coherence and particle currents across parameter variations ($g/\kappa_L$ or $J_0$), illustrating how different theoretical approaches (Redfield, global, semilocal) predict transport properties in various regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the system dynamics by deriving and comparing multiple master equation formalisms based on approximations related to frequency degeneracies. It then applies these tools to calculate thermodynamic quantities like work extraction and information flow across parameter sweeps that induce quantum-to-classical crossovers.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study a three-terminal thermoelectric engine, focusing on the role of quantum coherence and information flow. A double-dot connects two reservoirs at different chemical potentials, while a third dot monitors their occupation via Coulomb interaction and can be interpreted as an autonomous Maxwell demon. Within the parameter range where the device operates as an engine, we identify conditions under which this interpretation holds. The system dynamics is described within a Redfield master equation that allows us to identify two distinct dynamical regimes with steady states well captured by suitable Lindblad approximations. These two regimes define a first quantum-to-classical transition controlled by the interdot tunneling strength. We further consider the effect of a phonon bath coupled to the double-dot, which induces a second quantum-to-classical transition by generating incoherent transport and decoherence in the small interdot tunneling regime. We identify a parameter region where phonon-induced decoherence suppresses both the coherent transport contribution and the information flow toward the monitoring dot, suggesting that coherence can enhance the demon mechanism in this regime. By tracking information and transport properties across these crossovers, our model shows how coherent tunneling, decoherence, and incoherent phonon-assisted transport compete in an autonomous information engine, while clarifying which thermodynamic Lindblad description is appropriate in each regime.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Invariant Measures and Weak-Magic-Injection Asymptotics in Random Monitored Quantum Circuits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13470v1)

**Authors:** Guocheng Zhen, Xuanrong Yang, Chengkai Zhu, Ranyiliu Chen, Xin Wang

For each displayed pair (d, N) ∈{(3, 2), (3, 3), (3, 4), (5, 2)} and each value of θM, the trajectories are initialized at |0⟩⊗N, evolved to the stationary regime, and averaged over 30 or 50 independent replicas, depending on the displayed parameter set, to estimate the stationary mana M(θM). For each displayed system size N ∈{6, 8, 10, 12} and each value of θM, the trajectories are initialized at |0⟩⊗N, evolved to the stationary regime, and averaged over 20–50 independent replicas, depending on the displayed parameter set, to estimate the stationary 2-SRE S2(θM). On a log–log scale, the resulting values follow the dashed slope-two reference in the weak-magic-injection regime, supporting the quadratic law in Theorem D.53.</figcaption>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5: Schematic road map of the proof. The preliminary conventions feed into the Markov-kernel construction and the Clifford-twirl verification. The common first-order blow-up package is shared by the odd-prime mana and qubit 2-SRE response arguments, while the qubit response also requires a separate second-order module.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work establishes a rigorous analytical theory for random monitored quantum circuits by proving the existence of unique stationary ergodic laws. Crucially, it determines that the steady buildup of non-stabilizer 'magic' resource scales differently—linearly versus quadratically—depending on whether the underlying qudit dimension is an odd prime or exactly two (qubits).

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides deep mathematical tools—like tangent space analysis and spectral gap estimates—to characterize resource accumulation in open quantum systems, which is crucial for understanding decoherence and measurement back-action in complex quantum devices.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a rigorous analytical framework to study the stationary ergodic dynamics of random monitored quantum circuits, particularly determining the leading asymptotic behavior of injected 'magic' resource.

**Main result.** The steady-state magic response scales linearly with the injection strength $\theta_M$ for odd prime local dimensions ($d$), but quadratically ($\propto \theta_M^2$) for qubit systems ($d=2$).

**Method.** The analysis uses quantum-trajectory ergodic theory, blow-up analysis near the stabilizer layer, and tangent dynamics expansions to resolve the weak-magic-injection limit.

**Model / system.** Random Monitored Quantum Circuits acting on $N$ qudits of prime local dimension $d$. The process involves Clifford scrambling, a local non-Clifford rotation (injecting magic), measurement, and an inverse Clifford return.

**Key observables.** Stationary Gross–Wigner mana (odd primes) and 2-stabilizer Rényi entropy ($S_2$) for qubits; the resource measure 'magic'.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The weak-magic-injection strength $\theta_M \to 0$; the local dimension $d$ (prime); the number of qudits $N$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on identifying effective tangent dynamics near the stabilizer layer and assuming sufficient regularity for blow-up maps.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate schematic circuit steps, numerical convergence to stationary states independent of initial conditions, and log-log plots confirming linear (odd $d$) or quadratic ($d=2$) scaling laws in the weak-$\theta_M$ limit.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing general ergodicity theorems for the Markov process on pure states to analyzing the asymptotic behavior by performing blow-up analysis at $\theta_M=0$, separating linear (odd $d$) and quadratic ($d=2$) scaling regimes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Monitored quantum circuits provide a natural setting in which scrambling, measurements, and measurement-conditioned updates compete within a stochastic many-body dynamics. From the viewpoint of nonstabilizer resource theory, this competition is especially relevant because Clifford-compatible operations preserve the stabilizer structure, while weak non-Clifford perturbations inject magic resource. Most of the existing understanding of monitored quantum circuits has been shaped by numerical simulations and phenomenological descriptions, while a rigorous dynamics theory remains less developed. In this paper, we address this gap by developing an analytical framework which lays a rigorous mathematical foundation for the study of random monitored quantum dynamics. Specifically, we study a class of monitored quantum circuits driven by random Clifford. We prove the existence and uniqueness of the stationary law, which gives an ergodic description of the long-time dynamics. We then resolve the leading asymptotics of steady magic in the weak-magic-injection limit. This tangent description makes the contrast between resource measures transparent: in odd-prime local dimension, the steady Gross--Wigner mana has a linear leading asymptotic, whereas in qubit systems the steady 2-stabilizer Rényi entropy has a quadratic leading asymptotic. These different powers reflect the distinct local geometries of the two resource measures near the stabilizer layer. In this way, this work develops an analytical framework that first establishes the stationary ergodic dynamics of random monitored quantum circuits.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper reports on observing non-Gaussian dynamics in a 2D XY spin model simulated with trapped ions. By measuring high-order spin correlations and comparing them against Gaussian approximations, the authors confirm deviations that signal genuinely quantum many-body physics. This work establishes a verifiable pathway for exploring complex quantum regimes using state-of-the-art ion trap technology.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a crucial experimental benchmark for quantum simulation, demonstrating how to verify physics beyond the limits of mean-field or quadratic bosonic descriptions using controllable trapped-ion platforms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses the challenge of observing non-Gaussian dynamics in quantum many-body systems, as simple Gaussian approximations fail to capture all essential physical properties.

**Main result.** The authors experimentally demonstrate a clear crossover from predictable Gaussian behavior to genuinely non-Gaussian dynamics using trapped ions under strong initial excitation conditions.

**Method.** The research combines experimental measurements on spin correlations with theoretical comparisons against mean-field solutions and low-order approximations like Wick's theorem.

**Model / system.** The system is a two-dimensional XY model realized using $N=200$ trapped $^{171}	ext{Yb}^+$ ions. The Hamiltonian describes long-range, spatially structured interactions between the spins.

**Key observables.** Single-spin observables ($\langle\sigma_z^i(t)angle$), two-spin correlations ($\langle\sigma_z^i(t)\sigma_z^j(t)angle$), and four-spin correlations (in the X basis).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Initial excitation strength parameter $	heta$ (tested at weak $0.05\pi$ and strong $0.25\pi$ regimes); temperature ($T=6	ext{ K}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is robust because the non-Gaussian violation is shown to persist beyond experimental noise, independent of assumptions about Hamiltonian miscalibration or decoherence modeling.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare measured two-spin correlations against mean-field and Wick's theorem predictions, showing deviations under strong excitation. Other figures detail the experimental setup using multiple laser beams for ion trapping and interaction generation.

**Paper structure.** The paper details the physical realization of the XY model on trapped ions, presents theoretical approximations (HP transformation, Wick's theorem), measures various spin correlation functions across different initial excitation regimes, and concludes by quantifying the deviation from Gaussian predictions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Non-Gaussian evolution of high-order spin correlations characterizes important properties of quantum many-body systems. In practice, decoherence, statistical fluctuation and miscalibration of experimental parameters all hinder the witness of non-Gaussian dynamics. Here we demonstrate the crossover between Gaussian and non-Gaussian dynamics on a two-dimensional XY model with long-range and spatially structured interaction using a trapped ion quantum simulator. We prepare different initial densities of magnon excitations and verify the dynamics of single-spin observables for the engineered Hamiltonian. Then we compare the high-order spin correlations with the mean-field solution and the Holstein-Primakoff approximation, and demonstrate the non-Gaussian behavior in a way independent of the calibration errors. Our work provides a verifiable path from classically simulatable dynamics to regimes where quantum advantage may emerge.

</details>

### [Driven dynamics of an attractive Bose polaron](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13645v1)

**Summary.** The paper analyzes the driven dynamics of an impurity polaron coupled to a 1D Bose gas with attractive interactions. It demonstrates that the system exhibits drifted Bloch oscillations and derives unique scaling laws for the drift velocity ($V_d \sim F^\alpha$ with $\alpha < 1$). The research highlights how interaction strength and force fundamentally alter momentum transfer mechanisms in quantum gases.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides detailed insights into non-equilibrium transport phenomena in quantum many-body systems, particularly how attractive interactions modify fundamental dynamics like Bloch oscillations compared to repulsive cases.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the out-of-equilibrium dynamics of an impurity (polaron) driven by a constant external force through a 1D system of weakly-interacting bosons.

**Main result.** For attractive interactions, the drift velocity exhibits a sub-linear dependence on the applied force ($V_d \sim F^\alpha$ with $\alpha < 1$), and the Bloch oscillation amplitude shows rich non-monotonic behavior (decay $ightarrow$ minimum $ightarrow$ revival).

**Method.** The analysis combines theoretical derivations of stationary solutions in the weak coupling limit ($\gamma \ll 1$) with numerical simulations solving the time-dependent Gross-Pitaevskii equation.

**Model / system.** The model is a Bose polaron system in one dimension, described by an effective Hamiltonian involving the impurity momentum and the bosonic field. The interaction between the impurity and bosons is assumed to be attractive ($G<0$).

**Key observables.** Drift velocity ($V_d$), Bloch oscillation amplitude ($V_B$), oscillation period ($T$), and the total phase variation across the system.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The sign of the impurity-boson interaction ($G>0$ vs $G<0$), the strength of the external force ($F$), and the mass ratio ($M/m$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the weak coupling limit ($\gamma \ll 1$) and compares results derived from stationary state solutions against full time-evolution dynamics.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate energy vs. momentum showing forbidden intervals, time evolution of velocity/density profiles demonstrating shockwave emission, and plots quantifying $V_d$, $V_B$, and $T$ dependence on the driving force $	ilde{F}$.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the dynamics by first establishing stationary solutions at zero force, then numerically simulating time evolution under a small driving force to observe drifted Bloch oscillations, and finally characterizing the scaling laws ($V_d \propto F^\alpha$) across different force regimes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the out-of-equilibrium dynamics of an impurity driven by a constant external force through a system of homogeneous weakly-interacting bosons in one spatial dimension. The impurity-boson interaction is assumed to be attractive. We show that the impurity exhibits drifted Bloch oscillations in a wide range of forces in the absence of a lattice. We characterize the dynamical response of the host bosons and explain the mechanism underlying the Bloch oscillations. We analyse the behavior of the drift velocity, the Bloch amplitude and the time period of oscillations in a wide range of forces and other system parameters. In contrast to the case of repulsive impurity-boson interaction, the drift velocity exhibits a sub-linear dependence on a weak applied force, $V_d\sim {F}^α$ with a positive exponent $α$ smaller than unity. The drift velocity monotonically increases with force, though the scaling behavior varies considerably across different regimes of $F$. Moreover, the amplitude of the velocity oscillations displays rich behavior: it first undergoes a decay with force, reaches a minimum, and then presents a revival, increasing with force.

</details>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 5. (a), (b), (c) Two-dimensional diffraction intensity Ip(θx, θy) as a function of sin(θx) and sin(θy) for NV, TF (N = 0.2), SV (N = 0.2). (d), (e), (f) the corresponding contour plot of phase Φ/π for NV, TF, SV reservoir. Other parameters are assumed as follows: R/γ = 0.1, ∆c/γ = 0.2, Ωc0/γ = 0.1, L/z0 = 0.1, ∆p/γ = ϕ = 0, Nx = Ny = 5 and Λx/λp = Λy/λp = 4.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper theoretically explores controlling light propagation through an electromagnetically induced grating by coupling the system to different quantum reservoirs. It demonstrates that while thermal environments enhance overall modulation, squeezed vacuum reservoirs offer superior control, enabling highly selective amplification of specific diffracted angles via detuning. This establishes reservoir engineering as a versatile platform for programmable photonic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses open quantum systems dynamics in nonlinear optics, showing how engineered environmental coupling (reservoir engineering) provides a powerful tool to control macroscopic wave phenomena like diffraction efficiency and directionality.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how coupling a two-level medium, forming an electromagnetically induced grating (EIG), to engineered quantum reservoirs modifies the transmission and far-field diffraction of a weak probe field.

**Main result.** Reservoir statistics—specifically thermal vs. squeezed vacuum—significantly control the diffraction response; squeezing allows for highly directional power redistribution and amplification via detuning control.

**Method.** The analysis uses perturbative solutions to the optical Bloch equations in the weak-driving regime, followed by calculating the linear susceptibility and applying Fraunhofer diffraction theory.

**Model / system.** The system models an ensemble of two-level atoms driven by a standing-wave field and interrogated by a probe field, coupled to engineered reservoirs (Normal Vacuum, Thermal Field, or Squeezed Vacuum).

**Key observables.** Probe susceptibility ($\chi$), spatial transmission function $T(x)$, and far-field diffraction intensity patterns $I_p(	heta_x, 	heta_y)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Reservoir type (NV, TF, SV), reservoir detuning ($\delta/\gamma$), mean photon number ($N$), and the strength of the driving fields.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on a weak probe field assumption, utilizing Born–Markov and rotating-wave approximations to derive the effective Bloch equations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate $T(x)$ for different reservoirs (NV, TF, SV) and show 1D/2D diffraction intensities demonstrating how reservoir parameters control spectral components and angular distribution.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the theoretical model and derives the necessary Bloch equations. It then presents numerical results analyzing 1D and 2D EIGs under various reservoir conditions, concluding with a discussion of applications like beam steering.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We theoretically investigate the transmission and diffraction of a weak probe field from an electromagnetically induced grating formed in a weakly driven two-level medium coupled to engineered quantum reservoirs. Using a perturbative solution of the optical Bloch equations in the weak-driving regime, we analyze how normal-vacuum, thermal, and broadband squeezed-vacuum environments modify the probe susceptibility and consequently reshape both the spatial transmission function and the far-field diffraction patterns. We show that reservoir statistics have a pronounced impact on the diffraction response by altering the amplitude and phase of the induced grating. Thermal reservoirs enhance the transmission modulation and increase the intensity of the dominant diffraction orders, whereas squeezed-vacuum reservoirs generate strongly phase-sensitive modifications that selectively redistribute optical power among diffraction channels. We further demonstrate that the detuning between the squeezed reservoir and the driving field provides an efficient mechanism for controlling diffraction directionality, leading to substantial amplification of selected angular orders. In two-dimensional geometries, squeezed-vacuum correlations produce highly structured phase landscapes and strongly anisotropic diffraction patterns, enabling directional enhancement of specific diffraction channels while suppressing others. These results establish reservoir engineering as a versatile approach for controlling transmission, diffraction efficiency, and angular selectivity in minimal two-level systems, with potential applications in programmable photonic devices, beam steering, and quantum optical platforms.

</details>

### [A refined thermodynamic analysis of nonsecular master equations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13504v1)

**Authors:** Mohamed Boubakour, Talia Szikman, Cyril Elouard  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13504v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This paper establishes a consistent thermodynamic framework for analyzing complex, nonsecular master equations describing open quantum systems. It systematically compares different physical definitions of entropy production and energy flow, showing how transient coherent effects modify standard predictions derived from simpler secular approximations. The work provides crucial insights into the limitations and necessary corrections when modeling real-world quantum dissipation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for any theoretical physicist studying open quantum systems, as it rigorously addresses the breakdown of standard secular approximations and provides necessary corrections to the first and second laws in non-equilibrium settings.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops a unified thermodynamic framework for analyzing nonsecular master equations, which arise when approximations are used that cause the system to relax toward a steady state different from the true Gibbs equilibrium.

**Main result.** It shows that while two definitions of entropy production (microscopic vs. Spohn inequality) differ due to transient coherent effects in nonsecular dynamics, no net work can be extracted cyclically from the steady state when coupled to a single thermal bath.

**Method.** The analysis employs systematic perturbation theory starting from a microscopic second law based on system-environment correlations, comparing results derived from different approximations like Geometric-Arithmetic Master Equations (GAME) and Local Nonsecular Master Equations (LNME).

**Model / system.** The model is an open quantum system coupled to thermal reservoirs (baths), described by master equations that go beyond the standard secular Lindblad form. The analysis compares dynamics derived from Born-Markov approximations.

**Key observables.** Entropy production rates ($\dot{\sigma}_{tot}$ vs $\dot{\sigma}_{Sp}$), energy balance components (system, bath, interaction energy), and stationary coherences in the energy eigenbasis.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Weak system-bath coupling regime; time scales related to bath correlation decay ($	au_B$) versus system relaxation ($\gamma^{-1}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations such as the Born-Markov approximation and specific limiting procedures (e.g., deriving GAME from CGME).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate comparisons of heat flux components across different master equation regimes (local vs. full secular) and show the evolution of stationary coherence.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds by first establishing the Coarse-Grained Master Equation (CGME), then refining it through approximations like GAME, and finally performing a detailed thermodynamic comparison between microscopic entropy production and definitions based on system dynamics inequalities.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a systematic thermodynamic analysis of nonsecular master equations. We consider master equations resulting either from the partial secular and the geometric-arithmetic approximations, two approximations ensuring the positivity of the system's dynamics when some of its transition frequencies are too small to enable the full secular approximation. Both cause the system to relax towards a steady state which is not the Gibbs state of its bare Hamiltonian. Nonetheless, we build a unified, consistent thermodynamic framework for those dynamics. Starting from a microscopic expression of the second law based on system-environment correlations, we employ a systematic perturbation theory to preserve the positivity of the second law despite the approximations done on the dynamics. We show that, in spite of the weak system-bath coupling, the system-bath interaction energy participates to the energy balance, as well as the Lamb-shift. Those extra contributions give rise to work performed by the system on the bath when the former is out of equilibrium. We compare this microscopic entropy production with the definition based on the contractivity of the reduced system dynamics (Spohn inequality). We show that, unlike for secular master equations, the two entropy production rates differ because of the presence of non-vanishing stationary coherences in the energy eigenbasis. However, in the case of a single thermal bath, the difference is purely transient, and no work can be cyclically extracted from the steady-state despite its non-Gibbs form. Finally, we illustrate our results with a simple example, clarifying and completing the thermodynamic picture of Markovian dynamics in the quantum regime.

</details>

### [Influence-solvability: a systematic theory of $(1+1)D$ solvability and its application to brickwork circuits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12538v1)

**Authors:** Friedrich Hübner, Sun Woo P. Kim  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12538v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **4/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1: We say a circuit is influence-solvable if its influence-matrix is given by a uniform MPS of finite-bond dimension χ for all times t. Note that A is independent of t.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12538_figures/2606.12538_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">

Here, evolution of an observable O of a joint system between S coupled to an infinite circuit. The tensor A represents an exact finite memory bath generated by the circuit on the left.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12538_figures/2606.12538_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3: Examples of scenarios applicable to influence-solvability. Any time-evolved observable ⟨Ox(t)⟩or auto-correlator of the form ⟨O(n) x (tn) · · · O(1) x (t1)⟩are tractable in the above scenarios. Other applications include computation of entanglement entropies (see Sec. V C), computation of correlation functions (see Sec. V B) and potentially computing diffusion constants (see Sec. V A).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12538_figures/2606.12538_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">

The gate is influence-solvable for γ⊤= (γ0 γ1) at zeros of this function. </figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper establishes 'influence-solvability' as a rigorous criterion for classifying solvable dynamics in one-dimensional circuit models. By requiring the influence matrix to have a finite MPS representation, it provides necessary and sufficient local conditions. This allows researchers to efficiently compute long-time evolution of quantum observables by effectively integrating out the infinite bath.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful mathematical tool (influence-solvability) to tame the complexity of open quantum systems dynamics in 1D lattices, allowing for exact computation of long-time behavior that would otherwise be intractable.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to develop a systematic, general theory for classifying 'solvable' dynamics in $(1+1)D$ circuit models, as current classification methods are incomplete.

**Main result.** The authors introduce the concept of 'influence-solvability,' which characterizes circuits whose influence matrix can be represented by a finite bond-dimension Matrix Product State (MPS). This framework successfully encompasses known solvable systems and yields new classifications for brickwork circuits.

**Method.** The theory relies on deriving necessary and sufficient local conditions for influence-solvability using advanced MPS formalism, particularly adapting the fundamental theorem of MPS to open boundary conditions.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on $(1+1)D$ circuit models (both classical and quantum), specifically applying the framework to brickwork circuits coupled to a system $S$. The dynamics are analyzed via the influence matrix, which captures the coupling to an infinite bath.

**Key observables.** Influence Matrix, Effective Time Evolution Operator ($G_{	ext{eff}}$), Bond Dimension ($\chi$), Local Observables $\langle O(t) angle$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Bond dimension $\chi$ (determines the complexity of the bath memory); Local dimension $d$; Time $t$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory assumes that solvability is equivalent to the influence matrix being a uniform MPS with finite bond dimension. Some known solvable circuits, like DU($n \ge 3$), are noted as potential exceptions.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate how influence-solvability simplifies infinite circuit dynamics into an efficiently computable finite tensor network for calculating subsystem observables over long times.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the definition of influence-solvability, derives necessary and sufficient local conditions using MPS theorems, applies these conditions to systematically classify classical and quantum brickwork circuits, and discusses the computational advantages gained by this classification.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

`Solvable' circuits, such as dual unitaries and its generalisations, have arisen as paradigmatic examples of tractable chaotic non-equilibrium dynamics, both in classical and quantum systems. However, while increasingly more complicated sufficient conditions have been proposed, a systematic theory classifying and understanding general features of solvable circuits is missing. We develop such a theory by introducing influence-solvable circuits, a class of $(1+1)D$ circuits whose influence matrix, which represents the `bath' generated by its own evolution, is given by a uniform MPS with finite bond-dimension $χ$. This property allows for efficient computation of subsystem dynamics and essentially contains all known examples of solvable circuits. We derive a set of necessary and sufficient local conditions by using a version of the fundamental theorem of MPS for open boundary conditions. Next we apply our theory to brickwork circuits with $χ=1$ influence-solvability and perform a systematic classification of classical brickwork circuits with local dimension up to $d=3$ and quantum brickwork circuits with $d=2$. Our search reveals new solvable circuits that are not captured by known solvability conditions.

</details>

### [Beyond the Unruh vacuum: multi-time correlations in black hole collapse and evaporation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13383v1)

**Authors:** Konstantinos Xenos, Charis Anastopoulos, Andreas F. Terzis  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13383v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **4/5** · 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
