**Summary.** The paper argues that the black hole information paradox is based on an incomplete picture derived only from single-time measurements of Hawking radiation. By analyzing higher-order, multi-time quantum correlations, the authors demonstrate a 'memory term' that explicitly carries information about the matter that collapsed, suggesting these temporal correlations are key to resolving the paradox.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work extends concepts from open quantum systems theory to extreme astrophysical environments, showing how non-Markovian effects (captured by multi-time correlations) can preserve information otherwise lost under single-time approximations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The black hole information paradox arises because Hawking radiation appears thermal, suggesting information loss. The authors investigate if pre-collapse information can survive in measurable correlations beyond single-time observables.

**Main result.** Multi-time quantum-field correlations (specifically the memory term $C^{(2)}_{	ext{mem}}$) are shown to depend explicitly on parameters characterizing the initial pre-collapse state, indicating that information is encoded in higher-order temporal correlations.

**Method.** The analysis uses multi-time correlation functions, such as the second-order coherence function ($g^{(2)}$), calculated using advanced techniques like the Schwinger–Keldysh formalism and complex contour integration.

**Model / system.** The system is a quantum field ($\hat{\phi}$) undergoing gravitational collapse and evaporation in a two-dimensional model. The analysis contrasts measurements constrained by single-time observables (like those from the Hawking-Wald theorem) with joint probability distributions for multiple detection events.

**Key observables.** Second-order coherence function $g^{(2)}(t_1, E_1; t_2, E_2)$, decomposed into Unruh vacuum ($C^{(2)}_U$), memory ($C^{(2)}_{	ext{mem}}$), and correlation ($C^{(2)}_{	ext{corr}}$) terms.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pre-collapse state parameters (e.g., $D_0$), energy differences ($\Delta E$), and time separations ($\Delta t$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that multi-time measurements are possible and that the thermal approximation based solely on the reduced density matrix is incomplete.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate complex contours used for evaluating correlation integrals, showing the decomposition of coherence functions into Unruh components and non-Unruh memory/correlation parts.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds by first establishing that single-time measurements yield thermal results (like the Unruh vacuum), then introducing multi-time correlations via $g^{(2)}$, and finally demonstrating mathematically that specific terms in this correlation function retain dependence on initial conditions, violating pure thermality.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The black hole information paradox originates from the thermal character of Hawking radiation, which appears to erase information about the collapsing matter. However, thermality constrains only observables defined at a single time and leaves the structure of temporal quantum correlations largely unexplored. Here we show that multi-time quantum-field correlations provide a concrete mechanism for the survival of pre-collapse information in black hole evaporation. Using a two-dimensional model of gravitational collapse and evaporation, we demonstrate that late-time multi-time correlations are not fully reproduced by the Unruh vacuum. In particular, they contain a contribution that depends explicitly on parameters characterizing the pre-collapse state, despite the thermal character of the asymptotic radiation. Our results identify measurable multi-time correlations as carriers of information in Hawking radiation and suggest that formulations of the black hole information paradox based solely on single-time observables are incomplete.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.13606"></a>
### [Diffusive Dynamics of Nonstabilizerness](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13606v1)

**Authors:** Zhenyu Xiao, Shinsei Ryu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13606v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13606_figures/2606.13606_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. (a) Disorder-averaged four-replica transfer network for |ρ⊗4 t ⟩⟩. Blue tensor: Haar-averaged U(1)-symmetric gate W [Eq. (5)]; yellow tensor: averaged initial state |ρ⊗4 0 ⟩⟩[Eq. (4)]. (b,c) Contractions with I⊗4 and Q defining two-site transfer matrices with dominant eigenvalues λmax;tr and λmax;Q, whose ratio determines the annealed entropy density. (d) Two-site update of the infinite matrix product state: apply W, restore canonical form, and truncate by singular value decomposition within the S4 blocks. (e) Annealed stabilizer R´enyi entropy density m2 A versus time. Inset: late-time behavior on a log-log scale.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13606_figures/2606.13606_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. (a) U(1) random circuit: inverse gap ∆M −1 2 (t; N) for several N, linear in t in the diffusive window. Inset: post- Thouless exponential tail on a semi-log scale. (b) Higher R´enyi gaps in the same circuit at N = 24, plotted as [∆Mq]−2/q</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work analyzes the dynamics of nonstabilizerness, a key resource measure, in symmetry-constrained many-body quantum systems. Using advanced tensor network methods on random circuits and Ising chains, the authors prove that the approach to equilibrium follows a universal diffusive scaling law proportional to $t^{-1}$. This establishes hydrodynamics as a fundamental principle governing resource relaxation in such chaotic quantum dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete example of how hydrodynamic principles govern the relaxation of quantum resources (nonstabilizerness) in chaotic systems, linking concepts from entanglement theory to classical statistical mechanics universality classes.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how symmetries influence the dynamics of nonstabilizerness, a quantum resource complementary to entanglement, in many-body systems.

**Main result.** The late-time approach of nonstabilizerness to its random-state value exhibits diffusive scaling characterized by an algebraic decay $\Delta m_2(t) \propto t^{-1}$.

**Method.** The analysis employs replica tensor networks and infinite time-evolving block decimation (iTEBD) techniques applied to the disorder-averaged dynamics in the thermodynamic limit.

**Model / system.** The primary models are one-dimensional U(1)-symmetric random quantum circuits and energy-conserving nonintegrable Ising chains. The analysis is performed on the stabilizer Rényi entropy, which quantifies nonstabilizerness.

**Key observables.** Stabilizer Rényi entropy density ($m_2^A$), the gap to the Haar-random state ($\Delta m_2(t)$), and finite-size R\'enyi gaps ($\Delta M_q$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The decay is observed in a diffusive window $	au \ll t \ll t_D$, where $t_D$ scales as $N^2/D$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the ensemble average restoring translation invariance, allowing for an iMPS representation in the thermodynamic limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the replica tensor network structure and plot the time evolution of the entropy density, confirming the late-time $t^{-1}$ scaling and higher-order gaps $\Delta M_q \propto t^{-q/2}$.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces nonstabilizerness via stabilizer Rényi entropy in U(1) random circuits. It then develops the tensor network machinery to calculate disorder-averaged dynamics, culminating in the identification of a universal diffusive scaling law confirmed by comparison with an Ising chain model.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Symmetries shape the quantum-information dynamics of many-body systems, but their effect on nonstabilizerness, the resource complementary to entanglement, is less understood. We compute the stabilizer Rényi entropy, a measure of nonstabilizerness, in $\mathrm{U}(1)$-symmetric one-dimensional random circuits. The disorder-averaged dynamics is captured by a four-replica tensor network, which we evaluate by $S_4$-adapted infinite time-evolving block decimation (iTEBD) directly in the thermodynamic limit. Together with a hydrodynamic argument, our results identify a diffusive universality class for the late-time approach of nonstabilizerness to its random-state value, with the stabilizer Rényi entropy gap closing as $1/t$. The same scaling is verified in an energy-conserving nonintegrable Ising chain. More broadly, our framework provides a hydrodynamic perspective on nonstabilizerness generation and offers insight into the design of approximate Haar-random states in Hamiltonian dynamics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.13412"></a>
### [Kerr-induced nonreciprocal transparency and group delay in a hybrid cavity magnomechanical system](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13412v1)

**Summary.** The paper theoretically investigates how Kerr nonlinearity can induce nonreciprocal behavior in coupled magnon-photon-phonon systems. By controlling the system's couplings, researchers demonstrate the ability to generate multiple transparency windows and precisely tune the group delay, enabling directional microwave photonic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO physics as it explores cavity QED/optomechanics principles by integrating magnonics and nonlinear effects (Kerr) to achieve directional light control, a key area in quantum photonics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research aims to realize and investigate nonreciprocal transparency, Fano resonances, and control slow/fast light propagation in a hybrid cavity magnomechanical system.

**Main result.** The Kerr nonlinearity enables controllable nonreciprocal transmission and allows for reversible switching between slow-light and fast-light regimes by engineering the dispersion relation.

**Method.** The study uses theoretical modeling based on the quantum Langevin formalism to analyze the output field amplitude, absorption spectrum, and group delay.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is a hybrid cavity magnomechanical system comprising two YIG spheres (magnons), a mechanical resonator (phonons), and coupled within a microwave cavity mode (photons). The dynamics are governed by interactions including optomagnonical coupling, magnon-phonon coupling, and the magnon Kerr nonlinearity.

**Key observables.** Nonreciprocal absorption contrast ($\epsilon_{NP}$), nonreciprocal group delay factor ($	au_{NP}$), transparency windows, and dispersion spectra.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Kerr nonlinearity strength ($\Delta K$), various coupling strengths ($g_r, G_r$), cavity decay rates ($\kappa_c$), and magnon/phonon damping rates.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on linearization procedures and treating the solution to first order in the weak probe field. Specific approximations regarding detunings relative to decay rates are also noted.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate spectral responses (absorption) showing multiple transparency windows controlled by coupling strengths, and plots of phase/group delay ($	au$ vs $\delta$) demonstrating tunable nonreciprocal effects depending on the Kerr sign.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the system architecture and then develops the theoretical model using Hamiltonian and Langevin equations. Subsequent sections analyze MMIT, the role of the Kerr nonlinearity in generating Fano resonances, and finally detail the control over group delay and nonreciprocity.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a scheme for realizing nonreciprocal transparency, Fano resonances, and slow/fast light in a hybrid cavity magnomechanical system containing two YIG spheres and a mechanical resonator. The nonreciprocal behavior originates from the magnon Kerr nonlinearity, which induces direction-dependent frequency shifts and modifies the interference pathways among cavity photons, magnons, and phonons. We show that the hybrid system supports multiple transparency windows arising from magnon- and magnomechanical-induced interference processes. The Kerr interaction strongly reshapes these transparency features, producing asymmetric Fano line shapes and enabling controllable nonreciprocal transmission. Furthermore, the associated dispersion exhibits pronounced directional asymmetry, leading to giant differences in the group delay for opposite propagation directions and allowing reversible switching between slow- and fast-light regimes. We investigate the roles of hybrid coupling strengths and dissipation channels and identify parameter regimes where the nonreciprocal response is maximized. These findings establish Kerr-engineered magnomechanical systems as promising platforms for integrated nonreciprocal microwave photonics and quantum information technologies.

</details>

### [Statistical Mechanics and Symmetries of Non-Abelian Anyon Proliferation: From Deformation to Decoherence](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12527v1)

**Summary.** This work develops a statistical mechanics approach to study how non-Abelian topological order breaks down under decoherence. By modeling the system using coupled loop models and analyzing anyon condensation, the authors determine critical error thresholds and characterize the symmetry structure of the resulting trivial phases.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful framework connecting abstract topological order concepts to measurable thermodynamic quantities (like susceptibility or cumulants) in the presence of realistic environmental noise.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how topological order, maintained by non-Abelian anyons, degrades due to environmental noise (decoherence) or wavefunction deformations.

**Main result.** The proliferation of multiple non-Abelian anyon species can drive the system into a trivial phase below a finite error threshold, and the resulting symmetry structure is characterized by $G 	imes 	ext{Rep}(G)$.

**Method.** The authors employ statistical mechanics models derived from quantum double structures, utilizing Monte Carlo simulations, Binder cumulants, and analyzing anyon syndrome distributions.

**Model / system.** The system models topological order based on non-Abelian anyons (e.g., in $D_4$ TO), analyzed through coupled loop models, spin Hamiltonians, and the resulting statistical mechanics partition functions.

**Key observables.** Binder cumulants ($U$), correlation functions of Wilson operators ($\langle n_R^b \cdot n_R^g angle$), purity ($	ext{tr}(ho^2)$), and anyon syndrome distributions $p(\{a_v\})$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Decoherence rates ($p_\alpha$), deformation parameters ($eta_R, eta_B$), and the critical error threshold $p_c(\infty)$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on mapping complex quantum dynamics onto effective classical statistical mechanics models (e.g., loop/string-net models) and assuming specific symmetry structures.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate lattice structures, phase diagrams showing condensation thresholds for multiple anyon species ($mR, mB, eG$), and plots of Binder cumulants diagnosing transitions.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops the statistical mechanics description by deriving loop models from quantum double algebras, analyzing stability under noise via syndrome distributions, and mapping out phase diagrams based on condensation thresholds for different anyon species.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Topological quantum computation relies on braiding non-Abelian anyons, but requires the underlying topological order to survive imperfect state preparation and environmental noise. We show that the instability of topological order to wavefunction deformations and to decoherence, with the latter probed by syndrome distributions, are generically captured by stat-mech models whose symmetries naturally expose the corrupting anyonic excitations. As an example, we combine this framework with Monte-Carlo simulations to resolve the stability of $D_4$ topological order under deformations and quantum channels that proliferate multiple non-Abelian anyon species that individually are unable to condense. We show that beyond a finite threshold, proliferation of two non-Abelian anyon species parasitically condenses a shared Abelian-anyon fusion outcome, destroying the topological order. Our symmetry-based approach sharply differentiates the resulting trivial phase from that obtained by condensing all Abelian charges; in other words, the trivial phase "remembers" which anyons condensed. This framework provides a first step into identifying the relevant symmetry for optimal decoders, conditioned on syndrome measurements, of non-Abelian topological order.

</details>

### [Multi-entropy in heavy local quenches](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12526v1)

**Summary.** This work analyzes the genuine multi-entropy following heavy local quenches in holographic CFTs. By comparing bulk geodesic calculations with boundary CFT correlators, the authors show that this measure is governed by global topological constraints rather than local physics. This provides a powerful tool for understanding multipartite entanglement dynamics in strongly coupled quantum matter.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a deep connection between entanglement structure (multi-entropy) and global geometric properties (saddle selection/geodesic topology) in strongly coupled quantum systems, which is relevant to understanding thermalization dynamics beyond simple bipartite measures.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the time evolution of multipartite entanglement, specifically using genuine multi-entropy, following heavy local quenches in holographic CFTs.

**Main result.** The genuine multi-entropy is found to be controlled by global saddle selection mechanisms rather than purely local energy responses or quasiparticle propagation. In the sharp quench limit, its time dependence is kinematically fixed to logarithms of rational functions of time.

**Method.** The analysis combines holographic duality (geodesic minimization in AdS$_3$) with CFT calculations using replica methods and heavy-light conformal blocks.

**Model / system.** The system modeled is a two-dimensional holographic Conformal Field Theory undergoing a heavy local quench, dual to the dynamics of a falling massive particle in AdS$_3$.

**Key observables.** Genuine multi-entropy ($S^{(q)}_n$), bipartite entanglement entropy, and vacuum-subtracted genuine tripartite multi-entropy ($\Delta G_M^{(3)}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Heavy operator dimension, quench width ($\delta$), and the ratio of mass to AdS curvature scale.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations such as the heavy-light vacuum block approximation and assumes a holographic dual structure for the physics.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the minimal geodesic network $\Gamma$ in the bulk, showing how multi-entropy is calculated via geodesic lengths, and depict time evolution profiles of genuine tri-entropy across different quench regimes (conical vs. BTZ).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the multi-entropy diagnostics to performing perturbative analyses (showing first-order cancellation), then moving to fully back-reacted calculations in both conical and BTZ regimes, culminating in deriving kinematic formulas for the sharp-quench limit.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the time evolution of tripartite entanglement in heavy local quenches in two-dimensional holographic conformal field theories. Our diagnostic is the genuine multi-entropy of adjacent intervals, computed from both bulk and boundary perspectives. A perturbative bulk analysis shows that the first-order small-mass perturbation around the vacuum geodesic network cancels identically at any time after the quench. In the fully back-reacted geometry, a vacuum-subtracted genuine multi-entropy arises from a mismatch between the winding selected by the trivalent geodesic network and the windings selected independently by the pairwise geodesics. In the sharp quench limit, the time dependence of genuine multi-entropy is kinematically fixed to logarithms of rational functions of time and is independent of the heavy operator dimension. The CFT calculation reproduces the same formula within the heavy-light vacuum block approximation, where the branch choice in the heavy-background uniformization map corresponds to the winding selection in the bulk. These results indicate that, in this setup, the genuine multi-entropy is controlled by global saddle selection, rather than by a local energy response or quasiparticle propagation.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12914"></a>
### [Quantum charge pumping in helical systems: A comparative study of short- and long-range hopping](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12914v1)

**Authors:** Leila Eslami, Santanu K. Maiti, Fatemeh Bourbour  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12914v1>

**Summary.** The paper theoretically investigates quantum charge pumping in helical nanostructures by comparing short-range versus long-range electron hopping models. Using the Keldysh NEGF formalism, the authors find that long-range hopping facilitates robust, plateau-like DC currents characteristic of adiabatic pumping. Crucially, they demonstrate that the structural decay exponent ($\ell_c$) provides a geometric control mechanism to tune this quantum current.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed theoretical framework for understanding how geometric structure (chirality/helicity) and electronic coupling range influence non-equilibrium quantum transport phenomena in low-dimensional molecular conductors.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates quantum charge pumping mechanisms in single-stranded helical structures by comparing the effects of short-range versus long-range electron hopping.

**Main result.** Long-range hopping (LRH) leads to pronounced plateau-like regions in the pumped DC current under adiabatic conditions, while the decay exponent ($\ell_c$) acts as a structural knob to tune this current. Short-range hopping (SRH) yields less robust and more parameter-sensitive currents.

**Method.** The Keldysh non-equilibrium Green's function (NEGF) approach is employed to calculate time-dependent transport properties, utilizing the Floquet representation for periodically driven systems.

**Model / system.** The system is a single-stranded helical molecule modeled by a tight-binding Hamiltonian incorporating both short-range and long-range hopping terms. The structure is subjected to time-periodic potentials applied at its ends.

**Key observables.** Spectral current, pumped DC current ($J_{dc}$), dependence of $J_{dc}$ on chemical potential ($\mu$), phase lag ($\delta$), driving frequency ($\Omega$), and amplitude ($V_p$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hopping range (SRH vs. LRH), decay exponent ($\ell_c$), drive frequency ($\Omega$), and phase difference ($\delta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis compares adiabatic transport regimes (low $\Omega$) with high-frequency, non-adiabatic dynamics; the use of NEGF formalism is central to the calculation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate $J_{dc}$ vs. $\mu$ showing plateaus for LRH in the adiabatic limit; they also show the dependence on phase lag ($\delta$), driving amplitude ($V_p$), and spectral function comparisons across different hopping regimes and frequencies.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically compares the theoretical predictions of quantum charge pumping using NEGF, first establishing the model Hamiltonian for helical systems, then analyzing the distinct transport signatures arising from LRH versus SRH under varying driving conditions ($\Omega$, $\delta$).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Using the Keldysh non-equilibrium Green's function approach, we investigate charge pumping through a single-stranded helical structure described by a tight-binding model that includes either short-range hopping (SRH) or long-range hopping (LRH). While quantum pumping has been studied in various low-dimensional systems, the detailed behavior of the spectral current and the pumped dc current in helical geometries in the presence of higher-order electron hopping (beyond nearest neighbors) has not yet been systematically explored. Here, we focus on the interplay between helicity and extended hopping ranges, analyzing how they jointly control the energy-resolved and dc pumped currents under time-periodic end potentials. For LRH, the pumped dc current exhibits pronounced plateau-like regions as a function of chemical potential when energy levels are sparsely spaced -- consistent with adiabatic transport -- whereas SRH yields more parameter-sensitive currents without clear plateaus. The plateau stability is controlled by the drive frequency: at higher frequencies, Floquet side-band mixing destroys the plateaus, leading to oscillatory currents. The phase dependence remains nearly sinusoidal, and the current vanishes at zero phase lag, confirming the necessity of out-of-phase potentials. Crucially, in helical systems, the decay exponent $(\ell_c)$ acts as an effective structural parameter that can tune both the magnitude and sign of the pumped current, offering a geometric knob for controlling quantum pumping. Our findings not only fill a gap in the understanding of spectral and pumped currents in helical systems with extended hopping but also provide tools that can be applied to analyze similar phenomena in other chiral or quasi-one-dimensional systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.12813"></a>
### [Accidental Symmetry in the Tavis-Cummings Model via the Schwinger Boson Representation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12813v1)

**Authors:** Plato Deliyannis, Iman Marvian

The states are in the format |m⟩⊗|k⟩, where m and k are eigenvalues of Jz and a†a, respectively. Note that there are two types of 1D invariant sectors: states in the form |j = 0, m = 0⟩⊗|k⟩ for all k ≥0, and the state |j = 1, m = −1⟩⊗|k = 0⟩. All of these 1D sectors have eigenvalue 0 of HTC. The accidental symmetry pairs |j = 1, m = −1⟩⊗|k = 0⟩and |j = 0, m = 0⟩|k = 2⟩and implies they should have the same eigenvalue, a constraint that is not necessarily satisfied by arbitrary PI, U(1)-invariant Hamiltonians.</figcaption>

**Summary.** The paper uncovers a previously unnoticed 'accidental' symmetry within the Tavis-Cummings Hamiltonian governing light-matter interaction in multi-qubit systems. By employing advanced algebraic techniques using Schwinger bosons, the authors prove that this symmetry imposes strong constraints on system evolution. This finding has significant implications for understanding the controllability and theoretical limits of quantum computing architectures based on such interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides deep structural insights into complex many-body quantum models, revealing symmetries that are not obvious from simple inspection. Such accidental symmetries are crucial for understanding model limitations and designing robust quantum control schemes in cavity QED or superconducting circuit platforms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is identifying an additional, independent 'accidental' symmetry within the Tavis-Cummings (TC) Hamiltonian that goes beyond standard permutation and U(1) symmetries.

**Main result.** This accidental symmetry imposes strong constraints on the system's unitary evolution, leading to relationships between otherwise distinct quantum sectors of the Hilbert space.

**Method.** The analysis uses advanced techniques involving Schwinger boson representations of angular momentum and classifying invariant subspaces based on charge and angular momentum quantum numbers.

**Model / system.** The model is the Tavis-Cummings (TC) Hamiltonian, describing $n$ identical qubits coupled to a single bosonic mode. The system's dynamics are analyzed in terms of total angular momentum operators ($J_\pm$) and bosonic occupation number ($a^\dagger a$).

**Key observables.** Conserved observables derived from the accidental symmetry, such as specific expectation values related to sector couplings $S_{j, j'}$ or the second moment $\langle H^2 	ext{TC}(\phi) angle$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** $n$ (number of qubits), coupling strength ($g_{TC}$), and bosonic frequency ($\omega_{osc}$). The analysis is particularly relevant for $n \ge 3$ qubits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the validity of specific algebraic mappings derived from Schwinger's boson representation, which allows relating different sectors of the Hilbert space.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the conservation law associated with the accidental symmetry by comparing evolution under standard Hamiltonians versus violation under $J_z^2$. They also show pairs of unitarily equivalent sectors (one filled, one unfilled) related by the symmetry transformation.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from identifying known symmetries to proposing and characterizing a new 'accidental' symmetry. It then uses algebraic methods (Schwinger representation) and detailed calculations (second moment analysis) to prove the existence and implications of this hidden symmetry on system dynamics and controllability.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Jaynes-Cummings (JC) Hamiltonian is a paradigmatic model of light-matter interaction and, more generally, qubit-boson interactions, widely used across atomic, optical, and superconducting qubit platforms. In the multi-qubit setting, where n qubits are identically coupled to a single boson mode, this interaction is known as the Tavis-Cummings (TC) Hamiltonian. The structure of the TC model is usually understood in terms of two standard symmetries: permutation invariance of the qubits and a U(1) symmetry associated with conservation of the total excitation number. Here we identify an additional, independent "accidental" symmetry of the TC Hamiltonian and construct the corresponding conserved observable. We show that, for n>2 qubits, this symmetry imposes strong constraints on the realizable unitary transformations. These constraints persist in the presence of the global $J_z$ Hamiltonian, but are removed by adding $J_z^2$, even though $J_z^2$ preserves both permutation invariance and the U(1) symmetry. Finally, we explain the origin of this previously unnoticed symmetry using Schwinger's boson representation of angular momentum. These restrictions have important implications for controllability of the TC system and for its applications to quantum computing, which are investigated further in a companion paper.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces Apollo, a novel neuromorphic processor that achieves performance equivalent to superconducting quantum annealers at room temperature. It accomplishes this by using p-qubits whose stochastic fluctuations are driven by integrated quantum entropy units. This establishes a scalable, energy-efficient platform for solving hard optimization problems in classical CMOS technology.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, solid-state realization of quantum-inspired computation principles—specifically mapping complex Hamiltonian dynamics onto classical, room-temperature stochastic processes driven by fundamental quantum noise sources.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a scalable, room-temperature hardware platform capable of solving complex optimization problems by mimicking quantum annealing dynamics without cryogenic cooling.

**Main result.** The Apollo processor demonstrates quantum-equivalent performance on spin-glass benchmarks at ambient temperature, establishing a viable path for energy-based computation outside superconducting circuits.

**Method.** The work combines theoretical mapping (Suzuki–Trotter correspondence) with experimental realization using continuous-time stochastic dynamics in CMOS electronics.

**Model / system.** Apollo is a 10,000-node p-qubit neuromorphic processor fabricated in 16 nm CMOS. Its core element, the p-qubit, uses quantum-derived entropy to drive bistable stochastic transitions, allowing it to sample energy landscapes governed by Ising/QUBO Hamiltonians.

**Key observables.** Ground state energies relative to cryogenic annealers, residual energy scaling trajectories, and thermodynamic sampling correctness.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Room temperature operation ($\sim 0.5 	ext{ W}$), $16 	ext{ nm}$ CMOS fabrication, Hyperion $\Delta 256$ interconnect topology.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The dynamics of the p-qubit network correctly reproduce the equilibrium statistics and annealing behavior previously associated with quantum tunneling mechanisms.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the multi-layer tiled architecture (p-qubit cores, CMM core), the circuit diagram of a single p-qubit integrating VMM and IQEU inputs into an OTA nonlinearity, and the sigmoidal response curve.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from identifying limitations in existing quantum/classical annealers to proposing the Apollo architecture. It details the physical implementation (VMM using FG pFETs) and validates its ability to perform energy-based optimization via continuous-time stochastic relaxation, culminating in benchmark results.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce Apollo, a 10000 node p-qubit neuromorphic processor fabricated in 16 nm mixed signal CMOS and operating fully at room temperature with a typical analog core power envelope of about 0.5 W. Its fundamental element, the p-qubit, is a bistable stochastic unit whose continuous time state fluctuations are driven by integrated quantum entropy units that inject true quantum derived randomness. This enables ultrafast stochastic transitions at low energy while preserving a classical state representation. Apollo combines these p-qubits with a high degree Hyperion 256 interconnect topology, allowing efficient embedding of dense Ising and QUBO problems with substantially reduced minor embedding overhead compared with sparse annealing platforms. We show that, through the Suzuki Trotter correspondence, the equilibrium statistics and annealing dynamics of the p-qubit network reproduce key properties of transverse field quantum annealing without cryogenic cooling, long lived coherence, or microwave control. Beyond device level validation, Apollo is evaluated on a three dimensional spin glass benchmark previously used to study quantum advantage in superconducting annealers. Across 300 disorder realizations, Apollo reaches substantially lower ground state energies than reported cryogenic quantum annealing hardware, while remaining distinct from classical simulated annealing and simulated quantum annealing. A 350 nm release candidate device experimentally validates the core p-qubit dynamics, thermodynamic sampling correctness, and continuous time annealing behavior. These results establish Apollo as a room temperature, industrially scalable platform for quantum driven energy based optimization, probabilistic inference, generative modeling, and hybrid classical quantum workflows.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical study analyzes trapped dipolar quantum gases confined to a quasi-2D geometry, focusing on the interplay between strong dipole interactions and finite temperature effects. Using Bogoliubov theory, the authors derive an extended Gross-Pitaevskii equation that accounts for thermal fluctuations. The results show how temperature can induce spatial modulations in the density profile, providing insights into supersolid physics and potential thermometry applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses complex many-body physics in ultracold atomic systems (dipolar BECs) by extending mean-field theory to include thermal fluctuations in a quasi-2D geometry, which is crucial for understanding exotic phases like supersolids.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the physics of quasi-2D trapped dipolar Bose-Einstein Condensates (BECs) at both zero and finite temperatures in the strongly dipolar regime.

**Main result.** The research characterizes how increasing temperature can promote spatial modulations in the density profile, qualitatively agreeing with 3D Monte Carlo results. The theory also provides tools to assess thermal effects for potential thermometry applications.

**Method.** Bogoliubov theory and the Local Density Approximation (LDA) are employed to derive a finite-temperature Gross-Pitaevskii equation (TeGPE).

**Model / system.** The system consists of fully polarized dipoles trapped harmonically along the z-axis and in a box trap in the x-y plane. The interactions include contact and strong dipole-dipole interaction (DDI), making it relevant for dipolar supersolid stripes.

**Key observables.** Total column density, condensate fraction ($f_c$), spatial modulations/stripes, and thermal density profiles ($n_{th}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Tilting angle ($\alpha$), particle number ($N$), scattering length ($a$), and temperature ($T$). The strongly dipolar regime is key ($a \ll a_{dd}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis restricts itself to the limit of large condensate fractions ($f_c \geq 0.6$) and assumes the applicability of Bogoliubov theory, which breaks down in infinite 2D systems.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate density changes by varying particle number or scattering length at zero T; they also compare 2D densities at $T=0$ versus finite $T$, showing temperature-induced modulations.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from the general framework (Bogoliubov theory) to derive the TeGPE incorporating thermal fluctuations via LDA. It then analyzes structural changes by varying key parameters ($N, \alpha, T$) and concludes with applications for thermometry.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Motivated by the recent experimental observation of dipolar supersolid stripes in a quasi two-dimensional geometry [arXiv:2512.13280 (2025)], we study a trapped system of fully polarized dipoles in a strongly axially confined geometry, both at zero and finite temperatures, by means of Bogoliubov theory. The dipoles are strongly harmonically trapped along the z axis and subjected to a box trap in the x-y plane. We characterize the physics of the trapped system at zero and finite temperatures as a function of the tilting angle of the dipoles, the number of particles and the scattering length, restricting ourselves to the experimentally relevant regime of large condensate fractions. We also illustrate the influence of the aspect ratio of the box trap in the liquid character of the system and its structure. We observe a remarkable promotion of spatial modulations when temperature is increased while keeping the total particle number constant for specific configurations, in qualitative agreement with previous Monte Carlo results in a 3D geometry. Our results are useful to understand the zero temperature physics of the trapped dipolar system in the quasi-2D limit and in the strongly dipolar regime. In addition, they allow to assess the effect of temperature in its equilibrium properties in experimentally relevant conditions, which may be useful for thermometry applications.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12545"></a>
### [Spectral analysis of equilibration: information leakage in isolated quantum systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12545v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical paper introduces the Leakage Fidelity Function (LFF) as a precise measure of information leakage during equilibration in closed quantum systems. It establishes that the dynamics are controlled not by a single parameter, but by a hierarchy of spectral descriptors—including dynamical and power spectral dimensions—which quantify how fluctuations decay over time.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides powerful, quantitative diagnostics for open quantum systems theory by moving beyond simple time averages. The distinction between different effective dimensions offers new tools to characterize the rate and nature of thermalization in complex many-body models.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops a quantitative framework to measure equilibration in isolated quantum systems by analyzing how information leaks from an initial subspace over time.

**Main result.** Equilibration dynamics are governed by a hierarchy of spectral descriptors—ordinary, dynamical, and power spectral effective dimensions—which provide a state-dependent view of irreversibility.

**Method.** The core method involves defining the Leakage Fidelity Function (LFF) and deriving universal bounds on its temporal fluctuations using spectral analysis techniques like Fourier transforms and entropy measures.

**Model / system.** The theory is applied to isolated quantum systems evolving under unitary dynamics, analyzed via projection onto subspaces defined by an initial state's support. Specific examples include Ising-like chains and general many-body Hamiltonians.

**Key observables.** Leakage Fidelity Function (LFF), Leakage Coherence ($	ext{Cleak}$), Ordinary Effective Dimension ($d_{	ext{eff}}$), Dynamical Effective Dimension ($d_{	ext{dyn}}$), Power Spectral Entropy ($H_{	ext{pow}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spectral gap structure, effective dimension ($d_{	ext{eff}}$), time scale $T$, and the degree of spectral delocalization.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on deriving universal bounds applicable to fixed Hamiltonians; some parts assume Haar-typical state distributions or non-degenerate energy gaps for specific theorems.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate LFF behavior showing small fluctuations for equilibrating systems versus large oscillations for quasi-periodic ones. Later figures compare the growth patterns of $	ext{deff}$, $	ext{ddyn}$, and $	ext{dspec}$ using synthetic state distributions to separate their physical roles.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the LFF as an operational measure, deriving universal bounds on its variance (Theorem 1), connecting it to spectral properties ($d_{	ext{eff}}$), and finally introducing a hierarchy of descriptors ($	ext{ddyn}, 	ext{dspec}$) to show that fluctuation dynamics are richer than simple state-space measures.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a unified dynamical-spectral framework for equilibration in isolated quantum systems based on a subspace coarse-graining approach. Central to our formulation is the Leakage Fidelity Function (LFF), defined as the probability that a unitarily evolving state escapes the support of its initial subspace. This quantity provides a direct, operational measure of information flow and memory loss without invoking ensemble assumptions or perturbative arguments. We derive universal bounds on temporal fluctuations of the LFF, in terms of the spectral gap structure and the square of the effective dimension, evincing that large spectral delocalization suppresses fluctuations and guarantees equilibration on average. By introducing spectral power distributions and associated entropic measures, we establish a quantitative link between phase mixing, gap participation, and dynamical stability. We further investigate the equilibration timescale by connecting the LFF to quantum speed limits, thereby revealing the average time required for equilibration. Our results provide a state-dependent, geometrically transparent perspective on how spectral complexity and subspace information leakage jointly govern irreversibility in closed quantum many-body systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.12547"></a>
### [Vacuum photon emission and mean electromagnetic field in pair-creating external backgrounds](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12547v1)

**Authors:** I. A. Aleksandrov, E. V. Perelygin, D. V. Chubukov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12547v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper calculates how photons are emitted from the vacuum and determines the mean electromagnetic field when subjected to a strong, pair-creating external background. It employs advanced real-time (Keldysh) techniques necessary because the initial vacuum state is unstable. The results provide high-order perturbative corrections for these fundamental quantum electrodynamic observables.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of the Keldysh formalism to calculate expectation values in unstable quantum vacua is crucial for understanding non-equilibrium quantum optics and field theory effects beyond standard vacuum calculations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops a perturbative description of vacuum radiative processes and calculates the mean electromagnetic field in external, pair-creating electromagnetic backgrounds.

**Main result.** They derive the mean photon number density up to order alpha^2 and compute the mean electromagnetic field up to order e^3 using non-equilibrium quantum field theory techniques.

**Method.** The primary method is the Keldysh-Schwinger-Fradkin real-time formalism, supplemented by an alternative spectral decomposition approach for consistency checks.

**Model / system.** The system is Quantum Electrodynamics (QED) subjected to a general, spacetime-dependent external classical electromagnetic background field capable of inducing electron-positron pair production.

**Key observables.** Mean photon number density ($\langle N_\gamma angle$) and the mean electromagnetic field ($\langle A_\mu(x) angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fine-structure constant (alpha), external background field strength, and the critical scale for pair production.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The initial vacuum is unstable due to the external field, necessitating a real-time formalism beyond standard in-out approaches. Calculations are performed perturbatively in the coupling constant.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate Feynman diagrams contributing to the photon number density calculation at first and second order in alpha, and diagrams describing the mean electromagnetic field correction up to third order in e.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically calculates observables by expanding the generating functional on the Keldysh contour. It presents results for $\langle N_\gamma angle$ using diagrammatic methods and an alternative identity-based approach, followed by a detailed calculation of $\langle A_\mu(x) angle$ via Schwinger-Dyson equations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a perturbative description of vacuum radiative processes in quantum electrodynamics with a prescribed external electromagnetic background capable of producing electron-positron pairs. Since the initial vacuum is then unstable and the in- and out-vacua are inequivalent, radiative observables require a real-time formulation beyond the ordinary in-out approach of vacuum-stable QED. Using the Keldysh-Schwinger-Fradkin nonequilibrium technique, we derive the mean number density of emitted photons through the second nonvanishing order in the fine-structure constant. The leading term, of order $α$, reproduces the known vertex and tadpole mechanisms, while the complete order-$α^2$ correction contains interference, loop, and induced-current contributions. We also give an independent derivation based on the spectral decomposition of the identity operator in the in-Fock space, where the photon number density is represented as a sum of squared transition amplitudes and vacuum-disconnected terms are canceled by the optical theorem generalized to an unstable vacuum. In addition, we compute the mean electromagnetic field through order $e^3$, including the electromagnetic dressing of the induced vacuum current, and verify it using the corresponding Schwinger-Dyson equations. The final formulas are expressed in terms of exact solutions and propagators of the Dirac equation in the external background and apply to general spacetime-dependent field configurations.

</details>

### [Frustration effects on the magnetization plateau physics in a trimerized quantum spin-1/2 chain](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13265v1)

**Summary.** This work theoretically examines how competing interactions (frustration) destabilize and reorganize quantum spin chains modeled after $	ext{Na}_2	ext{Cu}_3	ext{Ge}_4	ext{O}_{12}$. By using advanced mean-field and exact diagonalization techniques, the authors map out distinct magnetic phases, explaining the stability of magnetization plateaus through emergent doublon correlations. The findings offer a microscopic framework for understanding fractionalized excitations in these complex quantum magnets.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed analysis of correlation functions and critical exponents ($z$, $c$) provides a deep theoretical understanding of how geometric frustration fundamentally alters the ground state structure, which is relevant for designing correlated electronic materials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates frustration-induced instabilities and the emergence of fractionalized excitations in trimerized quantum spin systems.

**Main result.** Frustration drives a reorganization of spin correlations, leading to a crossover from a collective ferrimagnetic state to one characterized by doublon-like correlations, providing a microscopic explanation for magnetization plateaus.

**Method.** The research employs a combination of the Cluster Mean-Field (CMF) approach and Lanczos exact diagonalization on finite clusters. Quantum information measures like fidelity susceptibility are used to characterize criticality.

**Model / system.** The system is a spin-1/2 Heisenberg model defined on a trimerized quantum spin chain, motivated by the compound Na2Cu3Ge4O12. The Hamiltonian includes competing interactions J1, J2, and J3, alongside an external magnetic field.

**Key observables.** Magnetization plateaus (especially 1/3), two-point spin-spin correlation functions, fidelity susceptibility ($\chi_F$), and von Neumann entanglement entropy ($S_{vN}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The frustration ratio $J_3/J_1$ is the key tuning parameter, analyzed across weakly frustrated regimes up to strong frustration.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary theoretical tool used is the Cluster Mean-Field approximation, which treats intercluster interactions via mean-field decoupling.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the evolution of correlation functions ($C_{ij}(r)$) and the magnetic phase diagram across different frustration regimes. $\chi_F$ peaks near a critical point $J_c 3/J_1 \approx 1.09$.

**Paper structure.** The paper analyzes the system in distinct frustration regimes, using CMF to map the phase diagram and ED/finite-size scaling on finite clusters to characterize quantum criticality via energy gaps and entanglement entropy.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate frustration-induced instabilities in a trimerized quantum spin chain motivated by recent experimental findings for the compound Na$_2$Cu$_3$Ge$_4$O$_{12}$. Employing a cluster mean-field approach combined with Lanczos exact diagonalization, we analyze the ground-state and quantum-information properties of a Heisenberg model with competing interactions in a magnetic field. In the weakly frustrated regime, the system exhibits a robust $1/3$ magnetization plateau associated with a collective ferrimagnetic-like trimer state. Increasing the next-nearest-neighbor intratrimer coupling drives a pronounced reorganization of spin correlations, leading to a crossover toward a doublon-like correlation regime and providing a static ground-state picture consistent with the composite excitations observed dynamically in trimerized chains. The resulting low-energy behavior can be interpreted in terms of weakly interacting emergent spins, offering a microscopic explanation for the extended stability of the magnetization plateau. Furthermore, through finite-size scaling analyses of the energy gap, von Neumann entanglement entropy, and fidelity susceptibility, we characterize the zero-field criticality of the model. Ultimately, our results suggest that frustration gives rise to qualitatively distinct quantum states and provide a microscopic framework for understanding the emergence of fractionalized excitations in trimerized quantum spin systems.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper analyzes a quantum Otto engine powered by an anisotropic two-qubit Heisenberg XYZ model. It systematically maps out the relationship between coupling strengths, magnetic fields, and energy extraction efficiency. The key finding is that entanglement asymmetry acts as a measurable witness of underlying energy level restructuring responsible for enhancing work output.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides detailed insights into how microscopic quantum correlations (entanglement) can be linked to macroscopic thermodynamic performance metrics like efficiency in engineered quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how an anisotropic two-qubit Heisenberg XYZ model can power a quantum Otto heat engine under independent local magnetic fields.

**Main result.** Engine efficiency is maximized by tuning the anisotropy parameter, and entanglement asymmetry between hot and cold strokes correlates strongly with performance enhancement. The work done is functionally partitioned among the qubits and their interaction term.

**Method.** The authors systematically analyze the quantum Otto cycle using exact diagonalization of the Hamiltonian and calculating thermodynamic quantities via partition functions and local work decomposition.

**Model / system.** The system is an anisotropic two-qubit Heisenberg XYZ model, whose dynamics are governed by a Hamiltonian incorporating coupling terms ($J, \Delta, J_z$) and independent local magnetic fields ($B_1, B_2$).

**Key observables.** Net work output (W_net), efficiency ($\eta$), quantum entanglement (concurrence), and the local work contributions from each qubit and their interaction.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Anisotropy parameter ($\Delta$), transverse coupling ($J$), longitudinal coupling ($J_z$), and the asymmetry of the local magnetic fields ($B_1, B_2$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on treating the cycle using an ideal adiabatic limit for work strokes and assuming specific field control protocols.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the four steps of the Otto cycle; one shows efficiency ($\eta$) vs. $J$ for various $\Delta$, mapping out optimal operating regimes and level crossing regions.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the Hamiltonian, calculates thermodynamic quantities across the four strokes (adiabatic/isomagnetic), decomposes the work into local contributions ($w_1, w_2, w_{	ext{int}}$), and finally correlates efficiency enhancement with changes in quantum entanglement.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study a quantum Otto heat engine whose working substance is an anisotropic two-qubit Heisenberg XYZ model. Independent local magnetic fields are used to control each spin individually. The influence of the longitudinal coupling, anisotropy, transverse coupling, and local fields on the net work output and efficiency is systematically examined. Reducing the longitudinal coupling is found to markedly improve both the maximum work and the peak efficiency. The engine performance reaches an optimum at a particular value of the anisotropy parameter. A local work analysis clarifies how work is produced during the cycle. Because of the asymmetric local fields and the intrinsic spin-spin interaction, the two qubits play markedly different thermodynamic roles; the interaction term itself contributes crucially to the total work. We further analyze the variation of quantum entanglement, quantified by concurrence, along the cycle. The results indicate that a pronounced change in entanglement between the hot and cold isomagnetic strokes is closely correlated with the efficiency enhancement. This work offers new insight into the operating principles and control of quantum Otto heat engines.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work explores the complex ground state physics of moiré materials using a Hubbard model incorporating valley degrees of freedom. By combining DQMC simulations and advanced spin-wave theory, the authors map out a phase diagram showing how tuning interaction symmetries drives crossovers between magnetically ordered insulators and correlated metallic states. The key finding is that persistent quantum fluctuations stabilize exotic intermediate regimes.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed study of how quantum fluctuations (valley fluctuations) stabilize a metallic or fluctuating state over a simple ordered insulator is highly relevant for understanding emergent phenomena in strongly correlated systems, which shares conceptual links with many open quantum system models.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates hidden antiferromagnetism, persistent valley fluctuations, and $U(6)$ crossovers in moiré materials. The goal is to understand the competition between local moment formation and itinerant behavior as interaction symmetries are tuned.

**Main result.** The system exhibits an extended intermediate-coupling regime where low-energy charged quasiparticles are dressed by neutral valley fluctuations. Quantum fluctuations favor a stripe order over other classical ground states.

**Method.** The research employs Determinantal Quantum Monte Carlo (DQMC) simulations, supplemented by advanced theoretical tools like perturbation theory, Random Phase Approximation (RPA), and Linear Spin-Wave Theory (LSWT).

**Model / system.** The model is based on a Hubbard-type Hamiltonian describing electrons in the low-energy states of twisted two-dimensional atomic monolayers forming a triangular-lattice M-point moiré superlattice. The system is studied at half-filling ($
u=3$).

**Key observables.** Momentum-resolved spin-spin correlations, imaginary-time Green's functions, valley fluctuation spectral weights, and local double occupancy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction anisotropy parameter $\alpha$ (especially near $\alpha=1$), coupling strengths ($U/W$, $J'/J$), and temperature ($T 	o 0$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The DQMC simulations rely on the existence of a sign-free formulation due to particle-hole symmetry at half-filling. Theoretical analyses often involve approximations like LSWT or mean-field decoupling.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate spin correlation temperature dependence, spectral function evolution showing quasiparticle behavior, and quantum zero-point energy calculations demonstrating the preference for stripe order over classical ground states.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the lattice model and symmetries to implementing DQMC simulations. It then analyzes different coupling regimes (weak, intermediate, strong) using various theoretical tools like RPA, perturbation theory, and spin-wave analysis to map out the phase diagram.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A new moiré material platform was recently proposed based on twisting two-dimensional atomic monolayers whose low-energy states lie at the three M-points of the Brillouin Zone. Continuum and ab initio modeling suggest that electrons in the conduction bands of these materials realize three-valley Hubbard models with valley-selective, quasi-one-dimensional hopping. Remarkably, the onsite Hubbard repulsion is almost $U(6)$-symmetric without fine-tuning. Here, we show that this class of systems naturally admits sign-free determinantal Quantum Monte Carlo simulations at a filling of three electrons per moiré unit cell. We use these to explore the phase diagram for interactions of various strengths and $U(6)$-breaking anisotropies. We show that for near-isotropic interactions as relevant to, e.g., AA-stacked twisted SnSe$_2$, the system exhibits an extended intermediate-coupling regime in which local-moment formation and itinerancy compete, and the crossover to a putative low-temperature ordered state can be understood in terms of fluctuating $U(6)$ local moments. We argue that many of these features persist beyond the idealized sign-problem-free limit.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12685"></a>
### [Multiple Topological Haldane Phases for Symmetry-Protected Quantum Information Processing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12685v1)

**Authors:** João Pedro Gama D'Elia, Irene D'Amico, Thereza Paiva

**Summary.** The paper proposes using a spin ladder exhibiting Haldane phases as a robust platform for quantum computing. By locally tuning the system's interactions, the authors create independent topological subsystems capable of hosting qubits. This architecture allows for the implementation of both single-qubit rotations and entangling two-qubit gates necessary for universal computation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete realization pathway for topological quantum computation, moving beyond abstract theory by mapping required gate operations onto tunable parameters (like local fields and couplings) within a solid-state spin model.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to demonstrate a scalable, physical architecture for universal quantum computation using symmetry-protected topological (SPT) phases.

**Main result.** The authors show that by engineering local couplings in a spin ladder system, they can partition it into independent Haldane subsystems, allowing for controlled single-qubit rotations and entangling two-qubit gates necessary for universal computation.

**Method.** The work relies on theoretical modeling using effective Hamiltonians derived from the low-energy projection of the full many-body Hamiltonian. Gate performance is quantified by calculating gate fidelity and leakage probability through time evolution simulations (TEBD/TDVP).

**Model / system.** The system is a spin-1/2 ladder composed of multiple coupled Haldane chains, where qubits are encoded in the two topological states of the $S^z=0$ sector. The Hamiltonian involves Heisenberg interactions and tunable rung couplings ($J_{\perp}$).

**Key observables.** Gate fidelity ($F_{gate}$), leakage probability ($\mathcal{P}_{leak}$), entanglement entropy, and the energy splitting ($\Delta_0$) between topological ground states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Local magnetic field strength ($B$), interaction coupling strengths ($g_2/J$), and the ratio of rung couplings to chain interactions ($J_{\perp}/J$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The protocol deliberately utilizes finite-size effects (residual coupling) as a controllable resource rather than an error source, allowing for coherent control axes.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the ladder structure and the process of partitioning into independent subsystems. Other figures show Bloch vector trajectories confirming single-qubit gate operations and entanglement dynamics confirming Bell state formation.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the physical model (ladder system) and demonstrating how local tuning induces quantum phase transitions to create isolated Haldane subsystems. It then details the implementation of single-qubit gates using finite-size gaps, followed by introducing inter-subsystem interactions to achieve universal two-qubit entangling gates.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Symmetry-protected topological phases have attracted significant interest at the fundamental level and as a potential platform for quantum information processing, owing to their protected edge states and resilience to perturbations. Applying these features for practical and efficient quantum computation is highly desirable, but remains an open challenge. Here, we demonstrate the partitioning into multiple independent Haldane phase subsystems of a single spin-1/2 ladder system and propose this as a scalable architecture for gate-based quantum computation, which takes advantage of the symmetry-protected topological order. We encode qubits in the two topological states of the $S^{z}=0$ sector of each subsystem. Finite-size effects, typically viewed as detrimental, instead provide a controllable energy splitting that enables single-qubit rotations using only local magnetic fields. An Ising-type interaction between neighboring subsystem edges generates entangling gates, enabling universal quantum computation driven by two control parameters that are easily accessible experimentally. Our results demonstrate how symmetry-protected topological phases can be directly harnessed for circuit-model quantum computation in realistic systems.

</details>

### [Global Control with the Tavis-Cummings Interaction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12906v1)

**Summary.** The paper rigorously investigates the controllability of qubits coupled via a Tavis-Cummings interaction to a bosonic mode. It demonstrates that while initial controls are limited by an 'accidental' symmetry, introducing a secondary global field ($J_z^2$) breaks this constraint, enabling near-universal control over the system's unitary operations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides rigorous mathematical tools for determining the fundamental limits of quantum control in complex, interacting many-body systems, which is crucial for designing fault-tolerant quantum computation protocols.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is characterizing the group of unitaries implementable on a joint qubit-bosonic system using global control fields derived from the Tavis-Cummings interaction.

**Main result.** The realizable unitary group is restricted by an 'accidental' symmetry when only the TC interaction and $J_z$ are used, but adding $J_z^2$ breaks this symmetry, achieving semi-universality for implementing arbitrary unitaries respecting key symmetries.

**Method.** The analysis relies heavily on Lie algebra theory to characterize the generated groups of unitaries by analyzing commutator subalgebras $[\mathfrak{g}, \mathfrak{g}]$ and comparing them to the full symmetry group $	ext{VU}(1)	imes	ext{Sn}$.

**Model / system.** The system consists of $n$ qubits identically coupled via the Tavis-Cummings (TC) interaction to a single bosonic mode (harmonic oscillator). Control is applied globally using $J_z$ and potentially $J_z^2$.

**Key observables.** The group of realizable unitaries ($V_z$), the structure of the Lie algebra ($\mathfrak{v}_z, \mathfrak{w}_z$), and the dimension/inclusion relationships between commutator subalgebras.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of qubits ($n$), coupling strengths ($g_{TC}$), and the presence/absence of additional symmetry-breaking terms like $J_z^2$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes that the bosonic mode acts as an ancillary system initialized in a fixed energy eigenstate, returning to it at the end.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate schematic relationships between Lie algebras generated by combinations of Hamiltonians and show how commutator subalgebras reduce or expand based on added terms.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from characterizing the initial restricted group $V_z$ using $\{	ext{iHTC}, 	ext{iJz}\}$, to showing that adding $	ext{iJ}_2^z$ breaks an accidental symmetry, leading to semi-universality. Later sections delve into detailed Lie algebra decompositions and structural proofs.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the controllability of a system of qubits under global control, where control pulses act identically on all qubits. Specifically, we consider a collection of qubits identically coupled to a single bosonic mode, or harmonic oscillator, via the Jaynes-Cummings interaction. This collective coupling, known as the Tavis-Cummings (TC) interaction, has been realized in several quantum computing platforms, including superconducting and atomic qubit systems. Although the qubits do not interact directly with one another, they can become entangled through their common coupling to the bosonic mode. We characterize the group of unitaries that can be implemented on the joint Hilbert space of the qubits and bosonic mode using the TC interaction together with a global $z$ field $J_z$, corresponding to identical z rotations on all qubits. We show that for n>2 qubits the set of realizable unitaries is restricted by an "accidental" symmetry of the TC Hamiltonian, distinct from its "standard" U(1) and permutational symmetries. On the other hand, we find that the Hamiltonian $J_z^2$ breaks this accidental symmetry and, together with the TC interaction and $J_z$, achieves semi-universality: it allows the implementation of arbitrary unitaries that respect permutational and U(1) symmetry, up to certain constraints on the center of the group. In a companion paper, we further analyze this remarkable accidental symmetry and show that it can be understood through Schwinger's bosonic model of angular momentum.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper analyzes the magnetic excitations in kagome antiferromagnets using the XXZ model and numerical simulations. By tracking spectral features across varying anisotropies, the authors establish that low-energy peaks scale universally with a characteristic energy scale $T^*$. This provides a sharp spectroscopic criterion to distinguish between different types of quantum magnetic excitations observed experimentally.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, quantitative method (the scaling law $\omega \propto T^*$) to interpret complex experimental signatures in frustrated magnets, bridging advanced quantum simulation techniques with measurable physical observables like inelastic neutron scattering spectra.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central problem is distinguishing between fractionalized excitations (spinons) and magnon excitations when interpreting broad spectral features observed in neutron scattering experiments on quantum magnetic materials.

**Main result.** The low-energy spin spectral function on the kagome lattice exhibits two robust, momentum-independent peaks ($\omega_1 \approx 3.4 T^*$ and $\omega_2 \approx 6.3 T^*$), suggesting these features arise from single-magnon scattering rather than a continuum of fractionalized excitations.

**Method.** The authors use adiabatic continuity by tuning the anisotropy parameter ($\alpha$) in the XXZ Heisenberg model on geometrically frustrated lattices, simulating the dynamical spin structure factor using advanced numerical techniques like LTLM.

**Model / system.** The system is the $S=1/2$ antiferromagnet on the kagome lattice, modeled by the anisotropic XXZ Heisenberg Hamiltonian. The analysis focuses on how spectral features evolve as a function of anisotropy and temperature.

**Key observables.** Dynamical spin structure factor ($	ilde{S}(\omega, T)$), low-energy excitation frequencies ($\omega_1, \omega_2$), and the 'hidden energy scale' ($T^*$) derived from heat capacity measurements.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Anisotropy parameter $\alpha$, temperature $T$, and the characteristic energy scale $T^*$ (related to spin-glass freezing).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The low-energy excitations are assumed to be well-described by single-magnon transitions between specific magnetization bands ($|M_z|=0 	o 1$).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the temperature and anisotropy dependence of $	ilde{S}(\omega, T)$, showing the robust scaling of peak energies ($\omega_1, \omega_2$) relative to $T^*$, alongside heat capacity data confirming $T^*$.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops a theoretical framework using adiabatic continuity on the XXZ model. It calculates $	ilde{S}(\omega, T)$ and demonstrates that low-energy spectral features scale universally with the characteristic temperature $T^*$, providing a spectroscopic criterion for interpreting neutron scattering data.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Neutron scattering is central to identifying quantum states of magnetic materials. In the search for quantum spin liquids, broad spectral features of inelastic spectra have been cited as evidence for spinon excitations, but can also arise from magnon excitations excitations in the presence of quenched disorder and strong magnon interactions. We develop a new approach to this problem, based on the adiabatic continuity in the $XXZ$ Heisenberg model on geometrically frustrating (GF) lattices as a function of the model's anisotropy. Using this approach, we identify universal features and energies of finite-temperature spin correlators. Focusing on the kagome lattice, we show that the low-energy spin spectral function contains robust, momentum-independent peaks with frequencies: $ω_1 \approx 3.4 T^*$ and $ω_2 \approx 6.3 T^*$, where the ``hidden energy scale'' $T^*$ is the characteristic scale of a low-temperature peak in the heat capacity, at which many GF magnets also display spin-glass freezing. We show that the spectral features at low energies $ω\lesssim T^*$ arise from single-magnon scattering and identify the magnetizations of the respective excitations. We explore the evolution of the spectral features with temperature and discuss extensions to other GF lattices. Our results provide a sharp spectroscopic criterion for interpreting neutron scattering in kagome and other GF quantum magnets.

</details>
