### [Instabilities in a Non-KAM System via Information Scrambling: A Note](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12761v1)

**Authors:** Naga Dileep Varikuti  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12761v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12761_figures/2606.12761_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. The figure depicts representative classical trajectories of the KHO near the resonance condition R = 4, obtained from three dif- ferent initial conditions and evolved for 104 time steps. Throughout, the parameters are fixed at K = 1 and τ = 1. At exact resonance, the phase space is organized into three distinct structures: a stochastic web spanning a large portion of phase space, period-four stability is- lands, and a central period-one regular island surrounding the origin. The chosen initial conditions are selected from each of these regions. As R is varied from 3.9 toward 4, following the sequence shown in panels (a)–(d), the trajectories undergo progressive deformation and...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12761_figures/2606.12761_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. The OTOCs are sensitive to the changes as the system tran- sitions from KAM to the non-KAM regime, which is demonstrated for R = 4 and R = 6. (a). The plots of OTOC for the parame- ter values R = 3.9, 4, and 4.1 are drawn. The values R = 3.9 and R = 4.1 correspond to the rational numbers 39/10 and 41/10, re- spectively. For rational R = p/q (with p and q coprime), the relevant arithmetic structure is governed by the numerator p. Consequently, the corresponding lower bounds on the linear-growth segment are determined by ϕ(39) and ϕ(41). The larger value of ϕ(41), a con- sequence of 41 being prime, accounts for the noticeably extended linear-growth regime observed for R = 4.1. (b)....</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper analyzes information scrambling in the quantized Kicked Harmonic Oscillator, a non-KAM system. Using OTOCs, the authors show that resonances fundamentally alter operator growth, leading to predictable quadratic scaling dictated by number theory (the Euler totient function). This highlights how arithmetic properties can control quantum dynamics even when conventional chaos indicators are weak.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work connects concepts from classical dynamical systems (KAM theory, resonance) with modern quantum diagnostics (OTOCs), providing a deep mathematical structure ($\phi(R)$) to understand how quantum chaos manifests in periodically driven systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates operator growth and information scrambling dynamics in quantized systems that violate the Kolmogorov-Arnold-Moser (KAM) theorem, focusing on the role of resonances.

**Main result.** Resonant structures significantly influence quantum information spreading, causing OTOCs to exhibit a quadratic-in-time growth at resonance compared to linear growth away from it. This behavior is governed by number-theoretic functions like the Euler totient function.

**Method.** The analysis employs Out-of-Time-Ordered Correlators (OTOCs) and utilizes perturbative expansions in the weak coupling limit, deriving closed-form expressions for these correlators.

**Model / system.** The primary model is the Kicked Harmonic Oscillator (KHO), described by a Hamiltonian involving periodic delta-kicks. The system is analyzed using bosonic ladder operators ($\hat{a}, \hat{a}^\dagger$).

**Key observables.** Out-of-Time-Ordered Correlators (OTOCs) and the averaged squared commutator function, $C\hat{a}\hat{a}^\dagger(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The resonance ratio $R$ (where $\omega R = 2\pi/	au$), the perturbation strength $K$, and the time scale relative to the Euler totient function $\phi(R)$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to the weak perturbative regime ($K \ll 1$) and relies on simplifying assumptions regarding the time evolution related to roots of unity.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate classical trajectories near resonance, showing stochastic webs, and plot OTOCs for specific rational ratios (e.g., R=4, R=6), demonstrating growth governed by $\phi(R)$.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the physical context of non-KAM systems using KHO and OTOCs. It then develops analytical techniques to calculate the time evolution of the commutator function in the weak coupling limit. Finally, it derives explicit forms showing the number-theoretic control exerted by resonances on information scrambling.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study operator growth in quantized non-KAM systems using out-of-time-ordered correlators (OTOCs), focusing on the kicked harmonic oscillator as a representative example. Since the classical harmonic oscillator is degenerate, the dynamics fall outside the usual Kolmogorov-Arnold-Moser (KAM) framework, and resonances play a central role in shaping the phase space. We examine the system near resonances, where the ratio between the oscillator and driving frequencies takes integer values. Even though the classical Lyapunov exponent remains small at these points, and hence no conventional chaos, the phase space still undergoes strong structural changes. The OTOCs are particularly sensitive to these resonances, with a quadratic-in-time growth at resonance compared to linear growth away from it. Within a perturbative treatment, we derive closed-form expressions for the OTOCs and uncover a number-theoretic structure emerging in the behavior of OTOCs, governed by the Euler totient function of the frequency ratio. Overall, the results we present in this short note imply that resonant structures can play an important role in controlling information spreading.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.13263"></a>
### [Phase diagram of the Kitaev-Heisenberg-$Γ$ model: Classical and quantum magnetism, frustration, and subdominant interactions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13263v1)

**Authors:** Kiyu Fukui, Yukitoshi Motome  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13263v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `analog quantum simulation` **4/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13263_figures/2606.13263_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Ground-state phase diagrams of the Kitaev-Heisenberg-Γ model in Eq. (1), parametrized by polar angle θ and azimuthal angle ϕ as defined in Eq. (2), for (a) classical spins for Γ ≥0, (b) quantum spins for Γ ≥0, and (c) quantum spins for Γ ≤0. The region enclosed by the red dashed line indicates where the Luttinger-Tisza method loses its validity. The hatched regions indicate where incommensurate magnetic order is suggested. **Summary.** This paper comprehensively maps the ground-state phase diagram of the Kitaev-Heisenberg-$\Gamma$ model, crucial for understanding real candidate materials. By comparing classical and quantum treatments, the authors reveal a complex interplay between magnetic interactions, showing that quantum effects significantly reduce the number of competing ordered states found classically. The results provide a vital guide for both interpreting experimental data and designing new spin liquid materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit comparison between classical and quantum predictions regarding magnetic ordering is highly relevant as it elucidates how quantum fluctuations stabilize or suppress competing ordered phases in strongly correlated magnets.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to clarify the complex ground-state phase diagram of the Kitaev-Heisenberg-$\Gamma$ model, specifically addressing how additional magnetic interactions perturb the ideal Kitaev spin liquid state.

**Main result.** Classically, the system exhibits a 'zoo' of noncollinear multiple-$Q$ magnetic orders, while quantum fluctuations suppress many of these competing classical phases, suggesting stabilized spiral spin liquid states in highly frustrated regions.

**Method.** The research employs advanced numerical techniques, including gradient descent optimization for classical spins and the Pseudo-fermion Functional Renormalization Group (PFFRG) method for quantum spins.

**Model / system.** The model is the Kitaev-Heisenberg-$\Gamma$ Hamiltonian applied to a 2D honeycomb lattice. It incorporates three types of interactions ($J, K, \Gamma$) and is analyzed across both classical spin configurations and $S=1/2$ quantum spins.

**Key observables.** Spin structure factor S(q), total scalar spin chirality $\chi_{	ext{tot}}$, static spin susceptibility $\chi_{\Lambda}(\mathbf{q})$, and the energy per site $\epsilon$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling constants $(J, K, \Gamma)$ are varied via parameters $(	heta, \phi)$, allowing mapping of the phase diagram across different interaction regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** For quantum calculations, the zero-temperature limit is used; for classical analysis, optimization relies on minimizing energy using automatic differentiation techniques.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate ground-state phase diagrams showing various magnetic phases (e.g., Néel, FM, QSL) as a function of coupling parameters, alongside plots detailing spin susceptibilities and eigenvalue distributions across the parameter space.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the model by first establishing the Hamiltonian and then performing separate, detailed analyses for classical ground states using variational minimization, followed by quantum calculations utilizing PFFRG to map out the full phase diagram.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Kitaev spin liquid provides a rare example of exactly solvable quantum spin liquid states. Intensive research over the past two decades has identified a variety of its candidate materials. In real materials, however, the Kitaev interaction is inevitably accompanied by additional magnetic interactions such as the Heisenberg and $Γ$ interactions. These interactions often induce magnetic ordering at low temperatures, making it essential to clarify their effects in the search for and design of Kitaev spin liquid candidate materials. In this study, we revisit the ground-state phase diagram of the Kitaev-Heisenberg-$Γ$ model from both classical and quantum perspectives, using state-of-the-art numerical techniques. In the classical case, we reveal a $zoo$ $of$ $noncollinear$ $orders$, where a variety of noncollinear multiple-$Q$ magnetic orders with and without incommensurate modulations emerge. In the quantum case, we unravel that quantum fluctuations suppress many of the competing orders found in the classical case, resulting in a reduced number of dominant incommensurate orders. We further identify $highly$ $frustrated$ regions, where spiral spin liquid states as well as new magnetically ordered states are potentially stabilized by other additional magnetic interactions. Our results provide a comprehensive perspective on the Kitaev-Heisenberg-$Γ$ model for both classical and quantum spins and offer a valuable guide not only for interpreting experimental results on candidate materials, but also for searching and designing new materials to realize the Kitaev spin liquid.

</details>

**Summary.** The research investigates strongly correlated physics in M-point moiré materials using an advanced Stochastic Series Expansion Quantum Monte Carlo technique. By developing a sign-problem-free algorithm capable of handling mixed dimensionality, the authors map out phase diagrams revealing correlated insulating and Wigner-Mott phases. This work advances computational methods for studying complex electronic correlations in novel 2D material platforms.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful numerical tool (sign-problem-free QMC) to study complex, quasi-low-dimensional correlated electron systems that are highly relevant for understanding emergent phenomena in 2D materials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To study the strongly interacting physics of M-point moiré materials, particularly exploring regimes beyond idealized limits like intermediate coupling or away from integer fillings.

**Main result.** The authors successfully applied a sign-problem-free Quantum Monte Carlo method to map out the phase diagram, finding evidence for correlated insulators and Wigner-Mott states in twisted SnSe2.

**Method.** Stochastic Series Expansion (SSE) Quantum Monte Carlo (QMC), which was adapted with custom global updates and parallel tempering to handle the mixed-dimensional nature of the system.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is AA-stacked $	ext{SnSe}_2$, modeled by a three-orbital Hubbard Hamiltonian exhibiting quasi-one-dimensional hopping within valleys, coupled into a fully two-dimensional network. The model captures strong electronic correlations in this moiré superlattice.

**Key observables.** Inverse compressibility ($\partial\mu/\partial
u$), charge and spin stiffnesses ($ho_C, ho_S$), susceptibility components, and the spin-spin correlation function $S(i)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angle ($	heta$), interaction strength ($U, V$), filling factor ($
u$), and temperature/inverse temperature ($eta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often neglects Hund's and anti-Hund's couplings due to their small magnitude or antagonistic nature; the method requires specialized inter-chain updates for full equilibration.

**Figures summary.** Figures display quantitative results across various parameters, showing how observables like compressibility and stiffnesses change with filling and temperature in the mixed-dimensional regime. Benchmarking figures compare SSE against ED/DQMC to validate the algorithm's accuracy.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops the model Hamiltonian, details the necessary adaptations of the SSE QMC method (including inter-chain updates), benchmarks this method against exact and determinantal methods, and finally applies it to map out the phase diagram by calculating various thermodynamic observables.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A new moiré-material platform has recently been proposed based on twisting two-dimensional triangular-lattice monolayers whose low-energy states lie at the three M points of the Brillouin zone. Continuum models derived from extensive ab initio simulations suggest that electrons in the conduction bands of one such M-point moiré material, twisted AA-stacked SnSe$_2$, realize a three-orbital Hubbard model with orbitally-selective, quasi-one-dimensional (quasi-1D) hopping, protected by a projective mirror symmetry. Here, we show that the resulting "mixed-dimensional" limit -- in which the hopping is exactly quasi-1D in each valley, while the valleys are coupled by interactions into a fully two-dimensional network -- can be sampled with Stochastic Series Expansion (SSE) quantum Monte Carlo (QMC) without a sign problem at any filling. We develop an efficient new SSE QMC algorithm that combines custom global updates with parallel tempering to overcome the equilibration challenges posed by the mixed-dimensional setting. We then use this algorithm to explore the phase diagram of M-point twisted AA-stacked SnSe$_2$. Over extended and realistic ranges of twist angles and interaction strengths, we find that at integer fillings the system supports correlated insulators whose nature and strength depend strongly on angle. At certain commensurate fractional fillings, we further find evidence for Wigner-Mott insulators. We analytically account for the main features observed numerically using a strong-coupling description. Finally, we discuss perturbations away from the mixed-dimensional limit and the possibility of applying our method to other realizations of mixed-dimensional Hubbard models.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces QuBE/Qubex, a comprehensive hardware-software system designed to control large superconducting qubit arrays. It details how this integrated stack automates complex tasks like broadband calibration and multilevel readout on 64 qubits. This work significantly advances the engineering feasibility of scaling quantum computation by providing an open, functional platform.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides an industrial-grade blueprint for scaling quantum processors by solving the critical interface problem between complex analog microwave electronics and high-level digital control logic, which is highly relevant to open quantum systems research.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is achieving high-fidelity, scalable quantum operations on large superconducting qubit arrays by integrating complex control hardware with sophisticated software orchestration.

**Main result.** The integrated QuBE/Qubex system successfully demonstrated multi-unit far-detuned cross-resonance calibration and multilevel readout fidelity exceeding 95%, validating a robust platform for scaling superconducting qubits.

**Method.** The authors employed an automated, layered software stack (Qubex) to manage hardware resources (QuBE), enabling complex characterization routines like Hamiltonian tomography and randomized benchmarking on a 64-qubit chip.

**Model / system.** The platform is a 64-qubit fixed-frequency transmon superconducting qubit chip. The control system uses broadband microwave electronics capable of generating signals across multiple GHz spans, synchronized via shared clock references.

**Key observables.** Measured two-qubit gate fidelity (98.34%), multilevel readout state assignment fidelity ($ar{F}$), and phase stability standard deviations ($\sigma < 0.456^\circ$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Instantaneous frequency span up to 1.6 GHz; operating temperature range from room temperature down to mK levels.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system assumes the ability to map abstract quantum operations onto specific, resolved microwave hardware settings (LO, CNCO, FNCO, AWG) via a systematic decomposition formula.

**Figures summary.** Figures detail the hardware architecture (QuBE rack), the software control stack mapping, frequency allocation maps of the 64-qubit chip, and measurement results like phase stability traces and gate fidelity benchmarks.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from introducing the need for integrated control to detailing the layered Qubex software model, characterizing hardware capabilities (broadband output, signal integrity), demonstrating advanced measurements (CR calibration, multilevel readout), and finally benchmarking performance on a large-scale chip.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Achieving high-fidelity operation in large-scale superconducting qubit systems requires not only control hardware with broad frequency coverage, low crosstalk, and tight synchronization but also software that coordinates system configuration, experiment execution, and data analysis. Here we present an integrated qubit-control system that combines broadband microwave hardware with a pulse-level software stack for scalable superconducting qubit experiments. The hardware provides broadband microwave coverage, including an instantaneous span of up to 1.6 GHz from a control output, while the software reduces setup and calibration overhead through automated configuration and built-in experiment workflows. We validate the system on a 64-qubit fixed-frequency transmon chip through full-chip frequency identification and representative demonstrations, including multi-unit far-detuned cross-resonance calibration and benchmarking that yields a measured two-qubit gate fidelity of 98.34%, and multilevel readout beyond the computational subspace. By disclosing the hardware architecture and releasing the software stack as open source, this work provides an inspectable hardware-software foundation for scalable superconducting qubit control experiments.

</details>

### [Toward Entanglement Bootstrap for Conformal Field Theory in Any Dimension](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12540v1)

**Summary.** This work proposes an Entanglement Bootstrap method to study Conformal Field Theories (CFTs) by reconstructing effective Hamiltonians from entanglement structure. By testing this on known critical states, the authors demonstrate that their reconstructed Hamiltonians yield spectra consistent with CFT predictions and provide a systematic way to mitigate finite-size errors in numerical simulations.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of entanglement measures ($K_A$) as a direct diagnostic tool for identifying universal properties like conformal invariance is highly relevant for understanding quantum criticality in strongly correlated systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The goal is to systematically understand Renormalization Group (RG) fixed points, particularly for Conformal Field Theories (CFTs), using the Entanglement Bootstrap approach in arbitrary dimensions.

**Main result.** The proposed method provides an automated way to improve finite-size effects by reconstructing Hamiltonians whose spectra exhibit characteristic CFT properties like integer spacing of descendants.

**Method.** The authors reconstruct a Hamiltonian, $H_{rec}$, from entanglement Hamiltonians ($K_A$) derived from reduced density matrices. They establish and test the Vector Fixed Point Equation (VFPE): $K_F |\psiangle = F |\psiangle$.

**Model / system.** The analysis focuses on CFT groundstates in arbitrary dimensions, using regularized approximate states on geometries like the icosahedron or fuzzy sphere as test cases.

**Key observables.** Von Neumann entropy ($S_A$), the reconstructed Dilation operator ($\langle D_{rec} angle$), and the error of the VFPE ($	ext{err}(\psi)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension $D=d+1$, and specific coefficients $\lambda_i$ chosen to cancel UV contributions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes that entanglement Hamiltonians can be decomposed into a CFT part and a manageable UV correction, allowing for systematic cancellation of divergences.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the geometry determining coupling constants, show minimization of the VFPE error ($	ext{err}(\psi)$) detecting phase transitions, and display reconstructed spectra matching known CFT predictions.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theoretical framework by defining the entanglement bootstrap using linear combinations of entanglement Hamiltonians to derive the VFPE. It then applies this method numerically to specific geometries (icosahedron, fuzzy sphere) to test its ability to recover known critical point physics and improve finite-size calculations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Given a quantum critical wavefunction in any dimension, we propose a reconstructed Hamiltonian, analogous to the ones previously found for 1+1d CFT and for 2+1d bosonic liquid topologically-ordered states. We test numerically that, for known regularized approximate CFT groundstates (on the icosahedron and the fuzzy sphere), (1) they are close to the groundstate of their reconstructed Hamiltonian, and (2) the spectrum of their reconstructed Hamiltonian on the unit sphere has CFT properties (integer spacing of descendants) and matches known low-lying energies. We show that this provides an automated method to improve the finite-size effects in a fixed Hilbert space.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12637"></a>
### [Roto-Reflection Geometry of Pure Two-Qubit Entanglement](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12637v1)

**Authors:** Stanislav Filatov, Marcis Auzinsh  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12637v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops the concept of the Entanglement Roto-Reflection Plane (ERRP) to geometrically characterize pure two-qubit entanglement. It shows that maximally entangled states exhibit correlation patterns described by roto-reflections within the Pauli correlation tensor framework. This geometric invariant provides a covariant complement to scalar measures like concurrence.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a novel, geometrically intuitive invariant for quantifying bipartite quantum correlations that is covariant under local unitaries, offering a powerful theoretical tool beyond standard entanglement measures.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To characterize pure two-qubit entanglement using a natural geometric form that goes beyond simple scalar measures like concurrence.

**Main result.** The authors introduce the Entanglement Roto-Reflection Plane (ERRP), a geometric object derived from the Pauli correlation tensor, which characterizes the correlations of any pure bipartite entangled state.

**Method.** The analysis uses the Pauli decomposition of the density matrix to construct the correlation tensor $T$, and then extracts an improper orthogonal factor ($O$) that defines the ERRP geometry.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on pure two-qubit quantum states, analyzed using their Pauli correlation tensor $R_{\mu
u} = 	ext{Tr}(ho \sigma_\mu \otimes \sigma_
u)$. The framework is inherently mathematical and theoretical.

**Key observables.** Pauli correlation tensor ($T$), Concurrence ($C$), Entanglement Roto-Reflection Plane (ERRP) defined by an oriented normal ($\mathbf{n}$) and a signed angle ($\phi$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The degree of entanglement, quantified by concurrence $C$, which dictates how the ERRP is factored out from the full correlation tensor.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The construction is currently restricted to pure two-qubit states; extensions to mixed states require further decomposition techniques.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the Pauli decomposition visualization, showing local Bloch vectors and correlated triads. They also show maximally entangled states whose correlations are described by a roto-reflection geometry (e.g., in the xz-plane).

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes that entanglement has a geometric characterization beyond scalars. It derives this geometry using the Pauli tensor, showing how maximal entanglement corresponds to an improper orthogonal map (roto-reflection), and then generalizes this structure to partially entangled states by factoring out the concurrence contraction.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Pure two-qubit entanglement is usually characterized by scalar quantities such as concurrence. Here we show that it also has a natural geometric form. In the Pauli correlation tensor, maximally entangled states appear as improper orthogonal maps between two local Bloch spheres. These maps are roto-reflections. For partially entangled pure states, the same roto-reflection geometry is recovered after separating the contraction associated with concurrence. We call the corresponding geometric object the Entanglement Roto-Reflection Plane (ERRP). It organizes the maximally correlated directions of the two-qubit state and provides a covariant geometric complement to the scalar magnitude of entanglement.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.13251"></a>
### [Kubo-Martin-Schwinger conditions for non-Hermitian systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13251v1)

**Authors:** Chen Lan, Luyao Ma, Hao Yang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13251v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This paper extends the fundamental Kubo-Martin-Schwinger (KMS) condition of statistical mechanics from Hermitian to non-Hermitian quantum systems. It establishes that quasi-Hermiticity is equivalent to a positivity criterion on the thermal state, allowing for the definition of an $\eta$-Gibbs state that satisfies KMS relations. The work rigorously analyzes failure modes when spectra are complex or Hamiltonians exhibit exceptional points.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO physics and open quantum systems because it provides the rigorous mathematical framework needed to define thermal equilibrium when the underlying dynamics are non-Hermitian, which occurs naturally in realistic settings like dissipation or gain media.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central problem is extending the Kubo-Martin-Schwinger (KMS) thermal equilibrium condition, traditionally defined for Hermitian systems, to non-Hermitian quantum Hamiltonians.

**Main result.** The paper establishes that quasi-Hermiticity (defined via a metric $\eta$) is equivalent to the positivity of the biorthogonal Gibbs functional, and under these conditions, the associated $\eta$-Gibbs state satisfies the full KMS condition.

**Method.** The analysis employs functional-analytic techniques, utilizing spectral theory adapted for non-Hermitian operators through an auxiliary metric ($\eta$), and comparing results from three routes: quasi-Hermiticity, biorthogonal traces, and Lindblad dynamics.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on general non-Hermitian Hamiltonians $H$, which can be analyzed in the context of pseudo-Hermiticity ($H^\dagger = \eta H \eta^{-1}$) or as generators of open quantum systems described by Lindblad master equations. The system is characterized by its spectral properties and associated thermal states.

**Key observables.** The primary observables are the correlation functions $\langle A \sigma_{t} B angle$ and the generalized Gibbs functionals ($\omega_{	ext{bi}}$ or $\omega_\eta$), whose analyticity and boundary behavior define the KMS condition.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Inverse temperature ($eta$) is crucial, as are the metric operator ($\eta$) and the spectral properties of $H$, such as whether the spectrum is real or complex.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Key assumptions include the existence of a positive-definite metric $\eta$ (quasi-Hermiticity) and the requirement for analyticity in the complex time plane, which are necessary to prove the KMS relation.

**Figures summary.** The notes summarize comparisons between failure modes (Exceptional Points vs. Complex Spectra) across different KMS conditions, and compare structural requirements like $C^*$-algebra structure across various theoretical routes.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically investigates three complementary routes to the KMS condition: 1) Spectral theory for quasi-Hermitian systems ($\eta$-Gibbs state); 2) Biorthogonal trace identities; and 3) Quantum Detailed Balance (QDB) for open systems. It concludes by analyzing failure modes at exceptional points and complex spectra.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the extension of the Kubo--Martin--Schwinger (KMS) thermal equilibrium condition to non-Hermitian Hamiltonians with real spectra and biorthogonal eigensystems, providing a systematic analysis through three complementary routes. Our central result is a thermodynamic characterisation of quasi-Hermiticity: for $H \in M_d(\mathbb{C})$ diagonalisable with real spectrum, the biorthogonal Gibbs functional $ω_{\rm{bi}}(A) = Z_{\rm{bi}}^{-1} \sum_n e^{-βE_n}\langleφ_n|A|ψ_n\rangle$ satisfies $ω_{\rm{bi}}(A^†A) \geq 0$ for all $A$ if and only if $H$ is quasi-Hermitian. The proof constructs the metric $η$ directly from the eigenprojectors of $ω_{\rm{bi}}$ via the Riesz representation theorem, with no prior choice of $η$, providing a metric-free certificate of quasi-Hermiticity outside the Mostafazadeh--Scholtz framework. Under the full quasi-Hermitian hypothesis, we prove that the $η$-Gibbs state $ω_η(A) = Z_η^{-1}\, \rm{Tr}[ηe^{-βH}A]$ satisfies all three analytic KMS conditions, using the Hadamard three-line theorem and Bari's theorem on Riesz bases. The result is non-trivial: the transported state $\hatω(X) = \rm{Tr}[e^{-βh}Xη]/Z_η$ differs from the Gibbs state of the isospectral Hermitian partner $h = η^{1/2}Hη^{-1/2}$ whenever $[η,h]\neq 0$, so the KMS property cannot be deduced from the Hermitian theory by similarity. The gap between this result and the full Haag--Hugenholtz--Winnink $C^*$-algebraic framework is identified. Failure modes at exceptional points and for complex spectra are analysed, and the relation to the Fagnola--Umanità quantum detailed balance condition for open systems is discussed.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [A solvable model for unsupervised federated learning](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13045v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical work develops a framework using statistical physics tools to model federated learning as an inference problem. It analytically proves that interactions between students significantly boost the ability to recover a hidden generative signal from noisy, distributed data. The key insight is mapping this cooperative process onto equilibrium sampling in structured neural network models.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on ML, the mathematical machinery—using replica theory to analyze collective inference in complex interacting systems—is highly relevant to understanding glassy dynamics or pattern retrieval in quantum memory models.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper analyzes how interactions among multiple students enhance learning performance when inferring an underlying generative teacher pattern from local, potentially noisy or incomplete data realizations in a federated setting.

**Main result.** Cooperative student interactions are systematically beneficial, reducing the sample complexity required for recovery in high-noise regimes and enhancing overlap with the ground-truth signal compared to isolated learning.

**Method.** The analysis employs theoretical tools from equilibrium disordered systems, utilizing the replica trick to compute the quenched free energy density in the thermodynamic limit.

**Model / system.** The system models a teacher generating patterns (Mattis model) and multiple students receiving data via two mechanisms: student-dependent noise (SDN) or dataset partitioning (SDD). The interactions are captured by coupling terms in an extended Hamiltonian, mapped onto an RBM structure.

**Key observables.** Magnetization ($m$), pairwise overlap ($q_{uv}$), critical sample complexity ($\alpha_c$), and the free energy density $f(\Lambda)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Student number ($y$), coupling strength ($\gamma$), noise levels ($r$), and data loads ($\alpha_u$). The analysis explores limits like $y 	o \infty$ and $\gamma 	o \infty$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies heavily on the Replica Symmetric (RS) ansatz for overlap matrices and various saddle-point approximations in the thermodynamic limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase diagrams showing transitions between Paramagnetic, Signal-Retrieval, and Spin-Glass phases as a function of noise/data load. They compare coupled vs. isolated learning thresholds ($\alpha_c$).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the general theoretical framework using disordered systems tools (replica trick), then analyzing specific scenarios like SDN and SDD, deriving order parameter equations via variational principles, and finally characterizing phase transitions by solving determinant conditions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce a theoretical framework for analyzing federated learning in a generative setting through a teacher-multiple interacting students scenario, in which each student receives a distinct realization of the data, either through a different noise corruption or by accessing a different subset, possibly of varying size. Using theoretical tools in equilibrium disordered system, we analytically show that interactions among students systematically enhance learning performance: highly noisy students require fewer samples to recover the underlying pattern, while low-noise students achieve a larger overlap with the ground-truth signal. We derive the optimal Bayesian conditions for teacher recovery as functions of the sample complexity, noise level, and interaction strength, and validate these predictions through numerical simulations. The resulting dynamics can be mapped onto equilibrium sampling in a Restricted Boltzmann Machine with a structured hidden layer, providing a principled theoretical understanding of how interactions improve distributed generative modeling.

</details>

### [Andreev Reflection to Probe Momentum-Dependent Spin Polarization in Altermagnet CrSb](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13395v1)

**Authors:** Yan Zhang, Yixuan Luo, Yue Yang, Zilong Li, Weilong Qiu, Lunhui Hu, Yuanfeng Xu, Yanfeng Guo, Chao Cao, Xin Lu  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13395v1>

**Topic relevance:** `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** This work uses mechanical point-contact spectroscopy to measure Andreev reflection signatures in the altermagnetic material $	ext{CrSb}$. The authors successfully quantify momentum-dependent spin polarizations ($P_k$) for different crystal planes, confirming the unique magnetic character of altermagnets. This establishes Andreev reflection as a novel technique for mapping $k$-dependent spin textures.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter magnetism, the use of superconducting contacts (Andreev reflection) to probe electronic spin textures is a powerful diagnostic tool relevant to open quantum systems where coupling to leads or environments dictates measurable quantities.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To probe momentum-dependent spin polarization ($P_k$) in altermagnetic materials, which is crucial for next-generation spintronics but differs from conventional ferromagnets/antiferromagnets due to zero net magnetization.

**Main result.** The study successfully measured finite and distinct values for $P_k$ ($\approx 73.4\%$ for (0001), $67.9\%$ for (-1-120), and $61.9\%$ for (10-10)) in $	ext{CrSb}$ along different planes.

**Method.** Mechanical Point-Contact Spectroscopy (MPCS) was used to probe Andreev reflection on $	ext{CrSb}$ single crystals, supplemented by spatial line-scans.

**Model / system.** The system is the altermagnetic candidate $	ext{CrSb}$, which exhibits $g$-wave spin splitting. The analysis relies on fitting differential conductance $G(V)$ using a modified BTK model to extract $P_k$.

**Key observables.** Differential conductance $G(V)$, momentum-dependent spin polarization ($P_k$), domain size ($\approx 250-500\ 	ext{nm}$), and domain wall width ($\approx 250\ 	ext{nm}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Low temperatures (down to $0.3\ 	ext{K}$), superconducting gap ($\Delta$), barrier strength ($Z$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of the modified BTK model and assuming that the extracted $P_k$ values are intrinsic properties independent of temperature or contact geometry.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate spin polarization effects in various magnetic systems, show $G(V)$ spectra for three orientations with derived $P_k$, confirm temperature stability of $P_k$, and map out the domain structure via spatial line-scans.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the scientific problem by introducing altermagnetism. It details the experimental setup using MPCS on $	ext{CrSb}$ to measure Andreev reflection signatures across three crystal planes, reports the quantitative $P_k$ values, and supports these findings with domain structure mapping.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Altermagnetic materials have recently emerged as promising candidates for next-generation spintronic applications, characterized by the k-dependent spin-splitted band structure and a simultaneous zero-net-magnetization. Among them, altermagnetic candidate CrSb has attracted considerable attention, owing to its g-wave spin splitting and high Néel temperature. In this article, we employed mechanical point-contact spectroscopy (MPCS) with superconducting Nb tips to probe the Andreev reflection on CrSb single crystals along three principal crystallographic orientations. The extracted momentum-dependent spin polarizations are approximately 73.4% for the (0001) plane, 67.9% for the (-1-120) plane, and 61.9% for the (10-10) plane, respectively, distinct from conventional antiferromagnets. Furthermore, conductance spectra from spatial line-scans on the sample surface support the existence of altermagnetic domains with a characteristic size of 250-500 nm separated by domain-walls with width about 250 nm. These results strongly support the momentum-dependent spin polarization in altermagnetic CrSb and establish Andreev reflection as a new paradigm to probe k-dependent spin textures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.13454"></a>
### [Optical Implementation of Equilibrium Propagation Using Spatial Photonic Ising Machines](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13454v1)

**Authors:** Dimitri Vanden Abeele, Daniele Veraldi, Davide Pierangeli, Claudio Conti, Serge Massar  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13454v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **2/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13454_figures/2606.13454_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. (a) Equilibrium Propagation implemented using SPIM. EP follows a bi-level optimization. During infer- ence the energy is minimized using a combination of opti- cal measurements and digital terms. During learning the en- ergy landscape is modified to include the cost, correspond- ing to the additional force ±β∂C/∂sm. The trainable pa- rameters θ = {λ, ξ} are updated according to how the vari- ables conjugate to θ respond, at equilibrium, to the nudg- ing. The variables ∂E/∂λk are evaluated optically, while the variables ∂E/∂ξk,i are evaluated digitally. (b) Experimental setup. The SPIM evaluates the value of Mattis Hamiltonians Hk ∝1</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13454_figures/2606.13454_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Experimental Results. (a) Average cost and accu- racy over 7 independent training runs (4 epochs of 71 steps, 2 samples per step, moving average over 20 samples). (b) Av- erage confusion matrix of 7 independently trained networks, evaluated on the test set.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13454_figures/2606.13454_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13454_figures/2606.13454_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">

(a) The Spatial Light Modulator is used to encode a series of Mattis problems and blazed gratings onto the optical beam’s phase profile, while (b) exploiting gauge encoding for spins and interaction coefficients. (c) A cylindrical lens fo- cuses the propagating beam in the x-direction. The induced phase shift from the blazed gratings creates a set of distinct spots on the camera plane (d). **Summary.** The paper presents a novel method to implement Equilibrium Propagation, an advanced energy-based learning technique, using a Spatial Photonic Ising Machine. By encoding the required energy calculations optically via phase modulations on an SLM, they demonstrate a pathway toward ultra-low-power physical hardware for training neural networks. The results confirm high accuracy comparable to simulation benchmarks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly bridges concepts from statistical physics (Ising models, energy minimization) with modern machine learning paradigms (EP). The use of photonics to realize complex many-body interactions is highly relevant for quantum optics applications in computation.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to demonstrate a low-energy, physical implementation of Equilibrium Propagation (EP), an energy-based learning framework, using optical hardware.

**Main result.** The authors achieved high test accuracies on the MNIST dataset both experimentally and numerically, validating the viability of this hybrid optical-digital approach for machine learning training.

**Method.** They employ a Spatial Photonic Ising Machine (SPIM) to optically evaluate the energy functional associated with EP. Gradient calculations are performed using finite difference schemes combined with digital processing.

**Model / system.** The system uses SPIMs, which encode spins and couplings into the spatial degrees of freedom of coherent light propagating through a Spatial Light Modulator (SLM). The underlying physics involves calculating Ising-like Hamiltonians derived from the EP energy functional.

**Key observables.** Test set accuracy (%) on MNIST/Wine datasets, average cost, and gradient components ($\partial E/\partial 	heta$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Trainable parameters include coupling strengths $\{J_{in}, J_{dyn}\}$ and pattern weights $\{\lambda_k, \xi_{k,i}\}$. Key regimes involve the rank $K$ of the Mattis problems and the number of hidden units $N_d$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The approach relies on approximating complex derivatives using finite differences (e.g., $\pm\pi/4$ phase shifts) and assumes that structured architectures can overcome limitations of simple all-to-all connectivity.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the EP process, the experimental SPIM setup, and show test accuracy scaling versus coupling rank $K$ or hidden units $N_d$ for MNIST simulations.

**Paper structure.** The paper moves from defining the theoretical framework of EP (energy functional) to detailing the physical mapping onto the SPIM Hamiltonian. It then describes the hybrid optical-digital gradient calculation methods, validates performance on Wine experimentally, and finally presents extensive numerical results on MNIST across various parameter regimes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Equilibrium Propagation offers a compelling alternative to traditional machine learning for training energy-based networks. Here we demonstrate a hybrid optical-digital implementation of EP using a Spatial Photonic Ising Machine (SPIM). The SPIM exploits the gauge transformation method to optically encode both continuous neuron states and rank-1 binary trainable patterns as phase modulations via a spatial light modulator, with inference realized using a finite difference scheme. The experimental system is evaluated on the Wine classification dataset. The potential of this approach, including the use of continuous couplings and structured coupling matrices, is evaluated numerically on the more complex MNIST dataset. Our work provides a concrete pathway toward energy-efficient physical implementations of Equilibrium Propagation.

</details>

### [Steady-State Noise Signatures of Lindbladian Exceptional Points](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13377v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical work demonstrates that while average currents do not reveal the signatures of Exceptional Points (EPs) in steady-state open quantum systems, current noise correlation functions do. By analyzing these higher-order correlations using a two-qubit model, the authors show that the spectral structure of the noise clearly distinguishes between different dynamical regimes defined by the proximity to an EP.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant to AMO physics and open quantum systems as it provides a powerful, non-trivial diagnostic tool (noise spectroscopy) to probe fundamental degeneracies (EPs) in dissipative dynamics where standard measurements fail.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The work aims to find diagnostic signatures of Lindbladian Exceptional Points (LEPs) in the steady-state regime of open quantum systems, as average observables fail to reveal this structure.

**Main result.** Current noise correlation functions retain clear signatures of LEPs in the steady state, distinguishing them from the behavior of average currents. The coalescence of eigenvalues leads to distinct polynomial time dependencies in the noise spectrum across different dynamical regimes (overdamped, underdamped, critical).

**Method.** The authors derive general expressions for two-point current correlation functions within a Lindblad master equation framework and analyze their spectral properties.

**Model / system.** The study uses open quantum systems modeled by the Lindblad master equation, exemplified by two interacting qubits coupled to external reservoirs. The dynamics are analyzed via spectral decomposition of the Liouvillian superoperator into Jordan normal form.

**Key observables.** Two-point current correlation function (noise), steady-state average currents, and the frequency spectrum of the noise $S^s_{\alpha\alpha'}(\omega)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling strength $g$ relative to a critical value $g^*$, which dictates the system's dynamical regime (overdamped, underdamped, or critical).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that local jump operators correctly capture the dynamics and relies on spectral decomposition techniques for solving the master equation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the distinct temporal structures of steady-state noise functions across overdamped, underdamped, and critical regimes. One figure shows the spectrum of a reduced Liouvillian exhibiting coalescence at an EP.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the theoretical framework using the Lindblad equation, derives expressions for both average currents and current noise, analyzes these observables in the steady state, and finally illustrates the distinct signatures of LEPs by analyzing the two-qubit model across different coupling regimes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Exceptional points (EPs) are non-Hermitian degeneracies at which two or more eigenvalues and their corresponding eigenvectors coalesce. In open quantum systems, exceptional points can arise in the Lindbladian governing the dissipative dynamics. Their signatures have so far been mainly identified in finite-time observables, such as transient currents, while steady-state average currents generally provide no direct evidence of the underlying exceptional-point structure. In this work, we demonstrate that signatures of Lindbladian EPs can nevertheless be accessed in the steady-state regime through current noise. We derive general expressions for current correlation functions within a Lindblad master-equation framework and show, in particular, how exceptional points affect their behaviour as a function of the time delay. We illustrate these results with the paradigmatic example of two interacting qubits coupled to two reservoirs, where the steady-state noise clearly distinguishes overdamped, underdamped, and critical regimes. Our results establish current correlation functions as a steady-state probe of Lindbladian EPs in open quantum systems.

</details>

### [Certifying Nonclassical Proper-Time Histories with a Quantum Clock](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12755v1)

**Authors:** Shuai Zeng

**Summary.** The paper develops a rigorous method to certify nonclassical proper-time histories in quantum clocks by treating the problem as a channel-certification task. It proves that coherent recombination of distinct history paths generates an observable signal that cannot be reproduced by classical mixtures of those same histories. This provides a fundamental, resource-theoretic test for probing relativistic time effects beyond simple dephasing measurements.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, generalizable quantum resource theory tool—channel certification—applied directly to fundamental physics measurements (time dilation), bridging foundational quantum information concepts with cutting-edge AMO metrology.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is developing a rigorous method to experimentally certify that quantum proper-time histories, imprinted on clock signals by relativistic effects, are genuinely nonclassical and cannot be simulated by classical mixtures of specified history outcomes.

**Main result.** A Choi-rank separation criterion, demonstrated via a minimal two-branch Ramsey protocol, proves that the conditioned operation associated with coherent recombination of distinct proper-time histories falls outside the convex set of classically simulable channels ($	ext{CPTH}(H)$).

**Method.** The authors employ channel-certification techniques by comparing the measured conditional signal against the theoretical prediction for classical mixtures. They utilize a two-branch Ramsey protocol and develop resource-theoretic diagnostics like the robustness measure $	ext{RH}_{	ext{PTH}}(\Phi)$.

**Model / system.** The physical system is based on precision quantum clocks, such as trapped-ion optical clocks, where relativistic time dilation phases are imprinted during clock evolution. The theoretical framework models the process using history unitaries ($V_h$) and analyzes the resulting channel structure.

**Key observables.** Nonzero postselected imbalance ($\langle Z angle_+ 
eq 0$), which serves as a witness distinguishing quantum from classical processes; visibility factors $\eta_{tot} = \eta_h\eta_c\eta_m$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The effective accumulated phase $	heta_{eff}$ and the fidelity parameters ($\eta_h, \eta_c, \eta_m$) that quantify experimental imperfections.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The certification is operational and relative only to a finite, specified set of proper-time histories ($H$). The analysis relies on identifying single-Kraus, subnormalized-unitary outcomes for the strongest theoretical bounds.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the comparison between the predicted classical signal ($\langle Z angle_{	ext{cl}} = 0$) and the measured non-zero signals from the Ramsey protocol, demonstrating the separation boundary.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing a hierarchy of classical limitations (single-time dephasing $	o$ averaged histories $	o$ conditioned recombination). It then introduces the Choi-rank criterion using a minimal two-branch witness and concludes with resource-theoretic visibility models to quantify experimental feasibility.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum clocks can acquire relativistic phases from motional or gravitational proper-time differences, but reduced clock dephasing alone does not certify nonclassical proper-time histories. We formulate this distinction as a channel-certification problem. First, we show that any two-level single-time dephasing signal, including one generated by an effective quantum proper-time label, admits a classical random proper-time representation. We then define the convex set of classical mixtures of experimentally specified proper-time histories and prove a Choi-rank separation criterion for conditioned coherent history recombination. A two-branch Ramsey protocol gives explicit bright- and dark-port population witnesses outside this classical set. The certification is operational and relative to the specified history set: it rules out classical mixtures of the same implemented proper-time histories, not arbitrary classical protocols with different histories or controls.

</details>

**Summary.** The research demonstrates that coupling twisted bilayer graphene with WSe2 induces strong Spin-Orbit Coupling (SOC), enabling the realization of robust, electrically switchable Quantum Anomalous Hall states. By controlling this SOC, researchers can engineer the magnetic state and tune the system between different topological regimes ($C=\pm 2$ to $C=\pm 1$) using gate voltages. This work establishes a powerful methodology for designing next-generation topological electronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete example of how engineered proximity effects (SOC) can be used in 2D materials to stabilize topological phases and enable electrical control over fundamental quantum properties like chirality, which is highly relevant for designing novel spintronic/topological devices.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research aims to engineer electrically-switchable Quantum Anomalous Hall (QAH) states in graphene moiré systems by controlling their magnetic properties.

**Main result.** Proximity coupling to WSe2 induces Spin-Orbit Coupling (SOC), which allows for the engineering of robust, nonvolatile QAH states and enables tuning between different Chern numbers without resetting the magnetic state.

**Method.** The study combines transport measurements (measuring Hall/longitudinal resistance) with theoretical modeling using continuum Hamiltonians and self-consistent Hartree-Fock calculations to analyze spin and orbital magnetization.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is twisted bilayer graphene (tMBG) interfaced with WSe2. The model incorporates the Bistritzer-MacDonald framework, adding proximity-induced Ising SOC ($\lambda_I$) and analyzing electron interactions via Hartree-Fock mean-field theory.

**Key observables.** Quantized Hall resistance ($R_{yx}$), longitudinal resistance ($R_{xx}$), magnetic hysteresis loops, Chern numbers ($C=\pm 2, \pm 1$), and orbital/spin magnetization components.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angle ($	heta \approx 1.1 - 1.5^\circ$), carrier density ($
u$), gate voltage displacement field ($D$), and the strength of the induced SOC ($\lambda_I$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that proximity-induced SOC dominates over intrinsic magnetic energy in certain regimes, allowing for controlled switching between spin/valley polarized ground states.

**Figures summary.** Figures show quantized QAH plateaus ($R_{yx}$), gate-driven magnetic hysteresis loops demonstrating state switching, and theoretical plots illustrating the dependence of total magnetization on filling factor ($
u$) and SOC strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical platform (tMBG/WSe2) to confirming the key mechanism (SOC via quantum oscillations), demonstrating the primary effect (quantized QAH states and switching), and finally detailing the theoretical underpinning of magnetization control through spin-valley coupling.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nonvolatile gate-driven switching of quantum anomalous Hall (QAH) states in graphene moiré systems provides a promising route toward topological electronics based on chiral edge states. However, deliberate use of this switching mechanism requires control over both the magnetic properties and metastability of QAH states. While previous demonstrations mostly relied on the intrinsic magnetic energy landscape of moiré devices, here we show that this landscape can be engineered through proximity coupling to WSe2. We find that proximitizing twisted monolayer-bilayer graphene by WSe2 reshapes the magnetization reversals responsible for nonvolatile electrical switching of QAH states. We attribute this effect to the proximity-induced spin-orbit coupling (SOC), which can lock spin and valley and modify the magnetization of the competing states involved in switching compared with non-proximitized graphene systems. Our findings establish proximity-induced SOC as a new way to engineer magnetic properties and switchable magnetic states in graphene-based systems. We further demonstrate that strong magnetic metastability in tMBG allows the magnetic states to be gate-tuned between QAH and metallic regimes, and between QAH states with Chern numbers |C| = 2 and 1 without resetting the magnetic state. This functionality points toward new device architectures based on QAH chiral edge states.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.13641"></a>
### [Generalized two-qubit Hamiltonian for Projective Quantum Feature Maps](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13641v1)

**Authors:** Rafael Simões do Carmo, Edson Amaro Junior, Felipe Fanchini  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13641v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13641_figures/2606.13641_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Example of the 4-qubit encoding 3 features using Heisenberg PQFM. Each qubit first undergoes a local Haar- random unitary transformation, denoted by U1, . . . , U4. The entangling stage, grouped here as Block 1, then applies the sequence of two-qubit rotations RZZ, RY Y , and RXX to the disjoint pairs (q0, q1) and (q2, q3), followed by the same se- quence on the central pair (q1, q2). More blocks can be added for encoding more features. The corresponding rotation an- gles are denoted by θ0, θ1, and θ2, where θi = 2γηi, γ is a scaling parameter, and ηi is defined in Eq. 24.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13641_figures/2606.13641_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: Circuit representation of a of a single-layer diagonal XYZ PQFM with twelve features mapped onto a four-qubit register by a multi-axis feature encoding. After Hadamard initialization, each qubit receives three local rotations, Rx, Ry, and Rz, allowing three distinct normalized classical features to be encoded per qubit through the active Pauli axes. The subsequent two-qubit rotations implement the diagonal interaction channels XX, Y Y , and ZZ, with angles determined by mutual-information coefficients between the features assigned to the corresponding qubit-axis slots. The nearest-neighbor chain shown here is illustrative; in the hardware-aware implementation, the interaction...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13641_figures/2606.13641_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4: Nested validation protocol used for model selection and external performance estimation.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13641_figures/2606.13641_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5: Paired statistical improvements over the classical baseline for the Molecular Toxicity dataset. Panel (a) shows the CD-Ising-glass PQFM, panel (b) the Heisenberg map, and panel (c) the XYZ feature-map family introduced in this work. The plotted intervals correspond to the mean paired deltas and 95% confidence intervals. Colors indicate the qubit budget used in each configuration, with blue, orange, and green corresponding to 52, 78, and 156 qubits, respectively. The labels in parentheses indicate the measured observable used to extract the corresponding quantum features. Unless otherwise annotated, results correspond to ibm_kingston; panel (b) uses ibm_marrakesh.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper proposes and rigorously tests generalized two-qubit Hamiltonian Projected Quantum Feature Maps (PQFMs) as an advanced method to encode classical data features onto quantum circuits. By allowing multiple feature encodings per qubit axis, the framework increases information density while remaining hardware-compatible. The results demonstrate that these generalized maps offer a promising pathway for achieving measurable performance gains in quantum machine learning tasks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges condensed matter physics concepts (Hamiltonian engineering, spin models) with applied machine learning, providing a concrete framework for mapping complex physical interactions into feature spaces relevant for near-term quantum computation benchmarking.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is generating high-quality, explicit classical features from quantum states using quantum processors for use in classical machine learning models.

**Main result.** A generalized two-qubit Hamiltonian family of Projected Quantum Feature Maps (PQFMs) provides a promising and consistent route toward achieving statistically supported gains over matched classical baselines on biomedical datasets.

**Method.** The authors construct PQFMs by defining data-dependent Hamiltonians that evolve the quantum state, followed by measuring local Pauli expectation values to extract explicit classical features.

**Model / system.** The models are based on generalized two-qubit Hamiltonian structures (e.g., XYZ type) applied to qubits, extending previous work like CD-Ising-glass and Heisenberg maps. The feature encoding allows for multi-axis assignment of classical variables onto the Pauli axes of the qubits.

**Key observables.** Local single-qubit ($\langle \sigma^a_j angle$) and two-qubit ($\langle \sigma^a_j \sigma^b_k angle$) Pauli expectation values, which form the explicit classical feature vector $x'$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis is performed across multiple biomedical datasets (e.g., Molecular Toxicity, Breast Cancer), varying qubit counts (up to 156), and different Hamiltonian structures (diagonal, cross-axis, full).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Performance gains are shown to be highly dependent on the specific dataset, the chosen encoding strategy, the measured observables, and the underlying hardware conditions.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate circuit representations of PQFMs for various qubit counts and feature map types (e.g., single-layer diagonal XYZ PQFM). Tables summarize quantitative performance metrics (AUC, F1) across multiple datasets and QPU architectures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the generalized Hamiltonian framework, details multi-axis encoding strategies, benchmarks these maps against established methods on several biomedical classification tasks using nested cross-validation, and concludes by assessing empirical quantum utility.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Projected quantum feature maps provide a strategy for using quantum processors as feature generators for classical machine-learning models. Building on counterdiabatic Ising-glass and one-dimensional Heisenberg PQFMs, we introduce a generalized two-qubit Hamiltonian-based PQFM that provides a unified way to encode classical features through local Pauli fields and pairwise two-qubit Pauli interactions. This construction allows distinct classical variables to be embedded along different Pauli axes of the same qubit, increasing the information density of shallow circuits while remaining compatible with hardware constraints. We develop and implement these methods in pqfmlib, a publicly available Python library for constructing, executing, and benchmarking Hamiltonian-based PQFMs.We then benchmark the generalized Hamiltonian PQFMs against reference PQFMs on four biomedical classification datasets under a nested cross-validation protocol with paired statistical tests. Quantum features are generated using both IBM quantum processors with up to 156 qubits and statevector simulations. Our results show that the generalized two-qubit Hamiltonian family provides the most consistent pattern of statistically supported gains over matched classical baselines, although the performance of all methods depends on the dataset, encoding strategy, measured observables, and hardware conditions. These findings support generalized Hamiltonian PQFMs as a promising route toward near-term quantum utility.

</details>

### [Population dynamics of surface-mediated autocatalytic processes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13498v1)

**Authors:** Denis S. Grebenkov, Yilin Ye  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13498v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** This work analyzes how a particle population evolves when diffusion is coupled with surface reactions involving both loss (absorption) and multiplication (catalysis). By deriving the generating function and applying advanced spectral analysis, the authors precisely map out three distinct long-term dynamic regimes—decaying, steady, or exploding—based on the relative strengths of the catalytic versus absorbing rates.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical statistical mechanics, the rigorous treatment of nonlinear boundary conditions and the classification of dynamic regimes (sub/critical/supercritical) using spectral theory provides powerful mathematical techniques applicable to open quantum systems or reaction-diffusion models where boundaries impose non-trivial constraints.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the population dynamics of autocatalytic processes where particles diffuse in a complex environment, undergoing competing mechanisms of killing (absorption) and replication (branching) at specific surfaces.

**Main result.** The long-time behavior of the population size is characterized by distinct regimes—decaying, steady-state, or exponential growth—determined by comparing catalytic and absorption rates against spectral properties of the underlying diffusion operator.

**Method.** The analysis employs systematic theoretical tools including generating functions, transformation into nonlinear integral/partial differential equations, and advanced spectral methods (Laplace transforms) to solve for asymptotic limits.

**Model / system.** The model describes particle diffusion in a bounded domain $\Omega$ with boundaries partitioned into an absorbing region ($\Gamma_a$), a catalytic region ($\Gamma_c$), and a reflecting region ($\Gamma_r$). The process is stochastic, involving both spatial transport and population change via branching.

**Key observables.** Population size $N(t)$, generating function $G_s(t|x_0)$, mean $\langle N(t) angle$, variance, and the asymptotic limit of $G_s$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Diffusion coefficient ($D$), catalytic rate ($q_c$), absorption rate ($q_a$), and geometric parameters defining the boundary regions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a bounded domain with smooth boundaries, allowing for spectral decomposition of the underlying diffusion operator; approximations are used for short-time behavior and asymptotic limits.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate random realizations showing fluctuations across different catalytic rates ($q_c$), and plot the critical lines separating subcritical ($\lambda_{-0} > 0$), critical ($\lambda_{-0} = 0$), and supercritical ($\lambda_{-0} < 0$) regimes based on reaction rates.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the physical setup (diffusion with boundary reactions) to deriving the governing mathematical equations for the generating function. It then systematically analyzes the long-time asymptotic behavior by transforming these equations into spectral problems and solving them in different rate regimes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the population dynamics of surface-mediated autocatalytic processes, in which particles diffuse in a complex environment towards surface regions where they can be either killed or replicated. These opposite mechanisms compete with each other and lead to a sophisticated stochastic evolution of the population size. We provide a systematic analysis of the generating function of the population size. We also deduce its distribution, mean, variance and higher-order moments. For this purpose, we employ several equivalent descriptions of these quantities in terms of nonlinear integral equations and partial differential equations with nonlinear boundary conditions. We inspect the long-time behavior of the population dynamics in three regimes when the mean population size vanishes, reaches a steady-state level, or grows exponentially. A numerical solution of the underlying integral equations and independent Monte Carlo simulations support our theoretical predictions.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.13205"></a>
### [Achieving Heisenberg limit under noisy conditions with quantum Zeno dynamics and dynamical decoupling](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13205v1)

**Authors:** Ke Zeng, Bakmou Lahcen, Yu Jiang, Kok Chuan Tan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13205v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**
