</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper rigorously analyzes how Quantum Zeno Dynamics and Dynamical Decoupling can suppress environmental noise in quantum sensors to reach the ultimate Heisenberg Limit. It establishes conditions for these methods and crucially demonstrates that they offer a viable, sometimes superior, alternative to traditional Quantum Error Correction when dealing with specific types of open system dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO physics and open quantum systems as it provides concrete, resource-aware protocols (QZD/DD) that can surpass theoretical limitations imposed by standard error correction techniques when dealing with realistic noise models.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is determining the necessary and sufficient conditions under which Quantum Zeno Dynamics (QZD) and Dynamical Decoupling (DD) can effectively suppress environmental noise to achieve the Heisenberg Limit (HL) in quantum metrology.

**Main result.** The authors prove that QZD/DD can achieve HL even when standard Quantum Error Correction (QEC) methods fail, demonstrating that combining both techniques allows saturation of the fundamental precision limit.

**Method.** Theoretical analysis involving deriving effective Hamiltonians for QZD and DD under various noise models (Markovian), and comparing these conditions against those required by QEC theory.

**Model / system.** The system involves a quantum probe ($S$) interacting with an environment ($E$), described by a total Hamiltonian $H_{tot}$. The analysis uses the Lindblad master equation for the Markovian regime, focusing on signal encoding and noise suppression mechanisms.

**Key observables.** Quantum Fisher Information (QFI), which is used to diagnose whether the measurement precision approaches the Heisenberg Limit ($\propto 1/t^2$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The key parameters include the system Hamiltonian $H_S$, the environment coupling terms ($S_i$), and the control pulse timings ($\Delta t$ or $	au$) for QZD/DD.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumptions include time-independent system-environment interaction, and approximations like Born-Markov and secular limits are used when comparing to QEC theory.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the Quantum Fisher Information (QFI) vs. time for various protocols, showing that while individual QZD or DD applications may fall short, their combination successfully saturates the Heisenberg Limit.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically establishes necessary and sufficient conditions for noise suppression via QZD and DD individually, then develops a comparison framework against QEC theory, culminating in proving the efficacy of hybrid protocols.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum Zeno dynamics (QZD) and dynamical decoupling (DD) are useful tools that enable the effective suppression of noise in quantum systems. We consider the problem of when (i) noise can be suppressed and (ii) Heisenberg limit (HL) can be achieved in quantum metrology, and prove necessary and sufficient conditions for when QZD and DD are useful for achieving these two goals. We also show that in the Markovian regime, there are scenarios where preventing errors using QZD/DD may enable HL to be achieved where current QEC methods may not. Finally, we demonstrate that the combination of both techniques can allow individually imperfect QZD and DD strategies to saturate HL.

</details>

### [Characterizing the functional role of quantum coherence in energy transfer](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13404v1)

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops a general theoretical framework using projection operators and memory kernels to quantify how quantum coherence influences energy transfer rates. By applying this to an electronic dimer model coupled to a structured bath, the authors demonstrate that coherence can act as a switch, either boosting or dampening the rate depending on system parameters.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous, quantitative tool ($f$) to diagnose coherence effects in energy transfer, which is crucial for designing artificial light-harvesting systems or understanding biological processes where coherent dynamics are suspected.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to quantitatively characterize the functional role of quantum coherence in modulating energy transfer rates within open quantum systems.

**Main result.** Quantum coherence can either enhance or suppress the overall rate of energy transfer, depending on system parameters like detuning and coupling strength. A figure of merit quantifies this relative contribution.

**Method.** The authors employ Nakajima-Zwanzig projection operators to derive a generalized memory kernel identity, allowing for the separation of coherent and incoherent contributions to the dynamics.

**Model / system.** The study uses an electronic dimer model (two coupled sites) interacting with structured phonon baths. The dynamics are governed by the Liouville-von Neumann equation, analyzed using techniques derived from HEOM.

**Key observables.** The figure of merit $f_{\alpha	oeta} = 1 - k^{(R)}_{\alpha	oeta}/k^{(Q)}_{\alpha	oeta}$ quantifies coherence's relative impact on the energy transfer rate.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Electronic detuning ($\Delta\epsilon$), coupling strength ($V$), reorganization energy ($\lambda$), and spectral density parameters ($\gamma, \Omega$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes a stationary state limit ($t 	o \infty$) and utilizes the HEOM framework, which requires truncating an infinite hierarchy.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate $f_{2	o 1}$ as a function of detuning ($\Delta\epsilon$) or reorganization energy ($\lambda$), showing distinct regimes of coherence-induced suppression (gray) or enhancement (pink).

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theoretical framework using projection operators to derive memory kernel identities, applies this formalism to the dimer model dynamics via HEOM, and analyzes how specific parameters tune the resulting rate modification quantified by $f$.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum coherence is understood to play a role in excitation energy transfer in open quantum systems, yet a quantitative approach to assessing its influence on the transfer process is still missing. Using Nakajima-Zwanzig projection operators, we derive a general memory kernel identity that enables us to characterize and quantify the impact of coherence in the eigenenergy basis on a generalized rate of energy transfer. Applying our approach to the electronic dynamics of a dimer coupled to a structured phonon bath, we demonstrate how quantum coherence acts to modulate energy transfer.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12769"></a>
### [Matrix phase-space representations for quantum symmetries](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12769v1)

**Authors:** Peter D. Drummond, Alexander S. Dellios, Margaret D. Reid  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12769v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

</div>

**Summary.** The paper introduces the novel 'matrix phase-space' formalism to simulate complex many-body quantum dynamics by incorporating global quantum symmetries directly into the state representation. This method projects the system onto a reduced Hilbert space, leading to significantly lower sampling errors in simulations of systems like Gaussian Boson Sampling. It provides essential mathematical identities for mapping quantum evolution equations into solvable forms.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it provides a powerful theoretical tool—the matrix-P representation—to tackle the exponential complexity of simulating quantum systems with symmetries, which is crucial for understanding near-term photonic quantum computing devices like GBS.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is developing a mathematically rigorous, general phase-space representation that can accurately simulate large-scale many-body quantum dynamics while explicitly incorporating global quantum symmetries.

**Main result.** The matrix phase-space method provides an expansion that projects the density matrix onto a reduced Hilbert space defined by symmetry projectors, significantly reducing sampling errors compared to standard methods like Wigner or Q-functions.

**Method.** The authors introduce the 'matrix phase-space' representation, which generalizes existing phase-space techniques by encoding symmetries ($\hat{\Pi}_{
u}$) directly into the basis expansion and kernel operator. This allows for deriving necessary operator identities mapping Liouville/master equations to generalized Fokker-Planck Equations.

**Model / system.** The theory is applied to general bosonic quantum systems, with specific detailed applications shown for Gaussian Boson Sampling (GBS) quantum computers and analyzing the dynamics of squeezed vacuum states in lossy environments. The formalism handles both discrete symmetries (like parity) and continuous symmetry limits.

**Key observables.** Total photo-count distribution; sampling errors/difference errors between simulated and exact distributions; expectation values of photon number ($\langle \hat{n}_j angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Squeezing parameter ($r$); loss rate (e.g., $\approx 3\%$); symmetry projection indices ($	ilde{p}$); the limit $M 	o \infty$ for continuum approximations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes that symmetries can be effectively captured by commuting projectors, and derivations often rely on specific factorizations of the kernel operator and vanishing boundary terms when transforming dynamics into FPEs.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the matrix-P distribution against exact results for GBS (lossless and lossy), demonstrating superior accuracy in reducing sampling errors compared to standard positive-P methods, especially in low-photon-number regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from general theory by defining the matrix phase-space expansion using symmetry projectors. It then derives fundamental operator identities for this representation (e.g., action of $\hat{a}_k$ on projected states). Finally, it validates the method through detailed applications to GBS simulations in both ideal and lossy settings.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce a general phase-space representation that includes global quantum symmetries in the basis expansion. This method, called matrix phase-space, projects the basis onto a reduced Hilbert space, which can greatly reduce sampling errors of many-body quantum simulations and unifies several previous phase-space methods. The purpose of this paper is to provide detailed proofs of basic theorems and operator identities. We also treat several different types of symmetries. To illustrate the benefits of matrix phase-space methods, we give a detailed derivation of a recent application to the topical problem of verifying the outputs of Gaussian boson sampling (GBS) quantum computers with photon number resolving detectors. This has exponential complexity, and using parity symmetry reduces sampling errors by very large factors relative to earlier methods.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.13284"></a>
### [Where a Quantum Reservoir Works: A Transferable Operating Band](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13284v1)

**Authors:** Markus Baumann, Itamar Fink, Johannes Wittmann, Claudia Linnhoff-Popien, Jonas Stein

**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper identifies a specific, transferable 'operating band' in the parameter space of a dissipative quantum reservoir that guarantees good performance across multiple learning tasks. By systematically testing and ablating individual physical mechanisms (like damping or mixing), the authors prove that this regime requires the joint action of all components. This provides a crucial diagnostic tool for designing stable quantum machine learning hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides concrete guidelines for designing quantum machine learning hardware by identifying robust operating regimes in the control space, which is crucial for realizing practical, stable quantum computation devices.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is determining the optimal, fixed operating regime for a quantum reservoir system so that it can perform well on various learning tasks without requiring dynamic optimization during training.

**Main result.** Good performance concentrates in a single, transferable 'operating band' within the control parameter space ($eta, \lambda, \gamma$), whose existence depends mechanistically on the joint action of all physical components (e.g., damping and mixing).

**Method.** The authors map performance across three key physical controls by defining an operating band using a stability criterion based on validation percentile rank across multiple tasks.

**Model / system.** The system is a dissipative quantum reservoir implemented on a small qubit architecture, evolving via input driving, local mixing ($U_{	ext{mix}}$), ZZ coupling ($U_\lambda$), and amplitude damping ($D_\gamma$).

**Key observables.** Validation percentile rank ($	ext{r}_{j,s}$), Memory Capacity ($	ext{MC}$), Information-Processing Capacity ($	ext{IPC}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Input drive strength ($eta$), Coupling strength ($\lambda$), Dissipation rate ($\gamma$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to simulated instances of this specific reservoir architecture family, and practical noise/calibration issues are ignored.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the consistent location of the operating band across different tasks (Fig. 1), demonstrate that ablating any core mechanism destroys the band (Fig. 3), and illustrate that memory capacity alone can locate this optimal regime before task testing (Fig. 4).

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the problem by noting fixed dynamics, then maps performance over three controls to find a stable 'Operating Band.' It rigorously tests this band's necessity and transferability by ablating individual physical mechanisms or comparing it against simpler diagnostics like memory capacity.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In quantum reservoir computing, a fixed quantum system transforms an input signal, while learning reduces to training a simple linear readout on its measured outputs. Since the quantum dynamics themselves are never optimized, the method is well suited to today's hardware. Yet these dynamics must still be chosen carefully, because their settings remain fixed throughout training and inference. It therefore remains an open question where, in its control space, a fixed quantum system learns well. We address this question for a dissipative reservoir by mapping performance over three central physical controls: the strength of the input drive, the coupling between neighboring qubits, and the rate of dissipation. Good performance concentrates in a single, well-defined operating region of this control space. This region transfers across tasks and reservoir initializations, and the same memory-defined regime persists under architectural changes. It is also mechanistically grounded, since it disappears whenever any of the mechanisms that create it is removed. Finally, the region can be located cheaply before any task is run, using a simple memory diagnostic.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.13650"></a>
### [To Cool, or Not to Cool? Displacement Sensing with Hot Quantum States](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13650v1)

**Authors:** Piotr T. Grochowski  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13650v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper challenges the assumption that quantum-enhanced bosonic sensing requires cooling to the ground state. By analyzing hot quantum states generated via squeezing or catification, it identifies two mechanisms—parity filtering and coherent branch interference—that maintain high sensitivity even with thermal noise. Ultimately, it shows that optimizing for the rate of information gain over time favors direct preparation of structured hot states over extensive pre-cooling.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a crucial resource for quantum sensing by shifting the focus from achieving perfect initial state purity to engineering specific coherence structures within mixed thermal states, which is highly relevant for practical implementations in trapped ions or cavity QED.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether quantum-enhanced displacement sensing using bosonic systems requires mandatory cooling to near the ground state, or if high sensitivity can be achieved directly from thermal (hot) states.

**Main result.** Hot-state engineering protocols—specifically those utilizing parity selection or coherent branch interference—can achieve quantum enhancement without requiring full ground-state initialization. Optimal sensing depends on maximizing coherence per unit total experimental time, not just single-shot QFI.

**Method.** The analysis involves deriving the Quantum Fisher Information (QFI) for various hot states and comparing the scaling of this resource under different decoherence channels (loss, heating, dephasing).

**Model / system.** Bosonic systems are modeled using master equations incorporating bosonic loss, motional heating, and number dephasing. Specific protocols analyzed include parity-filtered cat states (KPO/qcMAP) and Echoed Conditional Displacement (ECD) cat states.

**Key observables.** Quantum Fisher Information (QFI), $\Delta	ilde{\epsilon}$, noise susceptibility ($\dot{F}$), and the rate objective $R = F / t_{cycle}$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Initial thermal occupation ($n_{th}$), squeezing parameter ($r$), decoherence rates ($\Gamma_h, \gamma_-, \Gamma_\phi$), and protocol preparation time ($	au_d$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on effective two-branch models or approximations for the rate objective $R$, assuming certain overhead times are negligible or fixed.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate operational cycles, scaling laws for different mechanisms (parity filtering vs. branch separation), and normalized QFI dynamics under various noise channels (loss, diffusion, dephasing).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from identifying the core problem to analyzing two key enhancement mechanisms (Parity Filtering and Coherent Interference). It then rigorously compares these hot-state protocols across different decoherence models, culminating in an optimization comparison between cooling overhead versus direct preparation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum-enhanced displacement sensing with bosonic systems is typically formulated assuming that the oscillator is cooled close to its ground state before nonclassical probe preparation. We investigate whether such near-ground-state initialization is necessary, or whether sensitive probes can instead be generated directly from thermal states. We analyze hot quantum probes produced by squeezing, number-raising, and Schrödinger-cat-state generation applied to thermal inputs. We identify two distinct mechanisms by which thermal mixedness can remain compatible with enhanced displacement sensitivity. First, projecting a mixed probe onto a definite parity sector removes the usual thermal suppression of the displacement quantum Fisher information, which can then increase with initial thermal occupation. Second, coherent superpositions of opposite displacements can retain sensitivity through coherence between their displaced components, even when the underlying state is mixed. We use these two mechanisms to classify hot-state protocols according to whether their sensitivity comes from parity selection, coherence between displaced components, or both. Finally, we formulate an experimentally relevant optimization problem comparing initial cooling with direct hot-state preparation under realistic decoherence and show that complete cooling is not universally optimal. Our results establish hot-state engineering as a route to quantum-enhanced bosonic displacement sensing without mandatory ground-state initialization.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes 'spacetime quantum mechanics' (SQM) to resolve the conflict between space and time in QM by defining generalized 'spacetime states.' It demonstrates that various existing formalisms—like path integrals and pseudo-density matrices—are mathematically equivalent manifestations of this single underlying state object. This unification provides deep insights into how temporal correlations govern quantum dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, unified mathematical framework for understanding time evolution in quantum theory, which is crucial for developing consistent theories of quantum gravity or relativistic QFTs.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central problem is formulating quantum mechanics (QM) in a way that treats space and time symmetrically, addressing the conceptual tension between relativity and canonical QM.

**Main result.** The authors unify numerous existing spacetime approaches by showing they all derive from a single underlying mathematical object: the 'spacetime state' ($R$).

**Method.** They develop a formalism using a finite-time tensor product Hilbert space ($\mathcal{H} = igotimes_{t=0}^{N-1} \mathcal{H}_t$) and define spacetime states as generalized quantum operators that encode temporal correlations.

**Model / system.** The model is 'spacetime quantum mechanics' (SQM), describing systems over a finite spacetime region. The formalism uses time translation operators ($\mathbf{e}^{i\epsilon P}$) and constructs the state $R$ from initial states $ho_0$.

**Key observables.** Spacetime states ($R$), pseudo-entropies ($S_\alpha(R)$), Wightman functions, and Out-of-Time-Order Correlators (OTOCs).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Finite time window $T = \epsilon N$, the time step $\epsilon$, and system/environment bipartitions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The initial formulation assumes a finite time window and discrete time slices, although extensions to continuum limits are discussed. The derivation of certain properties relies on specific mathematical decompositions (e.g., for pure states).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the relationship between standard QM and spacetime approaches; one shows spectral analysis of $R$ across bipartitions, while another plots OTOC eigenvalues versus time.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds by first defining the necessary mathematical machinery (Spacetime Hilbert space, action operators $\mathbf{S}$), then deriving how standard quantum evolution pictures (Schrödinger/Heisenberg) are recovered via partial tracing, and finally analyzing advanced properties like isospectrality and entanglement measures.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Recent efforts to formulate quantum mechanics in a way that treats space and time on a more equal footing have led to a large variety of spacetime-oriented approaches. In this work we present a detailed study of spacetime states, the objects that play the role of quantum states in the recently introduced framework of spacetime quantum mechanics, and show that the main proposals in the literature are different manifestations of the same underlying object. Path integrals, quantum states over time, pseudo-density matrices, the Page and Wootters mechanism, superdensity operators, and timelike-entanglement proposals all arise from spacetime states through particular evaluations, reduced information, linear maps, or quantum channels. This unification provides explicit mathematical representations of these formalisms, reveals relations among them, and clarifies the spacetime information each one captures. We also study the broader relevance of the spacetime-state point of view for Leggett-Garg inequalities, OTOCs, temporal tensor networks, fermionic systems, relativistic QFTs, quantum reference frames, and classical physics, together with additional insights and perspectives revealed by the common unifying framework.

</details>

**Summary.** This work theoretically examines how an ultrafast optical pulse induces dynamic changes in the quantum geometry of graphene's electronic states. By calculating the quantum metric and Berry curvature, the authors predict the emergence of measurable 'quantum geometric waves.' These waves serve as a spectroscopic signature confirming the transient formation of Floquet bands in the material.

**Why it may be interesting.** The connection drawn between time-dependent Floquet band structure, quantum geometric invariants (metric/curvature), and experimentally accessible quantities like the Fisher information makes it highly relevant for understanding non-equilibrium dynamics in condensed matter systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how a short, intense optical pulse dynamically alters the quantum geometry of Bloch states in graphene.

**Main result.** The quantum metric and Berry curvature exhibit wave-like features (quantum geometric waves) in momentum-time space, which are directly linked to the formation of Floquet bands induced by the driving field.

**Method.** Theoretical calculation using time-dependent Schrödinger equation solutions for a two-band model, quantifying geometry via the quantum geometric tensor and Fisher information matrix.

**Model / system.** Graphene monolayer modeled on a honeycomb lattice, driven by an external time-dependent vector potential (optical pump). The analysis is performed in the momentum-time manifold (k, t).

**Key observables.** Quantum metric tensor components ($g_{\mu
u}$), Berry curvature ($\Omega_{\mu
u}$), and Fisher information matrix ($I_{\mu
u}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Optical pulse parameters include $\hbar\omega_{	ext{pump}} = 950$ meV, pulse duration $	au = 18$ fs.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis neglects correlations and out-of-equilibrium many-body effects by solving the time-dependent Schrödinger equation in momentum space.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate ring-like features (quantum geometric waves) in the momentum profiles of $g_{xx}, g_{tt}, \Omega_{xy}$ at various times, and show similar patterns for the Fisher information components ($I_{xx}, I_{yy}, I_{tt}$).

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theory by defining the quantum geometric tensor in the $(\mathbf{k}, t)$ manifold, calculating its real part (metric) and imaginary part (Berry curvature), and introducing the measurable Fisher information matrix. It then applies this framework to graphene driven by an optical pulse.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that, under a short optical pulse, the quantum metric of Bloch states in the momentum-time (kx, ky , t) of graphene becomes dynamic and exhibits a wave-like behavior near Dirac points. This quantum metric wave reflects the Floquet-band structure caused by the pulse, as revealed by solving the time-dependent Schrödinger equation assuming that correlations and out-of-equilibrium effects can be ignored. The momentum and temporal components of the metric have very distinct time dependence that persists even after the pulse has passed. In addition, the pulse also generates a Berry curvature wave that is otherwise absent in static graphene. The time-dependent electron densities in conduction and valence bands also give arise to a Fisher information wave that constitutes part of the quantum metric wave, and is readily measurable by pump-probe experiments.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12606"></a>
### [Spontaneous symmetry breaking under Bose--Einstein condensation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12606v1)

**Authors:** V. I. Yukalov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12606v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12606_figures/2606.12606_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: The relations between the BEC occuring without external ﬁelds, BEC in the sense of the Bogolubov quasiaverages, gauge symmetry breaking, and condensate ﬂuctuations.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This survey clarifies complex issues surrounding Bose-Einstein condensation by showing that the physical reality of BEC necessitates spontaneous gauge symmetry breaking. Using quasiaverages and fluctuation analysis, the authors demonstrate that this symmetry breaking mechanism is crucial for ensuring system stability and avoiding unphysical 'grand canonical catastrophes' in the thermodynamic limit.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a deep theoretical clarification of the mathematical underpinnings of BEC, particularly how gauge symmetry breaking resolves apparent paradoxes regarding particle fluctuations in many-body quantum systems relevant to ultracold atoms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to clarify conceptual confusions surrounding Bose-Einstein condensation (BEC), spontaneous gauge symmetry breaking, and particle fluctuations in the context of quantum gas theory.

**Main result.** The occurrence of BEC is shown to be mathematically equivalent to the necessity of spontaneous gauge symmetry breaking, which stabilizes the system by ensuring that condensate fluctuations are thermodynamically normal.

**Method.** The analysis employs Bogoliubov's method using quasiaverages in the grand canonical ensemble and utilizes ergodic decomposition techniques to relate symmetric averages to broken-symmetry terms.

**Model / system.** The model is a spinless Bose system described by field operators, analyzed under conditions leading to BEC. The methodology involves introducing symmetry-breaking terms into the Hamiltonian for calculation.

**Key observables.** Condensate order parameter ($\langle \hat{a}_0 angle_{	ext{na}}$), condensate number variance ($	ext{var}(\hat{N}_0)$), relative density fluctuations ($	ext{var}(\hat{N})/N$), and the fluctuation index ($\zeta$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Thermodynamic limit ($V 	o \infty, N/V = 	ext{const}$), the symmetry-breaking parameter $
u$, and the temperature $T$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a stable potential for pair interactions and relies on the rigorous justification of substituting operators with non-operator quantities in the thermodynamic limit.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the relationship between BEC descriptions (standard vs. quasiaverages), gauge symmetry breaking, and the resulting condensate fluctuations.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by first defining the problem space involving multiple theoretical approaches to BEC. It then rigorously connects standard averages to Bogoliubov quasiaverages via symmetry breaking, demonstrating that this mechanism is required for stability and avoiding catastrophic fluctuation predictions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Due to high current interest to Bose-Einstein condensation, the related topics, such as spontaneous gauge symmetry breaking, ergodic decomposition, and particle fluctuations, are intensively discussed in literature. These discussions, unfortunately, involve quite a number of controversies and confusions. The goal of the present brief survey is to clarify some of these confusions, concentrating on the principal points, such as the relationship between the conditions of Bose-Einstein condensation, the Bogolubov method of quasiaverages, spontaneous gauge symmetry breaking, ergodic decomposition, the presumed existence of nonthermodynamic particle fluctuations leading to the so-called ``grand canonical catastrophe", and the requirements for system stability.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.12636"></a>
### [Quantum Stochastic Inflation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12636v1)

**Authors:** Robson Christie, Jaewoo Joo, Greg Kaplanek, Vincent Vennin, David Wands  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12636v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12636_figures/2606.12636_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Steps undertaken to evolve the homogeneous, coarse-grained mode of the field. We use the following terminology: incoming bulk denotes the homogeneous mode at N, incoming boundary shell denotes the homogeneous shell mode added between N and N + dN, redefined bulk denotes the updated homogeneous mode at N + dN, and decoupled mode denotes the collective mode traced over after the redefinition.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12636_figures/2606.12636_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Selective SSE branch. The same entangling bulk–boundary redefinition as in fig. 1 is followed by a rank-one measurement of the decoupled mode, rather than a partial trace. Conditioning on outcome r leaves the bulk in the pure state |ψc,r⟩.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12636_figures/2606.12636_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: Phase-space diagrams of the Gaussian stationary state derived explicitly in eq. (4.7). (a) The stationary purity γ∞of the coarse-grained state. (b) Field variance ∆ˆQ2. (c) Magnitude of the cross-covariance |∆ˆQ ˆP|. (d) Momentum variance ∆ˆP 2. The solid colour contours (isolines) map lines of constant magnitude across the two-dimensional parameter space, while the dashed white line denotes the critical mass threshold m = 1.5H. All three covariance elements share the identical logarithmic colour axis to accurately compare their relative divergence magnitudes. In the light-field regime, the variances rapidly diverge as the cutoff σ is tightened, resulting in an increasingly mixed...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work develops a fully quantum open system description of stochastic inflation by modeling the field as an open system coupled to newly entering modes. It derives the evolution via a GKLS master equation, showing that this quantum process mathematically reproduces the classical Fokker-Planck equations used in inflationary cosmology. The paper is significant for providing a fundamental quantum justification for the emergence of classical noise during cosmic inflation.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous quantum mechanical derivation connecting QFT on curved spacetime (inflation) directly to classical stochastic processes, which is a major goal in quantum gravity phenomenology.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper formulates stochastic inflation within a fully quantum open system framework, aiming to derive the classical stochastic dynamics from fundamental quantum field theory principles.

**Main result.** The evolution of the coarse-grained bulk density matrix is governed by a GKLS master equation, whose Wigner function representation matches the known Fokker-Planck equations for stochastic inflation in various regimes (e.g., Starobinsky's slow-roll limit).

**Method.** The authors use canonical coarse-graining techniques and model the system evolution as an open quantum process involving tracing out entangled boundary modes, leading to a Lindblad master equation.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a free scalar field undergoing inflation on a de Sitter background. The dynamics are analyzed by partitioning the field into a coarse-grained 'bulk' patch and incoming 'boundary' modes, treating the process as an open quantum system.

**Key observables.** Purity of the coarse-grained patch ($	ext{Tr}(ho^2)$), Wigner function $W(q, p)$, and the expectation values $\langle\hat{Q}angle, \langle\hat{P}angle$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mass regime ($m/H$), e-fold number ($N$), and the coupling strength of the boundary modes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations like leading order in the super-Hubble gradient expansion, Markovianity assumptions for the bath interaction, and the validity of the thin-shell approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the steps of mode evolution (incoming bulk $	o$ inclusion of boundary shell $	o$ redefinition $	o$ tracing out modes) and show phase space dynamics via Wigner functions or conditional state evolutions based on measurement type.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the open system framework using canonical coarse-graining, deriving the GKLS master equation for the reduced density matrix, establishing its equivalence to a Fokker-Planck equation in the Wigner representation, and finally analyzing specific physical limits (light vs. heavy fields) and measurement readouts.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We formulate stochastic inflation in an open quantum system framework. The field coarse-grained in a patch of fixed physical size, and the total momentum of that patch, form a canonical pair and act on a one-mode Fock space which we identify as the "bulk". At each time step, new comoving modes join the coarse-grained patch and the bulk has to be redefined. This redefinition produces an entangled mode that is traced over, yielding a non-unitary evolution equation for the bulk's density matrix. For a free test field in de Sitter, one obtains GKLS dynamics, generated by an effective Hamiltonian and a single non-Hermitian Lindblad operator, hence diffusion and Hubble friction originate from the same quantum channel. The Wigner-Weyl transform of the GKLS equation leads to a Fokker-Planck equation for the Wigner function, which matches the one that applies to the classical phase-space distribution of stochastic inflation. We also provide several schemes under which one can unravel the GKLS dynamics into stochastic Schrodinger equations when continuous measurements of the decoupled mode are performed, making contact with Langevin formulations of stochastic inflation. In the light-field regime, an additional overdamped reduction can be performed by integrating out the momentum variable in the Wigner distribution, leading to Starobinsky's slow-roll Fokker-Planck equation. In that regime, the purity of the patch is strongly suppressed. In contrast, for heavy fields, field diffusion is suppressed and the coarse-grained patch remains close to a pure underdamped oscillator, which prevents a classical stochastic treatment.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Scalar Quantum Fields: Theory Space and its Geometry](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12580v1)

**Authors:** Viola Gattus, Peter Millington  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12580v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

<img src="2606.12580_figures/2606.12580_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: An example of possible paths between initial and final field configurations Φi and Φ f . Classi- cally, the field follows a single trajectory: the shortest path. In the quantum regime, however, an infinite number of paths contribute. The sum over all such possible paths is known as the path integral.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12580_figures/2606.12580_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">

The manifold M can be constructed either by specifying all its points x or by drawing the tangent plane at each point x on its surface.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12580_figures/2606.12580_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">

Figure 3a displays a one-particle-reducible diagram, with the corresponding internal line cut indicated explicitly. Figure 3b shows the so-called box diagram, which provides an example of a one-particle-irreducible diagram that is nevertheless two- particle reducible.</figcaption>
</figure>

<img src="2606.12580_figures/2606.12580_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">

</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12580_figures/2606.12580_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5: Schematic of the affine coordinate frame of the D connection, spanned by ϕa and ∆ab. Chang- ing the RG scale induces a vector field in configuration space, which connects hypersurfaces associated with different RG scales. All points in each hypersurface have the same value of Kab, but different value of ϕa. Geodesics of the D connection are RG trajectories with dKab/dt = constant. In the affine coordi- nate frame of the D connection, spanned by Kab and Ja, geodesics are straight lines and pierce through hypersurfaces of constant RG time perpendicularly. For a specific example of this, see Fig. 3 of [20].</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This lecture explores Quantum Field Theory by treating the space of possible theories as a geometric manifold. It develops advanced techniques using 2PI effective actions and functional integrals, culminating in mapping Renormalization Group evolution onto geodesic flow within this abstract 'theory space.' This provides a deep mathematical framework for understanding how physical laws change with energy scale.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on fundamental QFT rather than condensed matter systems directly, the rigorous mathematical machinery developed—especially the use of information geometry to describe parameter dependence (RG flow)—provides powerful abstract tools applicable to understanding phase transitions or critical points in strongly correlated materials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central goal is to develop a geometric understanding of Quantum Field Theory (QFT) by interpreting the space of possible theories, known as 'theory space,' as a mathematical manifold.

**Main result.** The authors establish that observables like the effective action and correlation functions can be derived using advanced differential geometry tools (like connections and metrics on infinite-dimensional spaces), showing how physical parameters flow with energy scale.

**Method.** The analysis employs functional methods, including path integrals, generating functionals, and the 2PI effective action formalism. Geometrically, it utilizes concepts from Information Geometry to map QFT dynamics onto geodesic flows in theory space.

**Model / system.** The primary model is a general scalar quantum field ($\Phi_a$) described by an action functional $S[\Phi]$. The framework generalizes standard QFT techniques (like renormalization) into a geometric language applicable across different physical theories.

**Key observables.** The 1PI and 2PI effective actions ($\Gamma$), correlation functions $\langle \Phi(x_1) \dots \Phi(x_n) angle$, and the metric tensor $g_{\alphaeta}$ defining the geometry of theory space.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Model parameters embedded in the action coefficients ($Z_{ab}, V$'s), the energy scale ($\mu$ or RG time $t$), and the coupling constants $\lambda$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies on perturbative expansions in $\hbar$, assuming that observables remain well-defined (e.g., on-shell) even when intermediate functional definitions might lack strict covariance.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate paths between field configurations, the relationship between reducible and irreducible diagrams, and schematically show how geodesics in theory space correspond to Renormalization Group flow trajectories.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining QFT via actions and path integrals (Chunk 1) to introducing generating functionals and the 2PI effective action (Chunk 2). It then details perturbative calculations of quantum corrections ($\hbar$ expansion, Chunk 3), before culminating in a full geometric treatment using connections and metrics on theory space for RG flow analysis (Chunks 4-6).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Scalar fields provide perhaps the simplest playground in which to develop our understanding of quantum field theory. In this lecture, we consider what it means to write down a scalar quantum field theory and how we can give geometrical interpretations to the space of such theories: the theory space.

</details>

**Summary.** This work provides an exact analytical solution for the Ising model on a decorated square lattice to study magnetic reentrancy. By analyzing how multiple, competing exchange interactions influence the system's energy landscape, the authors demonstrate that the material can exhibit up to five distinct magnetic phase transitions as temperature changes. This is significant for understanding complex ordering phenomena in frustrated quantum magnets.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical statistical mechanics, the concept of sequential, multi-stage ordering transitions (reentrancy) due to competing interactions is highly relevant to understanding complex ground states in quantum many-body systems and frustrated magnets.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the fundamental possibility of multiple magnetic phase transitions, known as magnetic reentrancy, in spin systems subjected to competing exchange interactions.

**Main result.** The model demonstrates that up to five distinct magnetic phase transitions can occur under specific conditions, which are governed by the interplay between direct and decorating exchange interactions.

**Method.** The analysis relies on an exact solution for the Ising model derived via a decoration-iteration transformation, determining critical temperatures by solving algebraic equations related to the principal eigenvalue of the transfer matrix.

**Model / system.** The system is the Ising model defined on a decorated square lattice, incorporating direct exchange interactions ($J_x, J_y$) and decorating exchange interactions ($J_{dx}, J_{dy}$), allowing for arbitrary decoration multiplicities.

**Key observables.** Spontaneous magnetization ($M$), critical temperatures ($T_c$), and the identification of distinct magnetic ordering phases (e.g., F–F, AF–AF).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The relative strengths and signs of direct vs. decorating exchange interactions ($J$ vs $J_d$) are crucial; reentrancy occurs when $|J_d| > |J|$ under certain conditions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the Boltzmann constant is unity, and computational methods are used to rigorously find roots for critical temperatures, bypassing difficulties near low-temperature divergences.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate magnetic phase diagrams showing $T_c$ dependence on interaction parameters ($J_d$, $J_{dx}$), demonstrating transitions ranging from one up to five distinct points under isotropic and anisotropic conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the model by first establishing the mathematical framework using exact solutions, then deriving parameter conditions for competing interactions, and finally mapping out complex magnetic phase diagrams showing the emergence of multiple transition points based on anisotropy.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In our work, based on an exact solution of the Ising model on a decorated square lattice with an arbitrary number of decorating spins, we have demonstrated the fundamental possibility of describing the phenomenon of multiple magnetic phase transitions (magnetic reentrancy) in the regime of competing exchange interactions. We analyzed the magnetic behavior of the system and established the conditions for the occurrence of magnetic reentrancy. We have determined the relationships of the model parameter under which the formation of one, three, and even five magnetic phase transitions is possible, which is confirmed by a complicated magnetic phase diagram. We have also proposed a unique method for finding critical temperatures that allows for the precise determination of their number and values, including those at extremely low temperatures.

</details>

**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops an advanced theoretical framework using Large Deviation Theory to analyze the statistical fluctuations of weak wave turbulence. It derives simplified principles that characterize both typical and rare spectral dynamics by treating the system's evolution as a stochastic process governed by conserved quantities like energy and action. This provides a general, replicable method for studying complex non-equilibrium phenomena in dispersive media.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous application of large deviation theory to non-equilibrium statistical mechanics problems like wave turbulence provides powerful tools for understanding rare events and fluctuations far from equilibrium, which is a major theme in modern AMO/open quantum systems research.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a simplified large deviation principle for wave turbulence to study the typical and rare fluctuations of the spectrum dynamics, going beyond mean evolution captured by kinetic equations.

**Main result.** The authors derive a new large deviation principle that fully characterizes spectral fluctuations and provide a general method to analyze wave turbulence under fixed-flux boundary conditions. They also show this theory can explain instabilities in inhomogeneous models like MMT.

**Method.** The work heavily relies on Large Deviation Theory (LDT), extending concepts from Macroscopic Fluctuation Theory, applied to the stochastic dynamics of the wave spectrum.

**Model / system.** The system is weak wave turbulence, modeled by dispersive waves with weakly nonlinear interactions. The analysis focuses on deriving rate functions and fluctuation equations for the wave spectrum $\hat{n}(\xi, t)$ in isotropic local limits.

**Key observables.** Fluctuations of the wave spectrum ($	ext{P}\{\dots\}$), long-range correlations, two-point correlation functions, and conserved quantities (mass and energy).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The small large deviation parameter $\epsilon$, and the inertial range between forcing and dissipation scales.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Key assumptions include assuming isotropy and locality for simplification, and utilizing the Random Phase (RP) hypothesis instead of the RPA to analyze multi-point correlations.

**Figures summary.** The notes mention figures showing stationary spectra computation, convergence of wave action/energy currents, and the structure of the discretized Lyapunov equation.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the general LDP framework for fluctuations, simplifying it via isotropy and locality to yield a stochastic PDE. It then applies this formalism to analyze out-of-equilibrium fluxes using two conserved quantities (Action and Energy), culminating in generalizations to inhomogeneous systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Wave turbulence describes the statistical dynamics of dispersive waves with weakly nonlinear interactions. While the classical kinetic equation captures the mean evolution of the wave spectrum, the study of its fluctuations due to finite-size effects and intermittency requires a probabilistic framework for space-time trajectories of the spectrum dynamics. Following the previous large deviation theories for wave turbulence, we develop a simplification meant for qualitative and numerical predictions of measurable quantities. We derive a new large deviation principle in the case of local wave interactions. It fully characterizes typical and rare fluctuations of the spectrum. In a joint article, we obtain a theory which is a generalised form of Macroscopic Fluctuation Theory, but with 2 conserved quantities (mass and energy). In this paper, we use it to analyse the structure of the equation for Gaussian fluctuations around out-of-equilibrium spectra. In addition to the usual equilibrium contribution, we obtain long-range correlations, which can be decomposed into 3 contributions: one is driven by the flux in the bulk and another is driven by the forcing and its possible fluctuations. In addition, these contributions are computed for the first time with a method adapted to boundary conditions where only the fluxes are fixed. The results provide a general, replicable method for analyzing wave turbulence in more complex settings. Finally, the generalization of this theory to the inhomogeneous wave turbulence provides a possible explanation to the instability of the Kolmogorov-Zakharov spectra in some 1D inhomogeneous models with 4-wave interactions such as the Majda-McLaughlin-Tabak. This work opens the discussion regarding universal and non universal properties in two-point correlation functions. This opens new range of study on the phenomena of intermittency which is partially developed here.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** The paper proposes using Quantum-Informed Machine Learning (QIML) to improve forecasts of chaotic systems by encoding system statistics into a compact quantum prior. It proves a significant computational speedup by showing that joint Bell measurements on two copies can efficiently read out complex correlations, an advantage demonstrated in both fluid dynamics and weather forecasting simulations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the interface between quantum computation and classical scientific modeling, providing a concrete, near-term pathway to demonstrating quantum advantage in complex physical simulations before fault-tolerant hardware is available.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a theoretically grounded, practical quantum advantage mechanism within Quantum-Informed Machine Learning (QIML) for predicting complex chaotic dynamical systems.

**Main result.** The authors prove a two-stage advantage: using superposition/entanglement to compactly store non-factorizable correlations in a Q-Prior, and achieving an exponentially faster read-out of Pauli statistics via joint Bell measurements compared to classical single-copy protocols.

**Method.** They introduce k-indexed higher-order quantum statistical priors (Q-Priors) that encode the invariant measure's marginals. This is coupled with a classical Koopman rollout, constrained by the quantum prior information.

**Model / system.** The framework applies to chaotic systems like turbulent channel flow and medium-range weather forecasting using ERA5 data. The core computational element involves encoding system statistics into a quantum state on $n_q$ qubits.

**Key observables.** Velocity-direction coherence (non-diagonal correlator in turbulence); Anomaly Correlation Coefficient (ACC) improvement of 10-39% in weather forecasting; provable separation in measurement copy complexity ($M_Q$ vs $M_C$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of qubits ($n_q=kq$), correlation order ($k$), and the required fidelity ($\eta, \delta$) for quantum advantage demonstration.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The existence of a well-defined invariant measure for the chaotic system; the ability to perform two-copy Bell measurements on superconducting hardware (IQM).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the comparison between quantum and classical copy costs, showing the exponential separation. One figure compares QIML results across different baselines for turbulent flow, highlighting directional coherence recovery.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the theoretical foundation by defining the two-stage advantage (Representation $ightarrow$ Extraction). It then validates this mechanism through two distinct case studies: turbulence (demonstrating non-diagonal correlation read-out) and ERA5 weather forecasting (improving long-term prediction skill via diagonal priors).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop theoretical foundations for a practical quantum-advantage mechanism in quantum-informed machine learning for chaotic dynamical systems. A family of k-indexed higher-order quantum statistical priors (Q-Priors) hosts the k-point marginal of the invariant measure on n_q = kq qubits, extending the single-site construction of prior work. We prove a two-stage advantage. In the representation stage, superposition and entanglement compactly store non-factorisable spatial correlations of the invariant measure on n_q qubits. In the extraction stage, joint Bell measurements on two copies estimate any post hoc Pauli functional with a copy-pair count independent of n_q, whereas any adaptive single-copy protocol for the corresponding full-Pauli read-out requires Omega(2^(n_q)) copies; this is a provable quantum-classical separation in copy-measurement complexity. The two-copy read-out is realised in simulation and on IQM superconducting processors. Two case studies instantiate the mechanism in workflows of independent scientific value: a turbulent channel-flow study in which the two-copy read-out yields a named non-diagonal correlator of the invariant measure (the velocity-direction coherence), and a medium-range weather forecasting workflow on the European Centre for Medium-Range Weather Forecasts ERA5 reanalysis in which the diagonal k <= 2 Q-Prior steers a Koopman rollout, improves anomaly-correlation skill by 10-39% across 48-240 h lead times, and reduces the long-horizon collapse of rollouts onto a static mean field. The two conditions of our practical-advantage definition are met at complementary levels, identifying a candidate route to practical quantum advantage before fault-tolerant hardware.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces the Noncommutative Skin Effect (NCSE), showing that spatial noncommutativity alone can induce boundary localization typically associated with gain/loss terms. By analyzing lattice models and deriving effective 1D Hamiltonians, it establishes rigorous criteria—particularly based on potential symmetry—for the existence of this effect.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a fundamental mechanism linking geometric properties of spacetime (noncommutativity) directly to observable quantum phenomena like topological edge states, which is highly relevant for designing synthetic dimensions in AMO platforms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To demonstrate that spatial noncommutativity can induce the Non-Hermitian Skin Effect (NHSE) without requiring an independent gain/loss term along the skin direction.

**Main result.** The phenomenon, termed the noncommutative skin effect (NCSE), is characterized by a skin length inversely proportional to the noncommutativity parameter ($\kappa_
u \propto 	heta$).

**Method.** The analysis uses mathematical transformations (like the Bopp shift) and asymptotic expansions (Taylor/adiabatic approximations) on effective 1D Hamiltonians derived from the 2D noncommutative lattice model.

**Model / system.** The system is a quantum mechanical model on a two-dimensional space where spatial coordinates do not commute, $[\hat{x}, \hat{y}] = i	heta$. The Hamiltonian involves kinetic terms and an imaginary potential $V(\hat{y})$.

**Key observables.** Inverse skin length ($\kappa_
u$), spectral non-reciprocity ($\epsilon_
u(k) 
eq \epsilon_
u(-k)$), and the collective reversal of eigenstate direction at geometric 'flip points'.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Noncommutativity parameter ($	heta$), lattice size ($L_x, L_y$), and potential strength/geometry.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations such as the adiabatic limit and Taylor truncation to derive solvable effective 1D equations from the full 2D problem.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the 'Sliding-window mechanism' showing momentum shearing into coordinate space, and numerical verification of exponential envelope localization ($\propto e^{	heta m}$) for various potentials.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the general noncommutative framework, deriving effective 1D models (Hatano-Nelson type), proving existence criteria based on potential symmetry, and finally applying these results to specific lattice potentials (quadratic, sine/cosine).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In all known schemes for the non-Hermitian skin effect, the non-Hermitian ingredient that drives the skin localization, whether asymmetric hopping or gain and loss, is invariably introduced by hand as an independent model parameter along the skin direction. Here we show that when two spatial coordinates do not commute, the skin effect can break free of this paradigm: a gain-loss potential applied along one coordinate automatically generates non-reciprocity along the other through the coordinate noncommutativity, driving all eigenstates to pile up exponentially at a boundary. We term this phenomenon the noncommutative skin effect. The inverse skin length is proportional to the noncommutativity parameter and is given by an analytic formula, exact in the thermodynamic limit and verified by exact diagonalization of lattice models; the reflection symmetry of the imaginary potential furnishes an exact criterion for the presence or absence of the effect, valid rigorously for finite-size systems. For a sinusoidal imaginary potential, the skin direction of all eigenstates flips collectively at parameter points fixed purely by geometry. Because the flip point is independent of the potential strength, the reversal constitutes a zero-crossing measurement scheme intrinsically robust against systematic errors, from which the noncommutativity parameter can be extracted directly. The qualitative transition of the eigenstates from uniform to exponentially localized renders the effect a nonperturbative probe of spatial noncommutativity, and the Peierls-phase structure of its lattice model is in principle accessible to cold-atom synthetic dimensions, photonic resonators, and topolectrical circuits.

</details>

**Summary.** This review provides a comprehensive theoretical and experimental overview of exotic spin-dependent interactions beyond the Standard Model. It focuses particularly on couplings mediated by light particles like ALPs and effects arising from Lorentz/CPT violation. The paper synthesizes knowledge across multiple detection platforms, guiding future searches for physics beyond our current understanding.

**Why it may be interesting.** While not strictly focused on quantum information or cold atoms as primary subjects, the paper heavily relies on advanced quantum sensing techniques (atomic magnetometers, spin precession) to probe fundamental symmetries and forces, making it highly relevant to AMO physics frontiers.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates exotic spin-dependent interactions beyond the Standard Model (SM) to address fundamental issues like the strong CP problem and the nature of dark matter.

**Main result.** It systematically reviews theoretical frameworks for these novel couplings, providing a roadmap for experimentalists to constrain parameters related to light mediators like Axion-Like Particles (ALPs).

**Method.** The approach combines deriving theoretical Lagrangians for various spin-dependent interactions with reviewing constraints derived from high-precision measurements on atomic and nuclear spins.

**Model / system.** The focus is on exotic couplings mediated by light particles (e.g., ALPs) or arising from extensions to General Relativity, such as those involving spacetime torsion or Lorentz/CPT violation. Experimental platforms include comagnetometers, atomic magnetometers, and molecular hydrogen systems.

**Key observables.** Spin precession rates, frequency shifts in comagnetometers, electric dipole moments (EDMs), and coupling constants for exotic forces ($\chi, d_e$, etc.).

**Important parameters / regimes.** ALP masses ($m_a$), interaction strengths ($f_a$), SME coefficients ($	ilde{b}_w^J, 	ilde{d}_w^J$), and characteristic energy/length scales (mesoscopic to macroscopic).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on low-energy approximations for light mediators and systematic classification schemes (e.g., based on spin order and gradient order) to manage the complexity of BSM physics.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically illustrate novel interaction couplings, detection strategies involving ordinary matter interacting with a dark sector, and Feynman diagrams showing ALP mediation between fermions.

**Paper structure.** The review progresses from outlining theoretical foundations for exotic spin-dependent interactions (ALPs, SME) to systematically classifying these interactions based on mathematical structure. It then details the experimental efforts and constraints derived from various precision measurements across different physical systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

New interactions mediated by novel particles propose solutions to several important questions in modern physics. Axions serve as examples of such particles; they are lightweight and interact weakly with ordinary matter. This category of particles, including those similar to axions-termed Axion-Like Particles (ALPs)-arises from diverse theoretical frameworks, such as the Peccei-Quinn mechanism addressing the strong CP problem, string theory, and spontaneous supersymmetry breaking. Given their light mass and weak coupling, ALPs are also possible candidates for cold dark matter. Introducing these new interactions mediated by novel particles not only tackles several challenges in modern physics but also raises a crucial question: Are there undiscovered interactions beyond the Standard Model? Many of the interactions predicted by these theories are spin-dependent, which is the primary focus of this review. In this review, we first outline the theoretical foundations for investigating exotic spin-dependent interactions, highlighting their importance in various models beyond the Standard Model. We examine the potential roles of new lightweight particles in mediating these interactions, which may enhance our understanding of dark matter. Relevant formulas derived from theoretical models are included to support experimental investigations. Following this theoretical framework, we conduct a detailed review of recent experimental efforts to detect these exotic interactions. A systematic review of current constraints on these interactions is presented, along with an assessment of various detection approaches.

</details>

### [Overview of the Theory of Extremely Correlated Fermi Liquids](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13541v1)

**Summary.** This paper reviews the Extremely Correlated Fermi Liquid (ECFL) theory as a comprehensive framework for analyzing highly correlated electron systems like cuprate superconductors. It details how this theory handles the $t$-$J$ model to predict key transport and spectral properties, such as density-dependent resistivity and vanishing quasiparticle weight. The work provides quantitative comparisons between these theoretical predictions and extensive experimental data from single-layer high-$T_c$ materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous theoretical development of ECFL theory provides a sophisticated many-body approach that attempts to bridge the gap between simple models ($t$-$J$) and complex experimental observations in strongly correlated materials, offering insights into emergent quantum behavior beyond standard Fermi liquid descriptions.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses understanding the physics of extremely strongly interacting electrons, particularly relevant for interpreting high-temperature superconductivity in cuprates.

**Main result.** The Extremely Correlated Fermi Liquid (ECFL) theory successfully predicts key features like a vanishing quasiparticle weight near half-filling and provides consistent theoretical frameworks for analyzing resistivity and spectral functions across various doping levels.

**Method.** The core method involves developing the ECFL framework by systematically expanding in a parameter $\lambda$ within the $t$-$J$ model Hamiltonian, ensuring compliance with the Luttinger-Ward theorem.

**Model / system.** The primary system is the single-layer High-$T_c$ cuprate superconductor, modeled using the $t$-$J$ Hamiltonian derived from the Hubbard model in the strong correlation limit ($U \gg W$).

**Key observables.** Resistivity $ho(T)$, quasiparticle weight ($Z$), spectral function $A(\mathbf{k}, \omega)$, and Hall conductivity ($\sigma_{xy}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hole doping ($\delta$), temperature ($T$), hopping ratios ($t'/t$), and the correlation strength (approaching Mott limit).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory relies on systematic approximations in powers of $\lambda$ and assumes that enforcing Luttinger-Ward compliance provides the correct physical solution class.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare theoretical predictions for resistivity ($ho(T)$) and ARPES spectral features (kinks, peak temperature dependence) against experimental data from various cuprate families (LSCO, Bi2212, etc.).

**Paper structure.** The paper reviews the ECFL theory framework by first establishing its connection to the $t$-$J$ model, then detailing the mathematical procedure involving path integrals and self-energies ($\Psi, \Phi'$), followed by applying this formalism to calculate transport (resistivity, Hall response) and spectral properties.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Extremely Correlated Fermi Liquids (ECFL) theory is reviewed as a framework for understanding the $t$-$J$ model in metallic systems close to the Mott insulating limit. This overview presents the underlying ideas and the resulting equations in a form accessible to nonexperts. We compare theoretical results with all available resistivity data for single-layer High-T$_{c}$ systems, and with some spectral data. The highlighted results include a density dependent quasilinear T-dependence in resistivity, an unusually small quasiparticle weight, and distinct low-temperature emergent scales that dominate transport, thermodynamics and spectral properties of single-layer High T$_c$ systems. Suggestions are made for further experiments to probe the physics of these challenging quantum many-body systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.13667"></a>
### [Semi-Device-Independent Certification for Nonlocality without Entanglement](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13667v1)

**Authors:** Hanwool Lee, Joonwoo Bae  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13667v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13667_figures/2606.13667_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. The scenario of comparing the capabilities of measurements in GLOBAL and SEP is shown. Alice and Bob prepare product states by LOCC, such as antiparallel states in Eq. (3), and send them to a device that realizes a measurement in GLOBAL or SEP. Specifically, two parties apply a measurement to maximize a confidence, see Eq. (1), in which the strategy is to rely only on detected events. A gap between measurements in GLOBAL and SEP exists if an optimal measurement is GLOBAL but not SEP. The gap can also be certified given the outcome rates in Eq.(7).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.13667_figures/2606.13667_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2. Given an outcome rate ηx, the certifiable MC for antiparallel states in Eq. (3) is shown. In a noiseless state preparation, the certifiable MC with SEP is 3/4. For an outcome rate ηx &lt; 1/3, NLWE can be certified if ˆCx &gt; 3/4 (region I and II). For an outcome rate ηx ≤1/5, the gap ∆x is maximal (region I). When outcomes are so frequent ηx ≥1/3, NLWE cannot be certified (region III).</figcaption>
