**Summary.** This work systematically studies how controlling the microstructure of superconducting Sn/Pb composites via roll bonding influences magneto-thermal switching. The key finding is that nonvolatile MTS requires micro-scale inclusions capable of trapping magnetic flux. This provides a design guideline for engineering superconductors with enhanced thermal management properties.

**Why it may be interesting.** While primarily condensed matter experimental physics, the study touches upon phase transitions and magnetic field effects in superconductors, which are foundational topics related to quantum many-body systems and superconducting qubits.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how controlling the microstructure of superconducting composites affects the nonvolatile nature of magneto-thermal switching (MTS).

**Main result.** Nonvolatile MTS is found to appear systematically as the microstructural refinement increases, confirming that magnetic flux trapping by inclusions comparable to or smaller than the magnetic vortex is essential for this effect.

**Method.** The researchers used accumulative roll bonding (ARB) to create Sn/Pb multilayered composites with controlled microstructure, followed by precise measurements of thermal conductivity ($\kappa$) under varying magnetic fields and angles.

**Model / system.** The physical system is superconducting Sn/Pb multilayered composites fabricated via ARB. The microstructural scale (layer thickness) can be systematically tuned by the number of rolling repetitions ($n$).

**Key observables.** Magneto-thermal switching ratio (MTSR), remanent magnetization ($4\pi M_r$), and thermal conductivity ($\kappa$) dependence on magnetic field ($H$) and angle ($	heta$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Repetition number of roll bonding ($n$), which controls the average layer thickness; temperature ($T$); applied magnetic field ($H$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The nonvolatile MTS is directly linked to the ability of micro-scale inclusions to trap magnetic flux.

**Figures summary.** Figures show SEM-EDX maps confirming microstructure refinement with increasing $n$; $\kappa$ vs. $H$ curves demonstrating hysteresis; and plots showing that both MTSR and remanent magnetization increase monotonically with $n$.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically varies the microstructural scale using ARB (varying $n$) to observe the emergence of nonvolatile MTS, confirming a direct correlation between microstructure refinement, flux trapping, and the switching behavior.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Thermal conductivity in a conductor changes by the application of an external magnetic field, which functions as a magneto-thermal switch. For superconductors, a large magneto-thermal switching can occur through a superconducting-to-normal conducting phase transition due to the change in the electron contribution in thermal conductivity. Arima et al. recently reported a nonvolatile nature of the magneto-thermal switching for superconducting solders, which consist of phase-separated Sn and Pb domains. Although they clarified that magnetic flux trapping is required to induce the nonvolatile magneto-thermal switching, a rule for such material design is still unclear. Here, we investigate the microstructure dependence of magneto-thermal switching in superconducting Sn/Pb multilayered composites, which are created by an accumulative roll bonding method. The thickness of each layer, that is the scale of microstructure, can be systematically controlled by the repetition number of roll bonding while the whole sample size and average composition are unchanged. We find that, as the formation of micro-scaled Sn domains proceeds by increasing the repetition number, a nonvolatility in the magneto-thermal conductivity gradually appears in correlation with the remanent magnetization. This study directly confirms that the inclusions with a size comparable to or less than the magnetic vortex in superconducting matrix is essential for magnetic flux trapping, enabling the nonvolatile magneto-thermal switching in superconducting composites.

</details>

### [Manipulation of the gyrotropic mode frequency and band structure in an FM / AFM disk via vortex imprinting](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13181v1)

**Authors:** E. V. Skorokhodov, E. A. Karashtin, I. A. Fedotov, I. Yu. Pashen'kin, M. V. Sapozhnikov  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13181v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** This work experimentally and theoretically investigates how coupling a Ferromagnetic layer to an Antiferromagnetic layer via vortex imprinting modifies the system's fundamental magnetic resonance frequency. Researchers report a significant frequency shift and observe a predicted double-frequency mode when applying an external field, confirming strong nonlinear dynamics governed by interfacial exchange coupling.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on magnetism, the study of highly nonlinear dynamics, symmetry breaking (noncentrosymmetry), and resonant modes in coupled magnetic systems shares conceptual overlap with understanding collective excitations in quantum many-body or open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the manipulation of the magnetic gyrotropic mode frequency and band structure within a Ferromagnetic (FM) / Antiferromagnetic (AFM) disk system.

**Main result.** The coupling between FM and AFM layers via vortex imprinting causes a significant shift in the gyrotropic mode frequency, and the application of an in-plane field reveals a predicted double-frequency resonance due to nonlinear dynamics.

**Method.** The research combines experimental measurements using Magnetic Resonance Force Microscopy (MRFM) with theoretical modeling using micromagnetic simulations.

**Model / system.** The physical system is an FM/AFM bilayer disk heterostructure, such as NiFe/IrMn. The AFM layer's vortex state is imprinted into the material via controlled thermal annealing processes.

**Key observables.** Gyrotropic mode frequency ($\omega_{	ext{exch}}$), resonance spectra (comparing single FM vs. FM/AFM disks), and the dependence of these frequencies on an applied in-plane magnetic field ($H$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The frequency shift is proportional to exchange coupling terms, and the system exhibits strong nonlinearity when subjected to external fields.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the observed frequency shifts and nonlinearities are governed by the surface exchange interaction between the FM and AFM vortices in a noncentrosymmetric environment.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare MRFM spectra showing the large frequency shift (single vs. bilayer) and demonstrate the emergence of a distinct double-frequency peak when an external magnetic field is applied to the coupled system.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the physical setup and characterization methods (MOKE, MRFM), presenting experimental measurements showing the frequency shift and nonlinear response, and then supporting these findings with theoretical micromagnetic calculations that predict the double-frequency mode.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We experimentally investigate the magnetic gyrotropic mode in a system of vortex ferromagnetic (FM) nanooscillator exchange coupled to an antiferromagnetic (AFM) layer. The micron-sized disks formed from the Ni80Fe20(12 nm) / Ir80Mn20 (5 nm) FM/AFM heterostructure are prepared so that the vortex magnetic state is imprinted into the AFM layer. We apply a magnetic resonance force microscopy (MRFM) method to locally study magnetic oscillations in single FM/AFM disks. We show that the gyrotropic mode frequency is significantly (approximately 4 times) shifted to the high frequency compared to similar structure consisting of a single ferromagnetic disk. Upon applying an in-plane magnetic field, we observe a strong peak at a double frequency which was previously predicted in theory. This is governed by the fact that vortex dynamics is strongly nonlinear in a noncentrosymmetric system under investigation.

</details>

### [Role of quantum confinement in semiconductor-superconductor core-shell nanowires](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12715v1)

**Authors:** Tudor Gabriel Dumitru, Anna Sitek, Gunnar Thorgilsson, Sigurdur I. Erlingsson, Tudor Dan Stanescu, Andrei Manolescu

**Summary.** This theoretical study models the induced superconductivity in semiconductor nanowires coated by a superconductor shell. By comparing full and half-shell geometries, the authors show that while both can exhibit superconducting behavior, the gap size is strongly dependent on structural details like partial coverage. The work provides critical insights into how quantum confinement dictates the stability of tubular supercurrents.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed analysis of proximity effects in low-dimensional hybrid systems using BdG theory is highly relevant to understanding superconducting quantum circuits and topological materials, bridging concepts from condensed matter theory with potential device applications.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The work investigates how quantum confinement and superconducting coupling affect induced superconductivity in semiconductor-superconductor core-shell nanowires.

**Main result.** Both full-shell and half-shell geometries can support tubular superconducting states, but the induced gap size is highly sensitive to the degree of superconducting coverage (half vs. full) and the chemical potential ($\mu$).

**Method.** The study employs microscopic model calculations using an effective Bogoliubov-de Gennes (BdG) Hamiltonian derived from Green's functions.

**Model / system.** The system is a core-shell nanowire heterostructure, where the semiconductor core is coupled to a metallic superconductor shell (either full or half coverage). The model incorporates electrostatic band-bending via a rectangular quantum well potential and superconducting proximity effects via an interface self-energy.

**Key observables.** Induced superconducting gap ($\Delta_{	ext{ind}}$), electron/hole character of eigenstates, supercurrent oscillations exhibiting Little-Parks periodicity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Superconducting gap ($\Delta$), coupling strength ($\gamma$), chemical potential ($\mu$), and the geometry (full vs. half shell).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The complex electrostatic potential is approximated by a thin rectangular quantum well, and the superconducting contribution is modeled via an interface self-energy term in the low-energy limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the cross-section of the nanowires (full/half shell), the Density of States ($ho(E)$) showing gap formation, energy spectra from the BdG Hamiltonian for different geometries and $\mu$, and spatial profiles of electron/hole components.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing the physical model using an effective Hamiltonian, calculating transverse modes, determining the superconducting properties via the self-energy, and finally analyzing how geometry (full vs. half shell) and external parameters ($\mu$, barrier height) modulate the induced gap and tubular superconductivity.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

This work is motivated by the experimentally observed coherence of the supercurrent in semiconductor nanowires covered by a half-shell metallic superconductor, which leads to flux dependent supercurrent oscillations with period h/2e, as expected for a tubular superconductor, i.e. Little-Parks oscillations. We perform microscopic model calculations and compare the results for full and half metallic shells. We use an effective Hamiltonian derived from the Green's function of the proximitized semiconductor nanowire, where the presence of the superconductor is represented by a self energy. Furthermore, we incorporate the electrostatic band-bending at the metal-semiconductor interface as a rectangular narrow quantum well on the semiconductor side. The properties of the eigenstates of the effective Hamiltonian are determined by the spatial profile of the corresponding transverse modes in the normal state. For half-shell wires, transverse modes with high-enough energy expand outside the interface quantum well and generate eigenstates with mixed electron-hole character that surround the entire circumference of the nanowire, similar to eigenstates of the full-shell system. We identify these states as being responsible for the observed Little-Parks effect.

</details>

### [Phonon polariton confinement in isotopically pure MOVPE-grown BN triangles](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13483v1)

**Authors:** Maximilian Scharpey, Oskar Schröer, Daniel Wigger, Lena Miler, Jakub Iwański, Andrzej Wysmołek, Johannes Binder, Iris Niehues

**Summary.** The research investigates phonon polaritons in epitaxial boron nitride (BN) films, moving from bulk characterization to engineered microstructures. By combining s-SNOM and nano-FTIR measurements on isotopically pure ${}^{10}	ext{BN}$ triangles, the authors successfully demonstrated highly confined polariton modes. This work paves the way for building scalable, high-quality nanophotonic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it pushes the boundaries of solid-state photonics by creating tunable, all-epitaxial polariton resonators, which are crucial components for quantum light sources and detectors.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to demonstrate efficient phonon-polariton propagation and confinement within large-area, epitaxial boron nitride (BN) structures for advanced nanophotonics applications.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated high-quality polariton propagation in MOVPE-grown natural BN and achieved clear evidence of mode confinement using isotopically pure ${}^{10}	ext{BN}$ triangular islands.

**Method.** Measurements combine scattering-type s-SNOM and nano-FTIR spectroscopy, analyzed using complex near-field optical signals and fitting to theoretical interference models.

**Model / system.** The system involves BN thin films grown by MOVPE, specifically utilizing isotopically pure ${}^{10}	ext{BN}$ triangular islands on a natural BN layer. The physics relies on the resonant hybridization of light and lattice vibrations (phonon polaritons).

**Key observables.** Phonon polariton dispersion relation ($\omega$ vs. $q$), complex near-field amplitude and phase maps, and spectral features from s-SNOM/nano-FTIR.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Wavenumbers like $	ext{TO}$ and $	ext{LO}$ modes (e.g., $1354 	ext{ cm}^{-1}$, $1608 	ext{ cm}^{-1}$), island size, and epitaxial growth quality.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes straight wavefronts near edges for initial measurements, and the comparison between s-SNOM and nano-FTIR requires complex geometric alignment algorithms.

**Figures summary.** Figures show s-SNOM characterization of MOVPE-grown BN (including topography, amplitude/phase maps), schematics of ${}^{10}	ext{BN}$ island structures, and plots comparing measured vs. simulated phonon polariton dispersion relations.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from characterizing high-quality bulk-like propagation in natural BN to demonstrating controlled confinement within engineered micro-triangular islands using advanced near-field optical techniques.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Phonon polaritons, quasiparticles formed by the resonant hybridization of light and lattice vibrations, exhibit unique properties like the possibility of hyperbolic dispersions. In this context, exfoliated hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) has emerged as a promising material for phonon polariton-based research. However, to advance toward practical applications, it is essential to demonstrate efficient phonon-polariton propagation and confinement in large-area epitaxial BN. To address this topic, we use metalorganic vapor phase epitaxy (MOVPE)-grown BN and investigate the phonon polariton properties using scattering-type scanning near-field optical microscopy (s-SNOM) and nanoscale Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (nano-FTIR). We report remarkably long phonon polariton propagation lengths, indicating the high crystalline quality of the BN layer. By using epitaxially grown isotopically pure triangular islands, we further demonstrate efficient phonon-polariton confinement with mode patterns tunable by the incident light wavelength. Our results pave the way for implementing all-epitaxial, microscale, high-quality polariton resonators for nanophotonics and quantum optics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.12598"></a>
### [Multiband Superconductivity and Charge Density Wave in HfxZr1-xTe3 single crystals](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12598v1)

**Authors:** F. F. G. Nogueira, M. S. da Luz, M. S. Torikachvili, L. M. Ishikura, Ebrahim Dib, A. J. S. Machado

The inset displays the normalized resistivity ρ(T)/ρ(5 K) in the low-temperature region (2–5 K), highlighting the superconducting transitions for both current directions; (b) Temperature dependence of the magnetic susceptibility in applied magnetic fields of 10 Oe (black dots), and 20 Oe (red dots). The divergence between the ZFC (zero-field- cooled) and FC (field-cooled) curves yields a T𝑐 ≈ 3.3 K for the 10 Oe applied magnetic field H // b-axis.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12598_figures/2606.12598_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4. Temperature dependence of the normalized resistivity ρ(T)/ρ(4 K) for Hf₀.₀₂Zr₀.₉₈Te₃ in magnetic fields μ₀H = 0–0.3 T for (a) I // a and (b) I // b. The superconducting transition is progressively suppressed with increasing field. A small difference of the data for the two orientations of the field can be attributed to slight difference in the broadening. Insets show ρ(T) from 1.8 to 300 K. The upper critical field μ₀Hc2 was determined using a 50% criterion for the resistive transition.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.12598_figures/2606.12598_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5. (a) Isothermal magnetization curves M vs µ0H for Hf0.02Zr0.98Te3 in the 1.9 - 2.8 K range. The linearity at low fields is disrupted by vortex penetration at µ0Hc1; (b) Temperature dependence µ0Hc1 extracted from the magnetization data and fit within a single-band model. The extrapolated zero-temperature value obtained from the fit is μ₀Hc1(0) ≈ 0.22 mT.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work reports on the interplay between Charge Density Wave order and superconductivity in Hf-doped ZrTe3 single crystals. By applying chemical doping or hydrostatic pressure, the researchers demonstrate that these external parameters can tune the delicate balance between the two competing ground states. The findings confirm a multiband superconducting nature alongside strong electronic anisotropy.

**Why it may be interesting.** The clear demonstration of competing orders (CDW vs. SC) driven by subtle structural/electronic perturbations is a canonical problem in strongly correlated electron systems, providing experimental validation for theoretical models of electronic instabilities.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the competition and coexistence between Charge Density Wave (CDW) order and superconductivity (SC) in layered van der Waals materials.

**Main result.** Hf doping or hydrostatic pressure can fine-tune this balance, showing that lattice compression reinforces the 1D CDW instability while suppressing the bulk superconducting state.

**Method.** The research employs a combination of transport measurements (resistivity, Hall effect, Seebeck coefficient) and thermodynamic characterization (specific heat, magnetic susceptibility).

**Model / system.** The physical system is HfxZr1-xTe3 single crystals, which are quasi-one-dimensional layered materials. The analysis involves fitting superconducting critical fields to two-band models.

**Key observables.** Superconducting transition temperature (Tc), CDW transition temperature (TCDW), upper critical field Hc2(T), and electronic anisotropy derived from Hall effect measurements.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hf doping concentration (x=0.02, 0.13) and hydrostatic pressure up to 2 GPa are key tuning parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the observed phenomena can be explained by a competition between CDW gapping of the Fermi surface and multiband superconducting pairing mechanisms.

**Figures summary.** Figures show XRD patterns confirming phase purity, resistivity vs. temperature curves detailing SC and CDW transitions along different axes, and plots illustrating how TCDW increases while Tc decreases under pressure or with doping.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically presents measurements on Hf-doped ZrTe3, first establishing the baseline competition between CDW and SC; then analyzing the effect of chemical substitution (Hf) to tune this balance; finally confirming the mechanism using external tuning parameters like hydrostatic pressure.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report on the bulk multiband superconductivity and charge density wave (CDW) in HfxZr1-xTe3 single crystals. The parent compound ZrTe3 is a layered van der Waals material that undergoes a CDW transition near 63 K and exhibits only filamentary superconductivity below 2.0 K. Upon the introduction of a small amount of Hf (x = 0.02), the CDW transition temperature is reduced to TCDW ~ 53 K, while a robust bulk superconducting state emerges at Tc ~ 3.3 K, underscoring a subtle competition between CDW order and superconductivity in this quasi-one-dimensional system. Electrical resistivity, magnetic susceptibility, Hall effect, Seebeck coefficient, and specific heat measurements consistently confirm the bulk nature of the superconducting phase. The temperature dependence of the upper critical field Hc2(T) deviates markedly from single-band behavior, and it is well described by a two-band model, consistent with multiband superconductivity. Analysis of the Hall effect, and thermoelectric behavior reveal pronounced electronic anisotropy, with enhanced effective carrier masses, indicating that the subtle structural modification introduced by Hf substitutions affects the Fermi surface topology, as well as electronic correlations. Measurements of electrical resistivity in hydrostatic pressures up to ~ 2 GPa reveal that pressure drives TCDW to higher temperatures while suppressing Tc. These findings show that Hf doping can be used to fine-tune the balance between the CDW instability and superconductivity, possibly by means of chemical pressure effects, stabilizing a multiband superconducting state in Hf-doped ZrTe3.

</details>

**Summary.** This work reports on epitaxial multiferroic thin films ($	ext{PZTFN}_{0.5}$) designed for next-generation optoelectronics. By combining ferroelectric, magnetic, and photovoltaic measurements, the authors confirm a strong, switchable coupling between polarization state and photocurrent generation. This validates these single-phase materials as promising candidates for advanced memory and energy harvesting devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** While primarily condensed matter physics, the study involves coupled order parameters (ferroelectricity and magnetism) influencing electronic transport (photovoltaics), which touches upon concepts of emergent quantum phenomena in engineered solid-state systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to explore multiferroic materials exhibiting a strong coupling between ferroelectric polarization and photovoltaic effects for advanced optoelectronic devices.

**Main result.** The epitaxial 0.5PZT-0.5PFN thin films demonstrated a robust, polarization-dependent photoresponse under monochromatic laser illumination, confirming intrinsic coupling in these single-phase multiferroics.

**Method.** Characterization involved Pulsed Laser Deposition (PLD) growth, followed by comprehensive electrical measurements including ferroelectric P-E loops, magnetic M-H loops, and photovoltaic J-E characterization using polarized light.

**Model / system.** The physical system is an epitaxial multiferroic heterostructure ($	ext{SRO}/	ext{PZTFN}_{0.5}/	ext{STO}$). The behavior is modeled by treating the material as a semiconductor forming two back-to-back Schottky junctions whose biasing depends on the ferroelectric polarization state.

**Key observables.** Remanent polarization ($17 \mu	ext{C}/	ext{cm}^2$), photocurrent density ($\pm 20 \mu	ext{A}/	ext{cm}^2$), and polarization-dependent $	ext{J–E}$ characteristics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Laser wavelength (403 nm), operating temperature, applied voltage range ($\pm 3 	ext{ V}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The observed photovoltaic response is attributed to the intrinsic coupling between ferroelectric polarization and photogenerated carrier separation at internal interfaces.

**Figures summary.** Figures show XRD/RSM confirming single-phase epitaxial growth, P-E loops demonstrating high remnant polarization, M-H loops showing magnetic ordering, and J-E curves illustrating switchable photocurrent dependent on the applied electric field and polarization state.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a standard materials science structure: synthesis (PLD), structural characterization ($	ext{XRD}$, $	ext{TEM}$), electrical/ferroelectric characterization ($P-E$, $C-V$), magnetic characterization ($M-H$), and finally, the core photovoltaic measurements demonstrating polarization switching.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The exploration of novel multiferroic materials with strong coupling between ferroelectric polarization and photovoltaic effects is crucial for next-generation optoelectronic devices. In this study, we characterized highly oriented 0.5Pb(Zr0.52Ti0.48)O3-0.5Pb(Fe0.5Nb0.5)O3 multiferroic thin films grown by pulsed laser deposition on SrTiO3 (001) substrates with a SrRuO3 bottom electrode. The films exhibited excellent crystalline quality, with a single perovskite phase and (001) orientation. They displayed good ferroelectric properties (remanent polarization $\sim$17 $μ$C/cm$^2$, PUND; coercive field $\sim$150 kV/cm), alongside weak ferromagnetic behavior at room temperature (remanence 1.30 emu/cm$^3$; coercive field 90 Oe). Photovoltaic measurements demonstrated a robust, polarization-dependent photoresponse under 403 nm monochromatic laser illumination, achieving Jsc values between $\pm$20 $μ$A/cm$^2$. This compelling observation confirms the intrinsic coupling between ferroelectric polarization and photovoltaic effects, highlighting the considerable promise of these single-phase multiferroic thin films for advanced photovoltaic and optoelectronic memory applications.

</details>

**Summary.** This work provides a deep theoretical analysis of the long-time decay behavior of solutions on the Volterra lattice. By proving self-similarity and employing sophisticated techniques involving Painlevé equations, it calculates the precise asymptotic profile of the decaying state. This is crucial for understanding wave dynamics in nonlinear discrete systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous mathematical treatment of nonlinear lattice decay problems using advanced methods like Painlevé analysis and asymptotic matching is highly relevant to understanding wave propagation in complex, non-integrable physical systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research focuses on determining the self-similar asymptotics for the decay of an initial stationary state within the Volterra lattice model under zero boundary conditions.

**Main result.** The process is shown to be asymptotically self-similar, allowing for explicit calculation of the propagation velocity and the leading terms of the asymptotic profile using advanced matching techniques.

**Method.** The analysis employs formal asymptotic expansions (FAS) in inverse powers of time, requiring a matching procedure near the front edge that involves solving equations related to the second Painlevé equation (P-II).

**Model / system.** The primary model is the Volterra lattice ($\dot{u}_n = u_n(u_{n+1} - u_{n-1})$), which models an idealized food chain. Generalizations involve varying the nonlinear coupling function $r(u)$ and considering non-zero boundary conditions.

**Key observables.** Propagation velocity of the decay wave ($2v$), leading asymptotic functions ($f_0, g_0$), and higher-order correction coefficients ($f_j, g_j$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Initial data parameters ($\alpha$), time evolution direction ($t 	o +\infty$ vs. $t 	o -\infty$), and boundary condition strength ($eta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies on the assumption of asymptotic self-similarity, requiring the use of matching techniques when standard series expansions fail near the wave front.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate numerical solutions showing decay wave formation for various parameters (e.g., $\alpha=1$ or $\alpha=1/3$), and comparison between theoretical inner and outer asymptotic expansions using stretched variables ($z$).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the self-similar nature of the solution, deriving leading order ODEs for coefficients, solving these systems by relating them to Painlevé transcendents (P-II), and finally applying matching techniques to determine the full asymptotic profile for $t 	o \pm\infty$.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The article is devoted to the problem of decay of initial stationary state for the Volterra lattice with zero boundary condition. We show that this process is asymptotically self-similar and calculate the propagation velocity of the decay wave, the leading terms of the asymptotics and corrections, in the main and transition sectors of the wave.

</details>

### [Nonmonotonic temperature dependence of the thermopower of atomic-size gold contacts](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12734v1)

**Summary.** This work measures the thermopower of atomic-size gold contacts across varying temperatures, revealing a nonmonotonic dependence that contradicts standard quantum transport theory. The authors argue this deviation necessitates considering phonon contributions beyond simple electronic models. This highlights the limitations of linear approximations even in highly confined quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on mesoscopic transport rather than fundamental AMO systems, the necessity of going beyond simple linear approximations (like the basic Landauer model) when studying complex quantum phenomena is a general theme relevant to open quantum system modeling.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the nonmonotonic temperature dependence of the thermopower ($S$) in atomic-size gold contacts.

**Main result.** The measured thermopower exhibits a minimum at about 150 K, which contradicts the linear predictions of simple quantum transport models like the Landauer formula.

**Method.** Measurements were performed using mechanically controllable break junctions (MCBJ) to probe atomic-size contacts across a wide temperature range, combining electrical and thermal measurements.

**Model / system.** The system is an atomic-size gold contact formed via MCBJ. Theoretical analysis involves extending the Landauer formalism by incorporating energy-dependent transmission functions ($	au(E)$) to model transport beyond simple free-electron approximations.

**Key observables.** Ensemble-averaged thermopower $\langle S angle$ as a function of temperature $T$, and conductance histograms ($G$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature range (18 K to 295 K), minimum thermopower value ($-2 \mu	ext{V K}^{-1}$ at 150 K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The deviation from linearity is attributed to phonon contributions beyond the standard Landauer model, suggesting non-electronic origins for the observed behavior.

**Figures summary.** Figures show $\langle S angle$ vs. $T$ exhibiting a minimum; theoretical fits relate $S(T)$ to higher-order transmission function parameters ($	au_n$).

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the experimental setup and measurements, contrasts the observed nonmonotonic behavior against simple Landauer predictions, develops advanced theoretical models incorporating energy dependence, and concludes that phonon contributions are necessary to explain the deviation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report measurements of the thermopower of atomic-size gold contacts realized by the mechanically controllable break junction (MCBJ) technique over a temperature range from 18 K to 295 K. A thermometer included in the lithographic structure close to the constriction provides a direct measurement of the temperature increase generated by heating one side of the contact with a focused laser beam. While the conductance histograms confirm the quantum nature of the transport, we observe a nonmonotonic temperature dependence of the ensemble-averaged thermopower with a minimum of $-2\,μ$VK$^{-1}$ at about 150 K. The values for the thermopower obtained at the lowest and the high temperature are compatible with values reported in the literature, but the nonmonotonic behavior in between disagrees with the expected linear dependence for quantum coherent conductors described by the Landauer formula. We develop a theoretical model based on an energy dependent transmission function that qualitatively reproduces the nonmonotonic behavior, but fails quantitatively. We therefore interpret our data as a result of phonon contributions to the thermopower beyond the Landauer model and with opposite sign than the classical phonon drag known from bulk systems. Our findings show that, firstly, the thermopower gives important insight into the transport properties of atomic-size structures and second that the linear approximation of the Landauer model has to be used with caution when studying more complex transport properties even for atomic contacts from free-electron metals.

</details>

### [Current patterns and loss contributions in CORT cables carrying AC current](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13512v1)

**Authors:** Benoît Vanderheyden, Julien Dular, Louis Denis, Steffen Elschner, Christophe Geuzaine, Mariusz Wozniak, Francesco Grilli  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13512v1>

(a) The magnetic field produced inside resembles that of a long solenoid. (b) The magnetic field produced outside resembles that of a long wire. (c) The resulting magnetic flux density is axial inside and azimuthal outside. **Summary.** This paper analyzes AC losses in CORT cables by resolving the complex, three-dimensional current flow patterns within the coated conductors. Using an effective 2D helicoidal model, it demonstrates that simple analytical approximations fail to capture the true loss mechanisms, which depend critically on the cable's geometry and gap size.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on superconducting magnet technology, the rigorous modeling of non-uniform current distributions and field penetration in confined geometries shares mathematical parallels with understanding flux dynamics or boundary effects in quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the complex current patterns and resulting AC power losses within Conductor-on-Round-Tube (CORT) cables carrying alternating current.

**Main result.** The detailed 3D current distribution is shown to exhibit a 'Garber pattern' alongside edge effects, leading to AC losses that deviate significantly from simplified analytical models, especially at small gaps.

**Method.** The research employs an effective 2D helicoidal model derived from first principles to simulate the non-trivial current flow in the superconducting coated conductors.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a CORT cable composed of multiple coated conductors wound helically. The analysis models the AC losses by considering three main contributions: surface losses, edge losses, and diving current losses.

**Key observables.** Current density components (axial $J_z$, azimuthal $J_\phi$, radial $J_r$), total AC power loss ($Q/L$), and the relative dominance of different loss mechanisms.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Conductor thickness, gap size ($g$), pitch angle ($\alpha$), current amplitude ($I_0$), and the exponent $n$ in the resistivity power law $ho \propto J^n$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a power-law relationship for resistivity and neglects complex field dependencies of critical current density on magnetic flux magnitude or direction.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the spatial distribution of current densities ($\mathbf{J}$) components across different regions (Garber, Mawatari) within the cable cross-section, and plot calculated AC losses versus gap size and pitch angle, comparing simulation results to analytical predictions.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the geometry and resulting current flow patterns in CORT cables. It compares detailed 3D simulations using a helicoidal model against simplified theoretical models (Surface/Mawatari) to quantify how geometric parameters dictate which loss mechanism dominates.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Conductor-on-round-tube (CORT) cables are a potential solution for carrying AC power in a small cross-section. Due to the geometry of the cable and the helical arrangement of the coated conductors (CC), the current follows a non-trivial pattern inside each CC. For instance, for the case of a single-layer cable, the current flow is mostly axial along the outer face of the CCs and mostly azimuthal along their inner face. Such a current distribution, known as the Garber current pattern, affects the transport AC losses. In numerical models, commonly adopted simplifications are either based on straight conductors or infinitely thin CCs. Such approaches neglect the Garber current pattern and thus misrepresent both the detailed current flow within the CC and the resulting 3D distribution of the fields. In this work, the detailed 3D current distribution in the CCs is investigated in a one-layer CORT cable, as a function of the cable geometrical parameters such as the conductor thickness, the pitch angle, and the gap between adjacent CCs. In particular, the impact of the Garber current pattern is studied on the two largest contributions to the AC losses, namely the surface losses (associated with the penetration of the component of the magnetic field parallel to the wide faces of the superconducting layer) and the edge losses (associated with the penetration of the perpendicular component of the magnetic field occurring in the vicinity of the gaps between the CCs). The detailed distribution of the currents in the CCs is examined and its relationship with the different AC loss mechanisms is established. This study is carried out by means of an effective 2D model that uses a system of coordinates conforming with the helical structure of the cable.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.12513"></a>
### [Localization of Chiral Electromagnetic Waves on Thick Axion Domain Walls](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.12513v1)

**Authors:** Nemanja Kaloper  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.12513v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `interference shaping light` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper theoretically investigates electromagnetic wave propagation near axion domain walls, finding that the wall structure inherently supports a robust, localized chiral photon mode. By extending spectral analysis from idealized delta-function limits to realistic finite thicknesses, the authors confirm this bound state exists even when previous adiabatic approximations fail. This suggests axion domain walls can act as natural waveguides for specific polarizations of light.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete example of how topological defects (domain walls) can act as natural waveguides or sources for exotic excitations (chiral photons), connecting high-energy physics concepts (axions) to condensed matter phenomena like surface modes and photonics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study analyzes the localization of chiral electromagnetic waves propagating along thick axion domain walls.

**Main result.** The analysis proves that such walls generically support a localized, normalizable chiral electromagnetic mode with linear, gapless dispersion, which was missed in prior analyses.

**Method.** The problem is formulated as a spectral boundary value problem solved using the Lippmann-Schwinger framework and analyzing poles in the analytically continued resolvent of the wave operator.

**Model / system.** The system involves Maxwell theory coupled to a background axion field $	heta(x)$ forming a domain wall. The analysis progresses from idealized delta-function walls to smooth, finite-width profiles.

**Key observables.** Localized chiral electromagnetic mode (chiral surface photon); Transmission ($T_{\pm}$) and reflection ($R_{\pm}$) coefficients; Dispersion relation ($\omega^2 = k_\parallel^2 / (1 + (\Delta	heta)^2/4)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Axion mass ($m_\phi$); Wall jump parameter ($\Delta	heta$); Frequency ($\omega$) relative to $m_\phi$; Wall thickness scale ($L$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on extending the spectral methods from the thin-wall limit to finite width, and in some steps, assumes small perturbations or monochromatic wave approximations.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically builds the problem by first examining the ultrathin (delta-function) wall limit using scattering theory, then generalizing this robustly to finite thickness walls by analyzing the full spectral structure of the resulting 1+1 dimensional wave equation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We analyze Maxwell theory coupled to an axion domain wall as a spectral boundary value problem. We find that a finite-width axion domain wall generically supports a localized normalizable chiral electromagnetic mode with linear, gapless dispersion. This mode arises from helicity-dependent coupling sourced by the axion gradient: one polarization experiences an effective attractive potential and forms a bound state, while the opposite polarization is repelled. The existence of this chiral surface photon is robust over a wide regime of wall structures and axion masses. Our result shows that axion domain walls generically support a localized chiral photon that has been missed in previous analyses.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.13526"></a>
### [Quantum geometric anomalous Hall response in orbitally nonunitary superconductors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.13526v1)

**Authors:** Viktor Frilen, Annica M. Black-Schaffer, Ankita Bhattacharya  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.13526v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5**

</div>

**Summary.** This theoretical work examines the anomalous Hall response in multiband superconductors using a honeycomb lattice model. The key finding is that orbital nonunitarity, even when spin-unitary and restricted to intraband pairing, can generate a finite AHR via quantum geometric effects. This establishes a new pathway for detecting time-reversal symmetry breaking signals in superconducting materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** The finding that spin-unitary but orbitally nonunitary pairing can induce an AHR without interband coupling provides a new, purely intrinsic mechanism for probing broken symmetries in superconductors, which is highly relevant to topological superconductivity research.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the anomalous Hall response (AHR) in multiband superconductors at optical frequencies, specifically determining if a finite AHR can arise from spin-unitary but orbitally nonunitary pairing when only intraband pairing is considered.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that for equal-spin triplet pairing, a finite AHR persists even with purely intraband pairing, attributing this to orbital nonunitarity interacting with SOC. This revises the criteria previously thought necessary for observing Kerr signals in superconductors.

**Method.** The analysis employs Kubo linear response theory applied to the Bogoliubov-de Gennes Hamiltonian within a multiband framework. Calculations involve analyzing Berry curvature terms derived from intraband pairing contributions.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled as a multiband superconductor on a honeycomb lattice, incorporating Kane-Mele spin-orbit coupling. The superconducting state is described by investigating chiral d-wave spin-singlet and chiral p-wave equal-spin triplet pairing states.

**Key observables.** Anomalous Hall Response (AHR) or anomalous Hall conductivity ($\sigma_H(\omega)$), related to the polar Kerr effect.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Optical frequency ($\omega$), Kane-Mele SOC strength, and the degree of orbital nonunitarity ($v$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes intraband pairing contributions only by setting interband pairing terms to zero in certain parts of the Hamiltonian. The study focuses on specific prototype TRS breaking states.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare AHR for d-wave singlet and p-wave triplet pairings under varying conditions, notably contrasting results with and without interband pairing contributions.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from establishing the theoretical framework using BdG Hamiltonians and Kubo response theory. It then analyzes specific pairing symmetries (singlet vs. triplet) to show how orbital nonunitarity can generate a finite AHR even when conventional mechanisms (like interband coupling) are suppressed.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the anomalous Hall response (AHR) in a multiband superconductor at optical frequencies, a phenomenon intimately related to the polar Kerr effect, a key probe of time-reversal symmetry breaking in superconductors. In translationally invariant multiband systems with purely intraband pairing, Galilean invariance decouples center-of-mass and relative motion of Cooper pairs, leading to the widespread expectation that a finite AHR requires either disorder or finite interband pairing amplitudes. However, this restriction can be lifted by the quantum geometric effects inherent to multiband Bloch states. Using a honeycomb lattice tight-binding model with Kane-Mele spin-orbit coupling, we analyze the AHR for the time-reversal symmetry broken chiral $d$-wave spin-singlet and chiral $p$-wave equal-spin-triplet pairing states with intraband pairing only. We demonstrate, through both analytical and numerical calculations, that the spin-singlet state yields a vanishing AHR, even with its broken time-reversal symmetry, whereas the equal-spin triplet state exhibits a finite AHR, even when it is spin-unitary. We attribute the latter to orbital nonunitarity, which, in the presence of spin-orbit coupling, generates the spin-polarized Bogoliubov quasiparticle states required for a finite AHR. The response is mediated by interband velocity matrix elements governed by the quantum geometry. This finding establishes that spin-unitary, but orbitally nonunitary pairing, can generate a finite AHR even without interband pairing and thereby revises the criteria for Kerr signals in superconductors.

</details>
